# 宋徽宗御解道德真经解义

**Preface to the *Exposition on the Dao and Its Virtue* by Emperor Huizong of Song**

I have heard that the Dao cannot be made manifest without words, nor can words convey the Dao without the Classics. The Dao fails to spread because the Classics are not transmitted, and the Classics fail to circulate because their meaning remains obscure.

In the late Zhou, the Dao declined and virtue waned. The various masters arose, each claiming their own heterodoxies as distinct schools. Their clever words and abstruse theories, though vast, could not be used to seek truth. They took turns as the clamor of flutes and drums, flowing into falsehood and deception. Like floating stones and sunken wood, they indulged in perverse doctrines, losing the true nature of life and sinking into ruin, unable to return.

Laozi set forth five thousand words to clarify the constancy of the Dao and virtue, intending to cut off learning and return to simplicity, to restore non-action and stabilize through purity and tranquility—this is the Classic that bears the Dao.

Yet its words are concise and its meaning profound. The more one delves, the more distant it becomes; the more one probes, the deeper it grows. Without divine insight, one cannot even begin to discuss its outline—this is why the Classic is so difficult to transmit.

Our Sovereign, born with innate knowledge, endowed by Heaven with divine sagacity, grasps the subtle words and profound meanings not merely in understanding but in true embodiment. From his imperial brush, he has composed a complete work. Though separated from Laozi by a thousand years of emptiness, his words align as if by tally. The true, constant, and wondrous origin is laid bare and made clear—this is how the Classic is transmitted, its meaning illuminated, and the Dao made to flourish.

I, undeserving of such an encounter, have purified my mind, cleansed my thoughts, and concentrated my spirit to unity. Reverently reading the Sacred Composition, I have pondered deeply and sought its utmost essence. My obscurity and delusion have been suddenly dispelled, as if gaining insight, and I could not restrain myself. Presumptuously offering my humble views, I have looked up to the Sagely Teachings and expounded this *Exposition*, dividing it into ten scrolls.

Though a frog in a well, my knowledge does not reach the clouds; though a firefly’s glow, my light cannot add to the sun and moon. Yet the transformative power of the Dao is vast. Even the smallest creature may express the wonders of Heaven and Earth’s creation, and this is sufficient to manifest the ultimate achievement of the Sagely Governance and the far-reaching influence of the Dao.

To have encountered this once in ten thousand chances is a stroke of fortune. Moreover, it allows all under Heaven and future generations to know that in this era of Great Peace and prosperity, even one of humble station is not dismissed for their words. They will see how the Classic is transmitted and the Dao made to flourish—how could this not be right?

Respectfully inscribed by your servant, Zhang An.

---

**Scroll One of *Exposition on the Dao and Its Virtue* by Emperor Huizong of Song**
*Exposition by Court Gentleman Zhang An*

**The Classic of the Dao**

The Imperial Annotation states:
The Dao is that which all people jointly follow.
Virtue is what the heart naturally attains.
The Dao, once established, endures for all ages without corruption.
Virtue fills the constancy of one’s nature and abides forever.

At the end of the Zhou, the Dao declined and virtue waned. Thus, the Old Master composed ninety-nine chapters to clarify the constancy of the Dao and virtue, calling it the *Classic*. Its words are concise, its meaning far-reaching—scholars should silently comprehend and deeply cultivate it.

**My Exposition:**
In the Great Beginning, there was Non-Being; Non-Being had no name, and it was called the Dao.
When the One had yet to take form, yet things received life from it, it was called virtue.
The Dao is fundamentally nameless; to call it the Dao is to speak of what all people jointly follow.
Virtue is fundamentally self-attained; to call it virtue is to speak of what all hearts naturally attain.

Only the Dao is boundless, empty and clear, eternally abiding. Only the Dao is formless, subtle and primordial, eternally real. It transcends the realm of being and non-being, dissolving the dwelling of self and other. In essence, it is essence; in function, it is function. Though Heaven and Earth are vast, they do not lie beyond it. Though an autumn hair is minute, it relies on it to complete its form. The beginning and end, exit and entry of the myriad things have never not originated from this—thus it is called the Dao. It is what all hearts naturally and equally attain.

Examining it, it requires no borrowing and does not transform with things. Profound in its abiding, it does not shift with things. Never obscured or diminished, never errant or hasty, the heart is whole with Heaven and free from the burdens of gain and loss—thus it is called virtue.

The Dao is formless; being formless, it is not subject to change or decay, enduring through all ages without corruption.
Virtue resides within me; being within me, it requires nothing external, thus filling the constancy of one’s nature and abiding forever.

When the Zhou declined and culture decayed, the world rushed toward superficialities, multiplying cleverness and intelligence, indulging in benevolence and righteousness. Primal simplicity was daily eroded, unable to achieve sudden clarity and perceive the unique. Lingering in the realm of honor and disgrace, drifting in the arena of likes and dislikes, they came to extinguish Heaven with man, and obliterate life with artifice—what then of the Dao and virtue?

Thus, the Old Master composed ninety-nine chapters to cut off learning and return to simplicity, to restore the discipline of the Dao, and to deepen the root and solidify the foundation, merging with the Great Beginning. By naming the constancy of the Dao, he discussed the primordial mother of Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things. By observing the constancy of being and non-being, he beheld the subtlest and most refined wonders. Returning to life to embody the spirit, knowing harmony to preserve qi. The Dao is constant and returns to the nameless; virtue is constant and reaches the return to simplicity—all to clarify the constancy of the Dao and virtue.

What is constant and unchanging is called the *Classic* (jing). The weft (wei) interweaves back and forth, thus it is called change. The *Classic* has a fixed essence, the meaning of constancy and enduring permanence. Words express meaning, thus they are called "words" (ci); intent points to meaning, thus it is called "meaning" (zhi). Only words that bear the Dao are concise and fitting. The meaning that embodies the Dao is distant yet clear.

The Dao fundamentally cannot be transmitted through words, nor can it be illustrated through meaning. Seeking it between the traces of words is certainly not the way to attain the Dao. Yet if one clings to the edges of words, the Dao again cannot be captured or described. Only those who merge with the Dao, who unite with the words and meaning beyond, who harmonize with the primordial One and return to true constancy, can silently comprehend and deeply cultivate it.

Alas! The Dao is not distant for those with intent, nor is it near for those without. Silent comprehension and profound attainment—this is called "mystic unity."

**Chapter One: The Dao That Can Be Spoken**
The Dao that can be spoken is not the enduring Dao.
The name that can be named is not the enduring name.

The Imperial Commentary states: "Without beginning is called the Dao; it cannot be spoken, for speech misrepresents it." It further says: "The Dao should not be named." That which can be spoken or named resembles things and affairs, like the four seasons—responding when fitting, alternating in decline and rise, yet not truly constant. The enduring Dao and enduring name are self-originating, self-rooted; before heaven and earth existed, they have ever abided.

Fuxi attained it and inherited the Mother of Qi; the Queen Mother of the West attained it and sat in the Lesser Expanse. None know its beginning, none know its end.

Minister Yi says: The Dao is supremely void, dwelling within the myriad concrete things yet showing no trace of disturbance—thus it has no form to describe. The Dao is supremely still, facing the myriad movements yet untouched by their entanglements—thus it has no direction to name. The Dao cannot be spoken; when entrusted to words and sounds, these are but its dregs. The Dao cannot be named; when lodged within forms and vessels, these are but its traps and snares. That which can be spoken or named belongs to the realm of existence—like things and affairs, ceaseless in their cycles of rise and fall, like the four seasons, endlessly revolving in succession. Adapting to principle and fitting change, each may be deemed fitting in its moment—how could this be truly constant? The wondrous root of true constancy precedes heaven and earth without decay, follows heaven and earth without exhaustion. Mystically uniting in silence, how could it tolerate sound?

Fuxi attained it and inherited the Mother of Qi—this may be called knowing the child while guarding the mother, thus merging with the Dao’s essence. Is this not the enduring Dao? The Queen Mother of the West attained it and sat in the Lesser Expanse—this may be called obtaining the mother to know the child, thus uniting with the Dao’s function. Is this not the enduring name? The enduring Dao and enduring name are self-originating, self-rooted; before heaven and earth existed, they have ever abided—how could one discuss their beginning and end in terms of novelty or antiquity? Thus it is said: none know its end.

**Nameless, it is the beginning of heaven and earth.**
**Named, it is the mother of the myriad things.**

The Imperial Commentary states: The Dao is eternally nameless; even heaven and earth await it to come into being—what *Zhuangzi* calls "giving birth to heaven and earth." Before heaven and earth existed, who could name it? Thus the nameless is the beginning of heaven and earth. Only after heaven and earth exist do the myriad things arise—thus the named is the mother of the myriad things.

Minister Yi says: In the great beginning, there was nonbeing, and nonbeing had no name. Thus the nameless is the inception of qi, that by which heaven and earth are born—hence it is the beginning of heaven and earth. From the One comes naming; the One generates the Two, the Two generates the Three, and the Three generates the myriad things. Thus the named is the origin of number, that by which the myriad things are completed—hence it is the mother of the myriad things. As the inception of qi, it is called "beginning"; as the ceaseless generation of life, it is called "mother."

**By constantly having nothing, one may observe its mystery.**
**By constantly having something, one may observe its manifestations.**

The Imperial Commentary states: *Zhuangzi* says: "Establish it in constant nonbeing and constant being." Not establishing a single thing—this is called constant nonbeing. Not discarding a single thing—this is called constant being. Constant nonbeing resides in principle, transcending all bounds; it is the utmost subtlety of the world—thus one observes its mystery. Constant being resides in affairs, embracing all transformations without confusion; it is the utmost mutability of the world—thus one observes its manifestations. Within the two realms of being and nonbeing, manifestations and mystery reside. Only great wisdom can observe both without obstruction.

In the midst of vagueness and indistinctness, there are images and things. Lesser wisdom, self-serving and partial, fixates on one corner—discarding being to cling to emptiness, pursuing ends while forgetting roots. Thus the Dao’s arts are torn asunder across the world.

Minister Yi says: To be within being yet have nonbeing is called constant nonbeing—not the emptiness of annihilation, but seeing nonbeing within being. Yet all things are empty—thus it is said: "not establishing a single thing," which is the true constancy of nonbeing. To be within nonbeing yet have being is called constant being—not the being of material forms, but seeing being within nonbeing. Yet all things are fully present—thus it is said: "not discarding a single thing," which is the true constancy of being. Constant nonbeing merges with the principle of unity, concealing the world’s utmost subtlety, penetrating right and wrong, blending past and present. Constant nonbeing does not fall into mere nothingness—thus one may observe its mystery. Constant being responds to the affairs of the present, facing the world’s utmost transformations—things and events in ceaseless cycles, forms and vessels in endless flux, emerging in infinite variety, chaotic and unceasing. Constant being does not stagnate in mere existence—thus one may observe its manifestations. Within the realm of being and nonbeing, the observation of manifestations and mystery resides. Yet how could manifestations and mystery be fully observed through being and nonbeing alone? It is rather by means of constant nonbeing and constant being that one seeks to observe them.

These two emerge from the same source yet bear different names—together they are called the Mysterious.

The Imperial Commentary states: The Dao is originally without form—what could be its manifestations or mystery? Self and other share the same root; right and wrong are of one qi—thus together they are called the Mysterious. The deluded of the world abandon the false to seek the real, yet the more they distance themselves from the real, the less they understand that being and nonbeing are but different names.

Minister Yi says: The Dao takes constant nonbeing as its essence and wondrous being as its function. Treasuring its formless nature, who could observe its manifestations or mystery? Self and other return to the origin of creation; right and wrong merge in the qi of emptiness. Detached from form and free of intellect, being and nonbeing both vanish—thus together they are called the Mysterious. To be within nonbeing yet have being, to be within being yet have nonbeing—this is what is meant by "these two emerge from the same source." Being and nonbeing generate each other, yet their distinctions become apparent—this is why they bear different names.

The Mysterious as color is both red and black—terms like "the first day of the north" in its direction all unite these two aspects. Only by penetrating both being and nonbeing to unite them as one can one speak of the Dao.

"Mysterious and yet again mysterious"—this is the gate of all wonders.

The Imperial Commentary states: *The Plain Questions* says, "The Mysterious gives birth to spirit." *The Book of Changes* says, "Spirit is that which wondrously brings forth the myriad things." What is wondrous and subtle is called the Mysterious; the Mysterious is the color of Heaven. That which is colored by color is manifest, yet that which colors all colors has never been revealed. "Mysterious and yet again mysterious"—this refers to that which colors all colors. The principle of the Mysterious and wondrous is inherent in all things. What moves Heaven, what stabilizes Earth, what makes humans numinous, what makes the hundred things flourish—all are wondrous, and all emerge from the Mysterious. Thus it is called "the gate of all wonders." When Confucius composed the *Book of Changes*, he first spoke of wonder in the "Discussion of the Trigrams," while Laozi placed it at the beginning of his work. The words of the sages form a continuous cycle.

Minister Yi says: Heaven originates its unity in the north—this is the sameness of the Mysterious. Earth pairs its duality in the south—this is the emergence of spirit. Spirit is born from the Mysterious, and the Mysterious is wondrous through spirit. Together with being and nonbeing, they merge without distinction—this is what is called the Mysterious. "Mysterious and yet again mysterious"—the Mysterious reaches its utmost here. Spirit emerges from this, numinosity becomes manifest through this, wondrously bringing forth the myriad things without fixed form. This is how Heaven and Earth attain their divine clarity, and how the sage accomplishes effortless action. The numinosity of humans and the flourishing of things all fulfill their wonder, and all wonder emerges from the Mysterious. Thus it is called "the gate of all wonders." When Confucius composed the *Book of Changes*, he used images and numbers to reveal spirit, so he first spoke of wonder in the "Discussion of the Trigrams." Laozi united being and nonbeing to reveal the Mysterious, so he placed it at the beginning of his work. Spirit is born from the Mysterious, and the Mysterious is the source from which all wonders emerge—thus the sequence begins and ends in this way.

**Chapter Two: When All Under Heaven Know**

When all under heaven know beauty as beauty, ugliness arises. When all know goodness as goodness, not-goodness arises.

The Imperial Commentary states: The Dao has no distinct forms—what is beauty, what is ugliness? Nature is originally one—what is good, what is not? Where there is beauty, ugliness stands in opposition, thus: "When all under heaven know beauty as beauty, ugliness arises." Where there is goodness, not-goodness stands in opposition, thus: "When all know goodness as goodness, not-goodness arises." What the world considers beautiful is called the divine and marvelous; what it considers ugly is called the foul and rotten. Yet the divine and marvelous may transform into the foul and rotten, and the foul and rotten may transform into the divine and marvelous—how then can beauty and ugliness be distinguished? What was once deemed right may now be rejected, and what is now discarded may later be used—how then can good and not-good be chosen? The sage embodies true nonbeing yet is constantly present, wields wondrous function yet remains nonbeing. Beauty and ugliness, good and not-good—these he simplifies yet cannot grasp. How then could ugliness and not-goodness ever burden him?

Minister Yi says: The Dao is originally without thing—what distinct forms could it have? Yet when it enters the realm of beauty and ugliness, distinctions arise. Nature is originally one in the Mysterious—what duality could it possess? Yet when it adheres to good and not-good, its manifestations diverge. The ancients returned to the origin of the Dao, obscured in celestial nature, content in their natural state, each at ease with what they attained, each acting spontaneously in what was fitting. How could they be said to act? But when the pure and simple scattered, they turned from the Dao toward things, abandoned nature for the heart-mind, used the heart-mind to know, and followed emotions to seek harmony. Disregarding nature and destiny, they were swept away by private desires. Thus beauty and ugliness, good and not-good, alternated in opposition—how could this ever end? The divine and marvelous or the foul and rotten in things depend on the world’s judgments of beauty and ugliness. As transformations shift, what is fitting is prized—how then can beauty and ugliness truly be distinguished? The right and wrong, use and discard of principles depend on the world’s judgments of good and not-good. In the interplay of subtle changes, what accords with the time is deemed proper—how then can good and not-good truly be chosen? Moreover, as emotions manifest, people’s knowledge differs; each follows their private interests, creating self and other. Thus beauty and ugliness, good and not-good, cycle endlessly. The sage transcends self and other in the unity of the Mysterious, dissolves being and nonbeing together, leaves behind oppositions to abide in solitude—no words can describe it. When Ji Xian appeared divine, yet Master Hu could not fathom him, how could beauty and ugliness, good and not-good ever burden him? When the Mysterious Pearl was lost, and knowledge was sent to seek it in vain—what further use was there for knowledge?

Being and nonbeing give birth to each other; difficulty and ease complete each other; long and short form each other; high and low incline toward each other; sound and echo harmonize with each other; front and back follow each other.

The Imperial Commentary states: Before the Great Primal separated, the ten thousand images shared a single body. Once the Two Modes emerged, each thing stood in opposition. These six pairs are where all transformations intersect, where a hundred thoughts arise, where divergent paths begin—this is how the world’s people become mired and cannot free themselves. Nonbeing moves to generate being, and being returns to nonbeing—thus "being and nonbeing give birth to each other." When one encounters perilous difficulty, one knows the ease of level ground—thus "difficulty and ease complete each other." Long and short form each other, as with a foot and an inch. High and low incline toward each other, as with mountains and marshes. When sound is raised, echo responds—thus "sound and echo harmonize with each other." When form moves, shadow follows—thus "front and back follow each other." The movement of yin and yang, the progression of the four seasons, the principles of the myriad things—suddenly they come into being, swiftly they transform into nonbeing. Their difficulty lies in acting to order the world; their ease lies in non-action to suit oneself. What is long by nature cannot be cut short; what is short by nature cannot be extended. Heaven is high of itself; Earth is low of itself. Strike the *gong* note, and the *gong* note resonates; strike the *jue* note, and the *jue* note responds. Spring leads, and summer follows; the elder leads, and the younger follows. Though these paired conditions all dwell within the Dao, they are far from the Dao.

Minister Yi states: Qi is that which the Dao moves. Things are that which qi transforms. Qi is obscure in nonbeing, empty yet ever-permeating; when it becomes manifest, it is grasped and imaged—hence called "image." It unfolds the Dao in the Great Simplicity, whence arises the image of the Lord, bestowing qi upon the myriad things through nonbeing, completing their forms through being. Qi disperses in the Grand Beginning, becoming yin and yang; forms separate in the Primordial Inception, becoming heaven and earth. At the extreme of qi’s transformations, where forms are transmuted—mountains’ stillness, seas and rivers’ flow, the manifest utility of the Five Materials, the names and terms of the host of things—their principles, their forms, their sounds, their numbers, their flowing, scattering, and shifting, their entry and exit into life and death—all that dwells within the realm of form and vessel: these six pairs emerge and submerge in alternation, yet cannot halt of themselves. People of the world grasp them as real, engage with them as constructs, take the illusory as constant, and so cut and wound one another in mutual harm, erode and dissolve one another in mutual decay. Weary and toiling, they know not where to return, thus sinking into the current of ceaseless flux, unable to extricate themselves.

All under heaven that can be named has never been without its counterpart: being and nonbeing, difficulty and ease, long and short, high and low, the harmony of sound and echo, the following of front and back—all are the necessary principles of their nature. All under heaven that can be named is invariably thus, never standing alone. Hence, from beauty arises ugliness, from goodness arises not-goodness—this is the inherent pattern. Though these six paired conditions all dwell within the Dao, serving as the lodging of its wondrous function, when enslaved to their entanglements and driven by their impulses, they depart from the Dao—one may say they are far indeed. How then can one speak of the Dao?

Thus the sage abides in non-action, practices the teaching without words. As the *Yuzhu* notes: "Abiding in non-action" is what *Zhuangzi* calls "using the world through non-action." "Practicing the teaching without words" is what the *Yi* calls "establishing teaching through the spirit-way, and the world submits." To act is to incur completion and loss; to speak is to risk rightness and error—how can one escape entanglement? How could this be the way the sage stands alone above the myriad things, transforming them while remaining unburdened by them?

Minister Yi states: When affairs arise, I respond to them—this cannot be avoided. Yet when seen as action, principle incurs completion and loss. When things arrive unexpectedly, I discern them—this cannot be without speech. Yet when seen as speech, principle incurs rightness and error. Entering the realm of action and speech—who can forget entanglement? The sage stands alone above the myriad things; where affairs are handled and teaching is practiced, the world thereby returns to constancy and restores simplicity. Mysteriously uniting right and wrong, he makes no distinction between beauty and ugliness, goodness and not-goodness. Those six pairs have no means to disturb—thus he has never been burdened by speech or action.

The myriad things arise, yet he does not refuse; they live, yet he does not possess; he acts, yet does not rely; his work is accomplished, yet he does not dwell. This may be called "using the world through non-action." Hence it is said: "Abide in non-action." He does not esteem worthies, does not prize rare goods, does not display what may be desired—this may be called "establishing teaching through the spirit-way, and the world submits." Hence it is said: "Practice the teaching without words." The myriad things arise, yet he does not refuse; they live, yet he does not possess; he acts, yet does not rely; his work is accomplished, yet he does not dwell. Only because he does not dwell does he not depart.

As the *Yuzhu* notes: The myriad things arise together, and he responds to their stimuli as a mirror reflects forms—beauty and ugliness are fully revealed; as an echo answers a valley—good and bad all arrive, with nothing refused. Hence it is said: "They arise, yet he does not refuse." They form and transform themselves, live and take on color themselves, each reaching its utmost height and greatness, each fulfilling its nature—who possesses them? Hence it is said: "They live, yet he does not possess." He orders the myriad things without cruelty, bestows blessings for ten thousand generations without claiming benevolence, covers and bears heaven and earth, carves and shapes all forms without claiming skill. Hence it is said: "He acts, yet does not rely." The movement of the four seasons—when their work is complete, they depart: this is the Way of Heaven. The sage embodies this, thus his merit covers the world, yet seems not to originate from himself. To grasp and claim it is already delusion. Hence it is said: "His work is accomplished, yet he does not dwell." To dwell is to depart—this is true of past and present. With nothing dwelling in himself, nothing can move him. Coming as it comes, going as it goes—what is added or diminished? Hence it is said: "Only because he does not dwell does he not depart."

Minister Yi states: The sage moves only when pressed, rises only when unavoidable—thus he does not engage in busyness. When things come, he responds; responding without exhaustion, he is not entangled in the moment, nor does he cling to the past. Following the times and riding the principle, he adapts to change as it suits, like heaven’s cycling of cold and heat, entrusted to nature, his heart free of accumulated grievances or scheming thoughts.

When the myriad things arise together, they arise of themselves—what need is there for refusal? Responding to stimuli without premeditation—what room is there for intention? Like a mirror facing forms, he neither sends forth nor welcomes, responds yet does not store. Like a valley echoing sound, he neither amplifies nor suppresses, leaving nothing large or small unanswered. Hence it is said: "The myriad things arise, yet he does not refuse."

The movement of the single qi, the molding of the great craft—its generation proceeds in order, its beginning and end in sequence. Movement and stillness, speech and silence, are driven by the workings of heaven’s mechanism; spirit and heart’s guard sprout from the qi’s dynamic. Heaven bestows form, scattering it into ten thousand differences—its transformations vary, its lives are not uniform, its bodies differ, its colors diverge. Each reaches its limit and fulfills its nature, yet none knows how this comes to be. To grasp and claim it—who could give life to life? Hence it is said: "They live, yet he does not possess."

Acting through non-action, though he acts—what does he rely on?

Success is achieved through heaven’s work—yet though achieved, what claim do I have?
To claim is to possess; when nothing is possessed within oneself, nothing can sway one. To possess is to drift and wander without constancy.

Thus it is said: "He acts yet does not rely; he succeeds yet does not claim." To claim and possess—this the world does not grant. Not to claim yet possess—this the world does not take away. Hence it is said: "Only by not claiming does one not lose."

**Chapter Three: Not Exalting the Worthy**

Not exalting the worthy causes the people not to contend.
Not prizing rare goods causes the people not to steal.

The *Imperial Commentary* says: Exalting the worthy breeds cunning, leading to great turmoil throughout the world, where Confucians and Mohists all arise. Prizing goods breeds desire, leading to theft in broad daylight and burrowing through walls at noon.

Not exalting the worthy allows each person to settle into their allotted nature and destiny, with no cause for overreaching—hence it is said, "they do not contend." Not prizing goods allows each person to rest in the truth of their nature and destiny, with no cause for covetousness—hence it is said, "they do not steal."

*Zhuangzi* says: "Shave away the conduct of Zeng and Shi, silence the mouths of Yang and Mo, and the world’s virtue will begin to attain profound unity."

*The Canons of Lü* says: "Do not prize exotic things; value useful things, and the people will be sufficient."

Minister Yi says: When the sage governs the world with indulgent freedom, he extinguishes the heart of predation and advances the solitary will. Thus, nature is rectified and does not flow astray; emotions are guarded and do not run wild. Resting in nature, one comes to share the Dao; mastering emotions, one comes to share heaven’s principle. Thus, one returns joyfully to the realm of self-attainment, delighting in the domain of non-action.

After the Three Dynasties, the Dao was insufficient to overcome desire, stillness insufficient to restrain movement. People rubbed against honor and disgrace on the path of profit and loss, pursued extravagance beyond the bounds of form. Thus, the noble clung to the Dao and grasped at being, boasted of virtuous conduct, and admired those who forgot themselves. The petty turned their backs on the Dao and raced away, peddled prized goods, and pursued profit at the cost of their lives.

They exalted worthiness and despised those who did not measure up; I, seeing their superiority, resented my own failure to surpass them—this is how contention arises. They prized goods and surpassed what I lacked; I, seeing their pride, envied what they possessed—this is how theft arises. Thus, fame is the root of contention, and profit the origin of theft.

Those drawn to fame lose themselves in external pursuits; those addicted to profit perish in chasing after ends. Therefore, not exalting the worthy leaves the people without boastful ambition; not prizing goods leaves the people without covetous desire. When the heart of overreaching is forgotten, what contention can there be? When the hope of covetousness ceases, what theft can there be?

Shave away the conduct of Zeng and Shi, silence the mouths of Yang and Mo—then words and deeds will not stand, right and wrong will both vanish, and virtue will return to its original unity. Hence it is said: "Not exalting the worthy causes the people not to contend." Not prizing exotic things keeps the people from losing their constancy; not devaluing useful things keeps the people from turning from their roots—thus the people are sufficient. When the people are sufficient, they do not steal. Hence it is said: "Not prizing rare goods causes the people not to steal."

**Not Displaying Desirable Things**

Not displaying what may be desired keeps the heart from disorder.

The *Imperial Commentary* says: When people have desires, they follow the impulses of their nature and destiny to contend for them, leading to usurpation, deceit, and every kind of excess. Boyi saw fame as desirable and starved beneath Mount Shouyang. Robber Zhi saw profit as desirable and raged atop Dongling. Their heat scorched like fire, their cold congealed like ice—thus their hearts were disordered by passion and pride, and could not be bound to the Dao.

As for the sage, he does not seek profit, does not shun harm, does not rejoice in long life, does not grieve over early death, does not take glory in success, does not feel shame in poverty—what, then, is desirable? With no desires stirring, his heart is serene. When moved, he responds; when at rest, he is unobstructed. In action, he is not driven—who could disorder him?

Confucius was free from doubt at forty; Mencius said, "At forty, my heart did not waver." Minister Yi says: Abiding in stillness without change, one returns to the obscurity of unified virtue, merging with the Great Beginning. Spirit congeals in the Great Void; qi is preserved in harmonious emptiness. Seeing the things of the world, one deems them insufficient to shake one’s celestial abode—thus the heart rests in tranquility.

Those who are benighted engage in the five senses’ mutual strife, where likes and dislikes agitate their emotions; the six apertures contend, and the impulse to grasp and discard flows into falsity. The eyes’ deluded seeing, the mind’s deluded desires—daily they battle the heart in the space of encounter and construction. Thus the heart is confounded, and heavenly principle departs.

Boyi and Robber Zhi died by starvation and violence because they saw fame and profit as desirable. Scorched by fire’s heat, frozen by ice’s cold, they sought after gain and loss—so confounded and disordered, they were seized by what was desirable. Confucius’ freedom from doubt, Mencius’ unwavering heart—they knew there was nothing desirable, hence no doubt, no wavering. What disorder could there be?

Thus the sage’s governance empties the heart, fills the belly, weakens the will, and strengthens the bones, ever keeping the people without knowledge and without desire.

The *Imperial Commentary* says: The valley is receptive because it is empty; the mirror reflects because it is empty; the pipe and lock receive because they are empty; the ear hears because it is empty; the eye sees because it is empty; the nose smells because it is empty. If something is full within, it obstructs these functions.

The sage, compelled to oversee the world, regards all with impartial benevolence, values the near and raises the distant, follows what is inherently so, and leaves things to themselves—what room is there for personal intent? Yao’s elevation of Shun and employment of Gun were nearly sufficient.

With an empty heart, one listens and observes impartially, without the emotions of like and dislike. With a full belly, one is content and at peace, without the will to covet—how could worthiness be exalted, or goods be prized? The sage acts for the belly, not for the eyes—the belly accepts without discrimination.

The will is the direction of the heart; the bones are the foundation of the body. When the will is strong, one may pursue fame heedlessly or chase wealth insatiably, boast of achievements, or vaunt one’s abilities—ever farther from the Dao. When the bones are weak, conduct drifts and shifts, clashing and yielding with things, sinking together into dissolution without return.

The sage’s will always humbles itself, yet others exalt it; always stays behind, yet others place it first. Knowing the male, he keeps to the female; knowing glory, he keeps to disgrace—this is called weakening the will. By rectitude he halts it, and the myriad things cannot sway him; by firmness he holds it, and the ten thousand changes cannot topple him. The unchanging appearance, like that of Guangchengzi, who lived twelve hundred years without his form ever declining—this is called strengthening the bones.

*Zhuangzi* says: “In accord with non-knowing, one’s virtue does not depart; in accord with non-desire, this is called plain simplicity. When plain simplicity is attained, the people’s nature is preserved.” The sage’s governance aims only to let the people preserve their nature. Much knowledge harms the allotment of life and fate; many desires muddy the feelings of life and fate. To call this “governance” is to invite chaos—what could be worse? Thus, he constantly ensures the people remain without knowledge and without desire.

Minister Yi says: The Dao is stored in nothingness, so emptiness is the Dao’s counterpart. Things are burdened by being, so fullness is the accumulation of things. Man opens his Heavenly nature, and things bore holes in his desires. Those with form are enslaved by form; those with a heart are driven by the heart. When private desires prevail within and external things seize without, yin and yang contend in mutual support, and Heavenly harmony is lost in mutual ruin. The numinous terrace, the chamber of Great Emptiness, is already choked within—when things come, they merely pass through their old patterns and respond to their stimuli. How could this not be a grave error?

The valley is empty, so it echoes sounds from all directions; the mirror is empty, so it reflects forms without limit; the flute and pipe are empty, so they can receive the qi of emptiness. How much more so the heart, which governs the five senses and presides over the ten thousand virtues? It responds to stimuli and does not hoard—its essence lies in emptiness. Only emptiness returns to the absence of things, unites with the beginningless—this is called embracing simplicity. Embracing this is called guarding the One. Guarding this, Yanzi advanced in this way.

Unifying the heart to attain the One, attaining the One to gather emptiness, entering and roaming its bounds, reaching the state before the first turning—then one can fulfill one’s nature and leave no lingering emotions of love or hate. Following what is inherently so and leaving things to themselves—this is because the heart is empty. Obscuring right and wrong, unifying likes and dislikes, forgetting the dusty burdens, the heart becomes empty.

When Yao elevated Shun and employed Gun, what personal intent did he harbor? He responded with emptiness—what burden of likes and dislikes could there be? The belly, as a thing, contains without discrimination, receives without filling. The qi’s course, the transformations of things, all return to it. Inhalation and exhalation, beginning and end—there is no limit, never any sufficiency. Beset by insufficiency, one cannot but seek; unceasing seeking leads to peril. Thus, the belly must be full. Fullness brings contentment and peace, with nothing to await from without. The impulse for demands vanishes, and the blessing of tranquility arrives—what room is there for thoughts of profit or desire? The burden of likes and dislikes is gone—how could worthiness be exalted? The thought of profit and desire is extinguished—how could goods be prized?

The will is the heart’s direction. When the will is strong, its orientation knows no bounds—it wearies in scheming, exhausts the spirit, toils the thoughts, chases fame and wealth, boasts of achievements, vaunts abilities—when will it ever cease? Thus, the will must be weakened, turning from activity to stillness, from being to non-being. Keeping to the female to return to non-action, keeping to disgrace to rest in one’s allotted place—humbling oneself without seeking elevation, staying behind without seeking precedence, seeking to return to the Dao—this is called weakening the will.

The body’s foundation lies in the bones. When the bones are weak, one loses oneself to things, drifting with changes, following others’ comings and goings in conduct, clinging to others’ likes and dislikes in use and discard, clashing and yielding with things without knowing one’s error, sinking with things without knowing how to return. Unable to stand on one’s own, it comes to this. Thus, the bones must be strong. Strength brings independence and constancy, standing alone and having attainment, preserving Heaven’s guard without being swayed by things, completing Heaven’s firmness without being lost to others. Its movement is not perilous, its conduct is vigorous, its strength unceasing, daily advancing in the Dao—this is called strengthening the bones.

The sage governs his heart with emptiness, leaving nothing to divide; his belly is full, leaving nothing to seek; his will is weak, leaving no external pursuits; his bones are strong, leaving no sway by things. This is how he ensures the people remain without knowledge and unburdened by fame, without desire and unburdened by profit, preserving their carefree wandering in the realm of inherent attainment—thus returning to plain simplicity.

Knowledge means the heart is enslaved by things, so much knowledge harms the allotment of life and fate. Desires mean the emotions are swayed by things, so many desires muddy the feelings of life and fate. Returning to non-knowing, one does not use knowledge; reverting to non-desire, one does not see what can be desired. The essence lies in constancy—thus it is said: “Constantly ensure the people remain without knowledge and without desire.” This is to make those with knowledge dare not act.

The *Imperial Commentary* says: Those skilled in debate dare not gallop their words; the brave dare not flaunt their prowess; the capable dare not vaunt their talents; the wise dare not display their discernment. Creating cleverness, boasting of cunning, multiplying laws and decrees to overawe the multitude—these the sage all forbids and halts. This is what is meant by “making those with knowledge dare not act.” The nine offices all attend to their duties, and the worthy submit—how could knowledge be used to bore holes?

Executing the ruler’s commands, one merely conveys them to the people.

*Ministerial Exegesis* says: In the sage’s governance, he abides in non-action and practices wordless instruction. Above, he embraces the Dao to grant the world its freedom; below, he transforms and perfects through the Dao. The people, though possessed of much knowledge, revert to non-knowing; though possessed of many desires, revert to non-desire. The ultimate of perfect governance is a return to the Great Antiquity—this may be called perfection. Even those with knowledge, though their words be ready, their courage steadfast, their talents capable, their discernment keen—where could they apply them? They call themselves wise, yet how dare they act? To act is to fail; where could such action find a place? Thus it is said: “they dare not act.” By acting through non-action, there is nothing that is not governed.

The *Imperial Commentary* says: Does the sage’s governance abandon people and sever ties with things, standing aloof in a realm of no affairs? It is merely that his action arises from non-action. The myriad things’ transformations lie below form; the sage embodies the Dao, standing above the ten thousand things, comprehending their inherent principles to govern them. When things arise, he follows them to observe their return. When things are born, he accords with them to bring them to completion—how could there be anything not governed? Thus, when governance is perfected above, the sun, moon, and stars maintain their order; when governance is perfected below, birds, beasts, grasses, and trees fulfill their natures.

*Ministerial Exegesis* says: The sage roams the human world, responding to emperors and kings, yet stands above affairs. In transforming the people and shaping customs, he aims to restore their nature and return to simplicity—how could he be aloof in non-action? His action arises from non-action, yet it is not mere non-action. He assists the naturalness of the ten thousand things, letting them grow as they grow, come to completion as they complete themselves. Curving to preserve Heaven’s wholeness, without the calamities of obstruction or constraint—there is nothing not governed. The sun, moon, and stars maintain their order: this is governance above. Birds, beasts, grasses, and trees fulfill their natures: this is governance below. When governance above and below is thus, the people reach a state of non-knowing and non-desire, and even the knowledgeable dare not act—such is the perfection of governance.

**Chapter Four: The Dao’s Depth**
The Dao is empty, yet its use is inexhaustible.

The *Imperial Commentary* says: The Dao has essence and reliability, hence it has use; it is formless and without action, hence it does not overflow. The *Classic* says: “The ten thousand things bear yin and embrace yang, blending qi to achieve harmony.” In the principles of things, leaning toward yang leads to strength, but may err through excess; leaning toward yin leads to weakness, but may err through insufficiency. Without excess or insufficiency—this is called “blending qi.” “Blending” means the center, which is called “Great Harmony.” The high are restrained, the low are raised; the excessive are diminished, the insufficient are supplemented. The Dao’s use finds the center in every situation. Poured into yet never full, drawn from yet never exhausted—giving to others, one’s own abundance increases; bestowing upon others, one’s own wealth multiplies. The Dao’s essence resembles the Great Void, enveloping the six directions—how could it overflow?

*Ministerial Exegesis* says: The Dao is concealed before the Great Simplicity. As qi, it has not yet appeared; as form, it has not yet divided; as substance, it has not yet separated—what could be its essence? If even its essence is absent, what could be its use? Yet when the pivot of the Dao turns, the mechanism of Heaven unfolds: yin and yang, as qi, are subtly present in non-being; the ten thousand things, as form, manifest in being. In their endings and beginnings, their goings and comings, all have use, yet none know why it is so. Such is the Dao’s use—filling all without boundary, its responses boundless, nourishing the ten thousand things without exhaustion. Such is the Dao’s non-overflowing. It has essence and reliability, yet these do not depart from the mind—this is called “use,” for there is that which employs it. The Dao takes non-mind as its mind; those qualities of essence and reliability, in use, become its use. Thus it is said: “the Dao’s use.” Formless and without action, it does not fall into being—this is called “non-overflowing,” for it does not cling to vessels. The Dao takes wondrous being as being; those qualities of form, in essence, become its essence. Thus it is non-overflowing. Qi disperses in the Great Beginning, dividing into yin and yang. Thus qi is what the Dao moves, and things are what qi transforms. Those bound by number cannot escape the mechanism of yin and yang; those confined by form cannot transcend the creation of yin and yang. Thus yin and yang are what the ten thousand things bear and embrace, yet they have never been separate. Leaning toward yang leads to excess and strength; leaning toward yin leads to insufficiency and weakness. Using strength, its hardness will surely break; using weakness, its softness will surely fail. “Blending” is the Dao’s manifestation in qi. When qi is balanced, harmony is blending. Interpenetrating, they achieve harmony; intertwining, they become one. The use of hardness and softness knows no excess or insufficiency; the changes of coming and going know no discord. The two merge as one, attaining Great Harmony—there is nowhere it does not reach the center. Such is the use of blending. Blending and using it resembles the Great Void, filling the six directions, enveloping Heaven and Earth, its measure inexhaustible, its responses unceasing. High without above, vast without limit, deep without below, unfathomable without measure—how could it overflow?

The *Imperial Commentary* says: *Zhuangzi* says: “The stillness of deep pools is called a gulf; the stillness of still water is called a gulf; the stillness of flowing water is called a gulf.” A gulf is empty and still, unmingled with things—this is the Dao’s essence. Only because it is empty do all realities return to it; only because it is still do all movements belong to it. It is what the ten thousand things are tied to, what the single transformation awaits. Thus it is said: “resembles the ancestor of the ten thousand things.” Yet the Dao is originally without ties; things naturally take it as their ancestor—thus it is only “resembles.”

*Ministerial Exegesis* says: A gulf is still and unified, yet flowing and returning; empty and unified, yet deep in essence.

The return of the Dao is unmingled with things; its depth is unfathomable by them. Roaming at the origin of things, it merges with the Great Void—this is the essence of the Dao. All things are born of the Dao, thus the Dao is the root and ancestor of the ten thousand things.

The reality of all existence returns to perfect emptiness; the movement of all existence belongs to perfect stillness. Only through emptiness and stillness are the ten thousand beings encompassed, and the great transformation made wondrous.

Things take the Dao as their ancestor, yet the Dao never sets itself up as their ancestor—things naturally take it as such. Thus it is said: “resembles.”

Blunt its sharpness, untangle its tangles, soften its brilliance, merge with its dust.

The *Imperial Commentary* says: Sharpness wounds, tangles confuse. Blunting sharpness prevents contention; untangling tangles prevents disorder. Softening its brilliance is what *Zhuangzi* calls “shining yet not dazzling.” Merging with its dust is what *Zhuangzi* calls “flowing with things and moving with their waves.”

If inner sincerity remains unbroken, the form’s plans become radiant—so much so that those who lodge with one rise from their mats in deference. Is this not what “softening its brilliance” means? Adorning wisdom to startle the foolish, cultivating the self to expose others’ filth, shining forth like a banner, striding like the sun and moon—is this not what “merging with its dust” means?

When the sage blunts sharpness, he dwells among things without harming them, and nothing can harm him. When he untangles tangles, why would he scheme? Why would he cling? Nothing in the world can disturb his heart. Thus he shows no arrogance in countenance, no haughtiness in bearing. Harmonious yet not drifting, he merges with things, opening the minds of all under heaven. There is nowhere he enters and does not find himself at ease.

The *Ministerial Exegesis* says: Sharpness is the edge of things; tangles are the entanglements of affairs. If sharpness is not blunted, its edge will wound. If tangles are not untangled, their entanglements will confuse. Wounding leads to contention; confusion leads to disorder. Thus, blunting sharpness allows one to remain whole within Heaven; untangling tangles allows one to observe their return with clarity.

When the form’s plans become radiant, when the self is cultivated to expose filth, the self has not yet been forgotten. Establishing the self as a burden is far from the Dao. Shining yet not dazzling means one’s brilliance harmonizes with things without discord. Flowing with things and moving with their waves means one’s dust merges with things without distinction. At this stage, emotional attachments are entirely forgotten, self and other become mysteriously one. Distinctions between this and that vanish, and there is nowhere one enters without finding ease.

"Abysmal, as if it might exist."
The *Imperial Commentary* says: The heart is like dead ashes, the body like a withered tree’s branch. In perfect tranquility, the light of Heaven shines of itself. Dimly, as if vanished yet present; effortlessly, without form yet spiritually manifest—is this not the Dao?

The *Ministerial Exegesis* says: Concealing the Dao’s profundity without revealing it, sealing the mechanism of virtue without activating it, returning to non-action, roaming at the origin of things—the heart is like dead ashes, the body like a withered tree, yet the essence merges with the Great Void. Profound and obscure, faintly discernible, as if something were there—this is what Wenbo Xuezi grasped in an instant of perception. Vanished yet present, formless yet spiritually manifest—such is the divine understanding of the great sage, beyond all description.

Those who study the Dao study this; those who transmit the Dao transmit this.

"I do not know whose child it is—it seems to have existed before the Ancestor of Images."
The *Imperial Commentary* says: *Images* are the first appearances of things. *The Ancestor* is the spirit’s response to things. After things are born, images appear; after the Ancestor emerges, things are wondrously transformed. The Ancestor of Images is the beginning of all things, yet the Dao truly precedes it. This is what *Zhuangzi* calls “spiritualizing ghosts and gods, giving birth to Heaven and Earth.”

Invisible, inaudible, intangible—it emerges yet no one sees its gate. Who knows it? Thus it is said: “I do not know whose child it is—it seems to have existed before the Ancestor of Images.”

The *Ministerial Exegesis* says: All that moves from non-being to being is attached to things, thus all things are born of the Dao. All that moves from being to non-being returns to the Dao, thus the Dao is the ancestor of the ten thousand things. The Dao originates from non-being—from what could it be born? Thus it is said: “I do not know whose child it is.” Things belong to being and are born of the Dao, thus it is said: “it seems to have existed before the Ancestor of Images.”

When things appear as images, *images* mark their beginning. When spirit responds to things, *the Ancestor* marks their beginning. Images and the Ancestor are the beginnings of all things, yet both originate from the Dao. Thus it is said: “it seems to have existed before the Ancestor of Images.”

*Exegesis on the True Meaning of the Dao and Its Virtue by Emperor Huizong of Song, Fascicle One—Concluded*
*Exegesis on the True Meaning of the Dao and Its Virtue by Emperor Huizong of Song, Fascicle Two*

**Exegesis by Gentleman-Official Zhang An**

**The Dao Scripture, Chapter Five: Heaven and Earth**

"Heaven and earth are not benevolent;
they regard the ten thousand things as straw dogs.
The sage is not benevolent;
he regards the hundred clans as straw dogs."

*Imperial Commentary* states: Favor gives rise to harm, and harm gives rise to favor. To take benevolence as favor is to invite harm in its wake. Heaven and earth, in their relation to the ten thousand things, and the sage, in his relation to the hundred clans, assist their natural course without any mind of love or benefit—thus benevolence cannot be named in this.

Straw dogs, bound for sacrificial use, are employed when needed and discarded when their purpose is past. As for the self-arising of the ten thousand things and the self-governing of the hundred clans, how could there be any room for deliberate intent?

*Minister’s Exegesis* states: Great benevolence is not benevolent—not that it is truly without benevolence, but to take benevolence as favor is to make it shallow. With harm following so closely, can benevolence be relied upon? In the age of the Great Dao, all things arise spontaneously, all attain their due—what need is there for benevolence?

Heaven and earth feel no love for things, yet things fulfill their allotted portion and thus share in spontaneous attainment. The sage feels no love for the hundred clans, yet the hundred clans find their own harmony and forget one another in the absence of emotion—this is why heaven and earth and the sage regard the ten thousand things and the hundred clans as straw dogs.

Heaven and earth, in their relation to things, and the sage, in his relation to the people, are neither without mind nor with mind. They allow things to arise as they naturally arise, to be governed as they naturally govern. They respond to the moment and employ what is fitting, yet their employment does not hoard. When the task is done, they set it aside, and their setting aside does not linger—how could there be room for deliberate intent? All is but the assistance of the natural.

"Between heaven and earth, is it not like a bellows?"
*Imperial Commentary* states: The bellows is empty yet able to receive; having received, it is able to respond. Thus it responds without exhaustion. If it were filled with something solid, it would be obstructed at every turn—this would be a partiality in the Dao, a one-sidedness in things.

*Minister’s Exegesis* states: Between heaven and earth, the movement of the single qi opens and closes, transforms, enters and exits, moves and rests—no trace of old or new, no beginning or end. Is it not like a bellows?

The bellows, as a thing, receives because of its emptiness, and its reception does not refuse. It responds because of its emptiness, and its response does not exhaust. If it were filled with something solid, its use would cease.

Heaven and earth animate the ten thousand things—how could they have a mind? Things move of themselves in perfect emptiness, and heaven and earth allow them to be thus. The sage, in his relation to the hundred clans, responds to their spontaneity without entanglement—thus he is compared to a discarded bellows:

"Empty yet inexhaustible,
the more it moves, the more it yields."

*Imperial Commentary* states: To empty oneself and wander through the world is to respond freely and meet every circumstance—thus it is said, "empty yet inexhaustible." Pressed to act, one’s capacity is deployed without depletion—thus it is said, "the more it moves, the more it yields." This is how the sage emerges to respond to emperors and kings without being burdened by words.

*Minister’s Exegesis* states: The qi dwelling in the bellows animates the ten thousand things because the bellows, being empty, can receive and respond. In stillness, it is empty, and qi returns to nothingness without ever being exhausted. In movement, it is empty, and qi responds to being without ever being depleted.

The sage empties himself and responds freely, meeting every circumstance without his Dao being exhausted. In dynamic transformation, his capacity is deployed without depletion, and his Dao ever unfolds—how could words be a burden?

"Much speech leads to frequent exhaustion;
it is better to guard the center."

*Imperial Commentary* states: The bellows waits in emptiness for qi; when qi arrives, it sounds, and when qi does not arrive, it rests. The sage’s words are like this. The confines of the disputatious are such that many words inevitably lead to entanglement—it is better to guard the center.

Be cautious within, close off without, withdraw your vision and reverse your hearing. Return thus to perceive the heart-mind of heaven and earth—this is called "guarding the center."

*Minister’s Exegesis* states: The sage is fundamentally without words; pressed by necessity, he speaks. His words are timely and fitting, responding to others and nothing more—like the emptiness of the bellows, sounded by qi.

When words accord with principle, they do not exhaust. The words of disputatious ones collide with right and wrong; though many, they quickly reach exhaustion. Only when good and evil are both forgotten and right and wrong are not established can one be empty and without entanglement. Without entanglement, there is no partiality; without partiality, one attains the center. Abiding in the center and guarding it, one’s mind becomes vast and forgetful, returning to the celestial unity. The heart-mind of heaven and earth may thus be perceived.

**Chapter Six: The Valley Spirit**

"The valley spirit does not die."
*Imperial Commentary* states: Where there is form, there is flourishing and decay; where there is number, there is completion and dissolution. Form and number together determine life and death—this is the principle of things.

The valley responds to all movements yet remains ever empty; the spirit works wonders upon the ten thousand things yet remains ever still. Within this true constancy, it is one with the Dao, neither clinging to form nor falling into number. It generates life without end, like the sun and moon, which never cease through all ages, like the Northern Dipper, which never errs through all ages—thus it is said, "does not die."

*Minister’s Exegesis* states: Confined by yin and yang, flourishing and decay take form. Enslaved by transformation, completion and dissolution follow number. Once form and number are involved, how can life be secure?

The valley, ever empty, responds to the stirrings of the multitude. The spirit, ever still, works wonders above the ten thousand things. Ever empty, it is not burdened by form; ever still, it is not bound by number. The emptiness of the valley and the wonder of the spirit merge in unity, sharing the same body as the Dao. Generating life without end, its responses are ceaseless; its transformations, unfathomable—like the sun and moon, the Northern Dipper, all enduring through all ages without ever ceasing or erring. Thus it is said, "does not die."

The reason it does not die is that it has no origin from which to die. The spirit is supreme, yet it is called the "valley spirit" because qi merges with emptiness, and the spirit lies hidden within qi. The spirit’s response is like an echo—able to contain, able to respond, able to be empty, able to be full, without end. Thus it is spoken of in terms of the valley.

"This is called the Mysterious Female."

The *Imperial Commentary* states: All things receive their mandate from Non-being and take form in Being. The valley’s function is without appearance; the spirit’s essence is without direction—this is how all things receive their mandate. "Mysterious" is the color of Heaven; "Female" is the kind of Earth—this is how all things take form. The "Valley Spirit" is likened to the constancy of the Supreme Dao; the "Mysterious Female" reveals the marvel of creation.

Minister Yi says: The Valley Spirit receives the mandate of the myriad things through Non-being; Heaven and Earth bestow form upon the myriad things through Being. Yet it is not called "Heaven and Earth" but "Mysterious Female" because it is spoken of in terms of their color and kind. Color and kind originate in qi, and thus the Mysterious Female is the means by which qi generates things. As the Mother, it nurtures the myriad things, and the principle of ceaseless generation is the marvel of creation. The gateway of the Mysterious Female is called the Root of Heaven and Earth.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: *Zhuangzi* says, "All things have that from which they are born, yet none see its root; all things have that from which they emerge, yet none see its gate." Only the sage perceives it. Thus it is here made clear: the gateway of the Mysterious Female is called the Root of Heaven and Earth. Heaven and Earth are the above and below of the myriad things. Things and Heaven and Earth have no before or after in origin, but when the sequence of the Great Dao is made manifest, there are first Heaven and Earth, and then the myriad things. Yet that from which Heaven and Earth themselves emerge is none other than the Mysterious Female. Who has ever seen that which precedes Heaven and Earth?

Minister Yi says: The Mysterious Female is the root of all generation, its substance belonging to yin and yang, the Mother of the myriad things. The "gate" implies opening and closing; the "root" implies the principle of generation and emergence. The qi of the Mysterious Female opens to become yang, and that which clings to yang is what it generates; it closes to become yin, and that which clings to yin is what it brings forth. Heaven and Earth are spoken of as the great forms of yin and yang. The myriad things emerge from this gate, yet none can see it—thus it is called the gateway of the Mysterious Female. "Unceasing, as if ever-present; its use is never forced."

The *Imperial Commentary* states: It is self-originating, self-rooted, enduring from antiquity—its end is unknown. This is what is meant by "unceasing, as if ever-present." Vastly, it is the creation of Heaven, entrusting itself to the self-movement of the single qi; suddenly, it is the transformation of Earth, committing the myriad forms to self-propagation. Qian is easy to know; Kun is simple in its power. It is not by forceful governance—how could there be any exertion?

Minister Yi says: The myriad things rely on qi for life and on spirit for numinosity. Thus qi is the mother of spirit, and spirit is the child of qi. When qi is firm, spirit is whole; when qi is turbid, spirit is disordered; when qi is lost, spirit departs. "Unceasing" means drawing out without end, continuing without cessation—existing before Heaven and Earth, enduring beyond Heaven and Earth. Dim and indistinct, yet it seems to be present—thus it is called "as if ever-present." Vastly, it is the creation of Heaven, entrusting itself to the self-movement of qi, yet none know how it is so. Suddenly, it is the transformation of Earth, committing the forms to self-propagation, yet none know whence they come. Its function is non-action, and it is not something that wisdom or effort can achieve—thus it is said, "its use is never forced."

---

**Chapter Seven: Heaven and Earth Endure**

Heaven and Earth endure. The reason Heaven and Earth can be long and enduring is that they do not live for themselves—thus they can live long.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: Heaven arches above, its position on high, its warp the sun and moon, its weft the stars, and the myriad things are covered by it. Earth spreads below, its position beneath, congealing into mountains and ridges, melting into rivers and marshes, and the myriad things are borne upon it. The myriad things are covered and borne by Heaven and Earth, yet Heaven and Earth have no intent toward them. Thus Heaven moves ceaselessly in its firmness, and Earth abides immovably in its stability—this is how they can be long and enduring. If Heaven and Earth had intent toward the generation of things, then Heaven could be measured in an instant, and its covering of things would be shallow; Earth could be fathomed in an instant, and its bearing of things would be thin. If this were so, they would depend on something else to live—how then could they live long?

Minister Yi says: Returning to Non-being, who commands its form? Merging with the beginningless, who possesses its life? At the inception of bestowed life, it is one with the Great Void. Congealing into my form is like water becoming ice. The people of the world, once they receive their formed bodies, take form as their own possession; recognizing their having life, they take life as their inherent nature. Thus when form is diminished, it is not whole; when life is severed, it is not embraced. Suddenly, it passes away—what difference is there from a fleeting glimpse? They do not know that the reason Heaven and Earth can be long and enduring is that they do not live for themselves. Heaven arches above, yet it has never had intent to cover things; Earth spreads below, yet it has never had intent to bear things. Their forms are those of Heaven and Earth, yet they do not know why they are so. Their function is to cover and bear, yet they do not know why they do so. Their long endurance is not because they live for themselves. Not living for themselves, life has never truly lived. Though dwelling in having life, they are not burdened by the cares of life—thus they can live long.

Therefore, the sage places himself behind, yet finds himself ahead; treats himself as external, yet finds himself preserved.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: Heaven moves above; Earth abides below. The sage stands between Heaven and Earth. When comprehended as the Three Powers, they have a mutually communicating function. When distinguished as the Three Ultimates, they have their individually established natures. When intermingled as the Three Spirits, they possess the numinous that leaves nothing unmarvelous. Thus Heaven and Earth and the sage all attain the Dao, and the sage’s way of ordering himself is none other than that of Heaven and Earth. Therefore, this chapter first speaks of Heaven and Earth not living for themselves, and then follows with the sage not possessing his own self. All people seek to be first; he alone takes the rear, saying he bears the world’s disgrace—this is called placing himself behind. Placing himself behind, he does not contend with things, and none in the world can contend with him—thus it is said, "places himself behind, yet finds himself ahead."

The sage on the path does not contend over the advantages of peril or ease; in winter and summer, he does not contend over the harmony of yin and yang. He puts death and life beyond concern, leaves misfortune and fortune behind, and his spirit is never once distressed—this is called putting his self beyond, yet his self endures.

If the sage’s way of ordering himself is thus, how much more so the things beyond his self? Meeting them without resistance, passing them without attachment, he embraces his nature and holds fast to the spirit, roaming among the customs of the world. His form will right itself, self and other become one. Born before Heaven and Earth, yet not thereby long-lived; enduring since high antiquity, yet not thereby old—are these not the effects of such unity?

Minister Yi says: If one establishes himself to precede things, then things become his adversaries, and he is overcome by them, yet his self has never truly come first. If one preserves the self through attachment to the self, then the self is lost to things, and he is overthrown by them, yet his self has never truly endured. This is the inherent principle.

The sage stands between Heaven and Earth and completes them. He can penetrate them as the Three Ultimates, distinguish them as the Three Natures, and intermingle them as the Three Spirits, merging without gap and embodying the Dao in all—his attainment is no different. Thus this chapter first speaks of Heaven and Earth not living for themselves, so they endure long and last. It then speaks of the sage putting his self behind and beyond, so he comes first and endures.

The sage has no mind to be first for himself, so he places himself behind, and none in the world can match his precedence. He has no mind to preserve himself, so he puts his self beyond, and none in the world can match his endurance. His self’s precedence, his self’s endurance—both transcend the burdens of self and other, for he has never truly possessed a self. Is it not because he is without self-interest that he achieves his self-interest?

The *Yu Zhu* says: The great virtue of Heaven and Earth is life; the great treasure of the sage is position. The Dao makes these public. Heaven and Earth embody the Dao, so they are without self-interest; without self-interest, they endure long. The sage embodies the Dao, so he is without self-interest; without self-interest, he endures always. Self-seeking is self-interest, yet none who are self-seeking can achieve their self-interest.

Minister Yi says: That Heaven, Earth, and the sage are without self-interest is because they are without self. Self-interest arises from attachment to the self, yet none who are self-interested can achieve their self-interest. Heaven and Earth have no self-serving life, so they live long; the sage has no self-serving attachment to his body, so he endures always—thus it is said he achieves his self-interest.

**Chapter Eight: The Highest Virtue Resembles Water**

The highest virtue resembles water.

The *Yu Zhu* says: The *Book of Changes* states, "The alternation of yin and yang is called the Dao; that which continues it is called virtue." *Zhuangzi* says: When virtue departs from the Dao to become goodness, once the name of goodness is established, the substance of the Dao is diminished. Yet the Heavenly One generates water, which has not departed far from the Dao. Profound and empty, still and luminous—this is called the most refined essence under Heaven, thus it is said the highest virtue resembles water.

Minister Yi says: Still and unmoving, returning to the wondrous constant—this is the Dao. Responding to things and emerging in succession to the Dao—this is virtue. Returning to the utmost Dao, virtue has no place to stand; this is called the highest virtue. Once the Dao is departed from, the name of virtue is established, and the substance of the Dao is diminished. As for water, it is born of the Heavenly One, an emanation of the Dao. Water nearly is the Dao—it has not departed far. Emerging from emptiness and nothingness, flowing into fullness and being—this is how it is when issuing from the Dao. Profound and empty, its depth can unite with the Dao; still and luminous, its unity can return to the Dao—thus it has not departed far from the Dao. Issuing from the Dao yet not far from the Dao, therefore it is said the highest virtue resembles water.

Water benefits the myriad things and does not contend, dwelling where the multitude of people abhor—thus it is near to the Dao.

The *Yu Zhu* says: Melting into rain and dew, it nourishes the ten thousand forms. Congealing into frost and snow, it completes the ten thousand treasures. Channeling into rivers and streams, pooling into marshes and islets; springs deep and seas vast, drawing from and storing within, enriching all. Generating and nurturing the myriad things, all come to rely upon it without exhaustion. In benefiting the myriad things, what could be more virtuous than this? Benefiting the myriad things, the myriad things receive its grace and its bestowal, yet it always dwells in the place of softness and non-contention, accepting filth and receiving impurity without self-regard, unburdened in its heart—thus it is near to the Dao. *Ji* means near.

Minister Yi says: The governance and function of the Five Phases are great, yet water is their root. The generation and nurturing of the myriad things are vast, yet water provides their life. Extending its benefits to things, those who receive it do not know whence it comes. Things receive its grace and benefit from it, yet do not know how it reaches them. Dwelling in a place of non-contention, yet benefiting the myriad things, they do not know how it is virtuous. The filth of flowing streams, the impurities of the multitude of things—these are what those with minds abhor. Water accepts filth and receives impurity, dwelling where the multitude of people abhor, yet does not know how it is virtuous. Abhorring yet not knowing its virtue, abhorring yet not knowing its abhorrence—thus it is near to the Dao.

Dwells in good earth,
The *Yu Zhu* says: Flowing within the earth, it moves without overflowing.
Minister Yi says: Water flows within the earth—this is its nature. Its flow tends downward without overflowing, thus it is called good. If earth were like water’s goodness, earth would have found its proper dwelling.

Heart good as an abyss,
The *Yu Zhu* says: The more one measures it, the deeper it becomes; the more one exhausts it, the farther it extends.
Minister Yi says: Empty and still, deep beyond measure; obscure and bright, distant beyond exhaustion—thus the heart is good as water’s abyss.

Accords with good benevolence,
The *Yu Zhu* says: Loving all impartially, bestowing without discrimination.
Minister Yi says: Loving all impartially—this is the breadth of love. Bestowing without discrimination—this is the vastness of giving. Broad and vast, benevolence is perfected. Thus it accords with water’s good benevolence.

Words good in trust,
The *Yu Zhu* says: Avoiding obstacles to reach all seas, traversing peril without losing its trust...

Minister Yi says: Avoiding obstacles and traversing peril, branching into myriad turns, flowing through the earth’s midst, and converging into the sea—thus words are good in trust as water.

Accords with good governance,
The *Yu Zhu* says: It cleanses the filthy, levels the treacherous, following the principle of things without imposing its will—thus nothing remains untended.
Minister Yi says: Cleansing filth to clarity, leveling peril to evenness, yielding to the self-so of principle—this is water’s governance. Thus governance accords with water’s good governance.

Accords with good ability,
The *Yu Zhu* says: Adapting to the land’s contours as curves or straightness, conforming to vessels as square or round, flowing without constancy yet ever level—can anything be so capable?
Minister Yi says: Curved or straight, round or square, adapting to change without uniformity, responding as fitting, ever attaining balance—this is water’s ability. Thus affairs accord with water’s good ability.

Moves with good timing,
The *Yu Zhu* says: When yang dissolves it, it flows; when yin congeals it, it freezes. Flowing eastward when positioned in the east, westward when in the west, moving without constraint, simply following the time.
Minister Yi says: Flowing when the time is open, halting when obstructed, dissolving with yang, congealing with yin, moving with the time, yet moving without constraint—thus movement accords with water’s good timing.

Only by not contending does one avoid blame.
The *Yu Zhu* says: The sage embodies the Dao to govern the self, ensuring long-lasting existence. Embracing all with virtue brings benefit; dwelling where none contend, one occupies the ground of non-contention. *Zhuangzi* says: Is it easy to possess? To possess is to defy heaven’s propriety. To act without striving and seldom err is easy; to act and remain free from harm is difficult. Having acted for benefit, to avoid blame is thus difficult. The highest good benefits things as water does by nature: though it benefits all, it does not choose what to benefit; though it does not contend with things, nothing can contend with it—thus it is free from blame. Therefore, nothing in the world is softer or weaker than water, yet in attacking the hard and strong, none can surpass it.

Minister Yi says: Water embraces all virtues yet does not claim them as its own, thus it acts without striving and nearly attains the Dao. People may possess all virtues yet not claim them as their own, thus they do not contend and avoid blame.

**Chapter Nine**
Hold it and fill it, yet it is better to cease.
Hone it to a point, yet it cannot be long preserved.
The *Yu Zhu* says: Fullness leads to overflow, sharpness to blunting. The principle of all things is that fullness must incur loss.
