Not knowing to hold back and yield precedence, to grasp emptiness to govern fullness—if one indulges in the desires of overflowing fullness, this only increases the calamity of collapse. Thus, it is better to cease. The transformations of things are endless, yet human wisdom is finite. Foreknowledge is the flower of the Dao, the beginning of folly. Gauging the nature of things and sharpening one’s pursuit of advancement—can wisdom, limited as it is, long preserve this?

Minister Yi says: Those skilled in holding fullness do not overflow; overflow brings the peril of collapse. Those skilled in gauging things do not sharpen; sharpness brings the setback of failure. Unable to forget possession, one busies oneself with prideful restraint—though momentary fullness may not yet topple, in the end there is the danger of overflow. Unable to forget emotion, one excels in calculation—though sharp advancement may repeatedly hit the mark, in the end there is the burden of exhaustion. Holding fullness and honing sharpness establish the self. With a self, things become adversaries. As things arise endlessly, to rely on the self to overcome them is perilous.

Gold and jade fill the hall, yet none can guard them.
Wealth and honor breed arrogance, inviting one’s own misfortune.
The *Yu Zhu* says: Gold, jade, wealth, and honor are not the principles of life and destiny; they are external things, unreliable and transient. Those who treasure gold and jade are burdened by things. Burdened by things, can they avoid loss? Thus, none can guard them. Wealth and honor breed arrogance, harming virtue. Harming virtue, can one escape misfortune? Thus, one invites one’s own misfortune. The sage does not confine the world’s benefits for private gain, nor does he regard ruling all under heaven as a personal display of eminence. How could he treasure gold and jade or be burdened by wealth and honor? Thus, the wealthiest state accumulates wealth without end, the most honored state accumulates rank without peer—yet the Dao remains unchanging.

Minister Yi says: External things are all transient, borrowed and recognized as one’s own, yet equally subject to decay. Gold, jade, wealth, and honor belong to the external; life, destiny, virtue, and the Dao reside within. To forget what is within and chase what is without is to lose one’s natural truth and drift into the illusory—this is the folly of the deluded. Gold and jade are treasures of the world, yet not inherently one’s own. Relying on them as possessions only burdens one with things. Burdened by things, can one avoid loss? Wealth and honor are what people desire, yet not inherently one’s own. Possessing them with arrogance only harms one’s virtue. Harming virtue, can one escape misfortune? The sage, free from the burden of self, though ruling all under heaven, does not regard it as a personal display of eminence. Free from the burden of things, though seizing the world’s benefits, he does not claim them for private gain—this is because he embodies the Dao, and thus his Dao remains unchanging.

When merit is achieved and fame attained, withdraw the self—this is the Way of Heaven.
The *Yu Zhu* says: Achievement leads to decline, fame to loss. The sun reaches its zenith and then wanes; the moon waxes and then wanes—this is the principle of things. The sage perceives the cycle of formation and decay, recognizing the inevitability of fullness and emptiness. Transcending all, self-possessed, unburdened by things, free from the peril of arrogance and fullness—who but one who knows Heaven could attain this? Thus, when merit is achieved and fame attained, withdraw the self—this is the Way of Heaven. The revolutions of the four seasons, where achievement leads to departure—this is the Way of Heaven. Knowing advance but not retreat, knowing existence but not demise, knowing gain but not loss—can one avoid regret?

Yi Yin said: "Your servant dares not dwell in success through favor or profit."
Your servant comments: Yin and yang alternate in their movements; cold and heat succeed one another. Where there is advance, there must be retreat; where there is fulfillment, there must be decline. Heaven bestows achievement upon things, yet its function lies in withdrawal and concealment—this is the natural Way. Those who regard merit and fame as their own affairs, advancing without knowing retreat, may possess the skill of maintaining fullness and the craft of wielding sharpness, yet can they avoid regret? The sage perceives the principle of formation and decay, recognizes the numerical patterns of fullness and emptiness, understands the subtle shifts of creation, and discerns the interdependence of fortune and misfortune—thus free from the burden of merit and fame. Knowing advance and retreat, existence and demise, they do not lose their alignment, attaining the Way of Heaven.

**Chapter Ten: Bearing the Corporeal Soul**

"Bearing the corporeal soul"—
The *Imperial Commentary* states: The *po* (corporeal soul) is yin, clinging to the body and having a fixed abode; thus, Master Lao speaks of it as being *nurtured* (*ying*). The *hun* (ethereal soul) is yang, entrusted to qi and going everywhere without restriction; thus, the *Book of Changes* speaks of it as *wandering*. The sage governs form with spirit, restrains the *po* with the *hun*, so that spirit constantly *bears* the *hun* and is not borne by the *po*—like a chariot conveying all things, like the seasons carrying cold and heat in their course. The heart roams freely in the heavens, the six senses interpenetrate, transcending heaven and earth, leaving behind the myriad things, so that spirit is never constrained—how could it be fettered by the *po*?

Your servant comments: The *hun* is entrusted to qi, and spirit pervades all without exception—this pertains to yang. The *po* clings to the body, and its nurturing has a fixed abode—this pertains to yin. When spirit bears the *hun*, life is spiritual; when it follows the *po*, death ensues. The sage’s qi is complete and firm, their spirit whole; their entry and exit, their transformations, are unburdened by any attachment to form. With yang prevailing over yin, the *hun* restraining the *po*, spirit constantly bears the *hun* and is not borne by the *po*. As for transcending heaven and earth, leaving behind the myriad things, moving alone and returning alone, responding to transformations without end—yet spirit never suffers constraint—this is because it is not borne by the *po*. The term *nurturing* (*ying*) implies a sense of lodging, settling, and gathering—referring to the *po*’s abode. The term *bearing* (*zai*) implies a sense of conveyance and movement—referring to spirit’s wandering. Thus, *bearing* is used for the *hun*, and *nurturing* for the *po*.

"Embracing the One, can you not depart from it?"
The *Imperial Commentary* states: Heaven’s One generates water, which in things becomes essence. Earth’s Two generates fire, which in things becomes spirit. Spirit and essence are born of the Dao; form fundamentally arises from essence. Guarding it without loss, uniting with spirit as one, essence and spirit merge without separation. With essence gathering spirit, with spirit employing form, with form preserving spirit—essence remains whole and unharmed, spirit is used yet inexhaustible, form lives without decay. Like the sun and moon adhering to the heavens, like grasses and trees adhering to the earth, they never depart.

I have ventured to elaborate: When a person is born, essence gathers spirit, and the bodily form is thereby complete. Flowing in all four directions, reaching everywhere without limit, above it joins with heaven, below it coils upon the earth, transforming and nurturing the myriad things—yet it cannot be represented by any image, and is called "equal to the Emperor." Yet the foolish of the world enslave themselves to things, lose their nature to custom, not for a single moment inwardly preserving spirit, while their boundless desires run outward, scattering their essence. The *hun* returns to follow the *po*, form becomes a burden to spirit, and they descend to be transformed along with the myriad things—is this not delusion? The sage is not thus. Bearing the *po* to achieve communion, embracing the One to guard it, embodying spirit in stillness—form will naturally align. Their spirit traverses Mount Tai without changing, dwells in deep springs without being drenched—who knows its beginning, who knows its end? Thus, the sage values essence.

Your servant comments: The Dao generates the One; the One is the generative number of water, the root of essence—thus, Heaven’s One, water, becomes essence. The One generates the Two; the Two is the generative number of fire, the source of spirit—thus, Earth’s Two, fire, becomes spirit. The primordial qi of the One, blending with the Great Void, is originally formless; essence arises from it. When essence is firmly rooted in the One, spirit gathers and becomes complete. When essence gathers spirit, spirit’s function is inexhaustible; when spirit governs form, form lives without decay. Thus, essence is the root of life, and the One is the number of essence. When essence is firm and unshaken, it returns to the One; when the One is embraced without departure, essence is whole. When essence is whole, spirit is whole; when spirit is whole, it harmonizes with the transformations of things and ascends to wander with the Creator. In stillness, it embodies the Dao; in action, it accords with principle—who can fathom its beginning or end? Those drowned in excessive desires, entangled in external engagements, scatter their essence and lose their numinosity; spirit is worn down by form, the *hun* is borne by the *po*, sinking into darkness. Yin prevails, and they become the lowest of fools, their heavenly principle extinguished. Only those who embrace the One without departure, though traversing myriad changes, keep their spirit whole in the One.

"Concentrating qi to attain suppleness, can you be like an infant?"
The *Imperial Commentary* states: The *Book of Changes* says: "In its stillness, Qian is *concentrated*." Yang Xiong said: "Harmony and suppleness suffice to pacify things." Stillness without admixture is called *concentration*; harmony without violence is called *suppleness*. The infant dwells without knowing what it does, moves without knowing where it goes, harboring no judgments of right or wrong, beauty or ugliness—thus its qi is concentrated and attains suppleness. Mencius said: "The stumbler and the hastener—this is qi—but it reversely agitates the heart. If the heart is insufficient to concentrate qi, then qi may stumble or hasten improperly, and the heart may become indignant or arrogant, beyond control." The sage empties the self to wander through the world, the heart does not forcibly direct qi—thus, in stillness without admixture, in harmony without violence, how do they differ from an infant? Hence, it is said: "Can you be like an infant?" Mencius said: "This qi is most great and most strong; nurtured with uprightness and without harm, it fills all between heaven and earth." Why then does Master Lao speak of *concentrating qi* as *attaining suppleness*?

The utmost hardness is employed to enact righteousness; the attainment of suppleness is used to restore nature—such was the art of the Dao in antiquity, present in all things without exception.

Minister Yi says: Qi is that which the Dao moves; things are that which qi transforms. Qi resides within heaven and earth, issuing forth and returning, while the myriad things live and die through qi. Thus, qi is the origin of the myriad things, and humans are especially noble in their ability to nurture it well.

Mencius said: "The stumbler and the hastener—this is qi—but it reversely agitates the heart." Yang Xiong said: "Qi is the steed that carries one toward good or evil." Therefore, the nurturing of qi depends fundamentally on concentration. In stillness without change, in unity without admixture, qi attains its concentration—how then could there remain the burden of stumbling or hastening, of the heart being moved, or of good and evil losing their proper course? When qi is concentrated, it is still and unified, constant and harmonious, without violence. By attaining suppleness to preserve one’s true nature, the body unites with the Dao, becoming equal to an infant.

When the fire of the heart blazes within and the steed of qi stumbles without, the heart of violence and force prevails while the virtue of pure harmony wanes. Joy and anger contend like yin and yang; desires and cravings cling to sounds and sights—this is how people fall into the calamity of reckless action and cannot return. Having cleansed and beheld the mysterious, can one be without flaw?

The *Imperial Commentary* says: The sage uses this to wash the heart, thereby cleansing all actions without possession. Retiring to concealment in the profound, one silently comprehends the wondrous principle. Such a one embodies purity and simplicity without encumbrance, neither regretting transgressions nor self-satisfied in correctness—what flaw could there be?

Minister Yi says: When the heart returns to nothingness, emotions vanish and defilements are exhausted; the heart fuses and the form is released—thus it is said, "cleansing the myriad things without possession." In profound union with the Dao, one leaves behind people and departs from things, clearly perceiving the solitary and silently comprehending the wondrous principle—thus it is said, "beholding the mysterious and silently comprehending." Having cleansed and beheld the mysterious, self and other become profoundly one—what flaw could remain?

In loving the people and governing the state, can one practice non-action?

The *Imperial Commentary* says: To love the people with benevolence and govern the state with wisdom, to enact teachings and establish laws, to bring goodness to the world—this is difficult to achieve through non-action. The sage’s beneficence extends to all generations without being for the sake of loving others; his achievements cover the world yet seem not to originate from himself—thus he practices non-action, employing the world with abundance.

Minister Yi says: To love the people with benevolence is still not free from the heart of intention; to govern the state with wisdom is still not free from the use of knowledge. Such is the labor of being bound to the heart and intellect, of toiling in action. Not acting for the sake of love, love pervades the people; not acting for the sake of governance, governance is complete in the state.

Can you open and close the gates of heaven while remaining the female?

The *Imperial Commentary* says: Knowing the male, yet guarding the female, one becomes the valley of the world. The sage embodies the transformations of heaven’s Dao, rolling and unfolding, opening and closing, never contrary to the times. Whether soft or hard, subtle or manifest, he employs them as needed. Yet he never precedes others but always follows them; he never overcomes things but always yields to them. Thus, the world delights in exalting him without weariness—this is how he remains the female.

Minister Yi says: The opening and closing of heaven’s gates are the movement of yang. Yang moves naturally, hence it is called "heaven." The Dao has its issuing forth and returning, hence it is called "gates." The sage has no intention to act, yet in opening and closing, in transformation, he responds without contrivance—is this not being the female?

With clarity extending in all four directions, can one be without knowledge?

The *Imperial Commentary* says: To guard wisdom with foolishness shares the same meaning as this. Minister Yi says: The tassels and floss of the ceremonial cap are designed to restrain brilliance; guarding it with foolishness means having no use for knowledge. With clarity extending in all four directions, one does not employ knowledge—this is how true wisdom is achieved.

To give life and to nurture, to give life without possession, to act without reliance, to grow without domination—this is called profound virtue.

The *Imperial Commentary* says: The sage preserves the spirit and knows transformation, uniting with the Dao in body—thus he matches the divine luminaries and nurtures the myriad things without exception. He gives life to fulfill their nature and nurtures them to perfect their sustenance. Without the heart of love or profit, he gives life without possession. Without the conduct of self-praise, he acts without reliance. Without the skill of carving or controlling, he grows without domination. Such virtue is deep and far-reaching, returning to things in reversal—thus it is called profound virtue.

Heaven’s Dao ascends in the north, distinguishing itself from things. Yet the profound is the color of heaven, and when the sage descends from heaven’s Dao to become virtue, nothing but the profound suffices to name it.

Minister Yi says: When the sage embraces the One and concentrates qi, reaching the point of profound vision, that which is within him is perfected. From practicing non-action and remaining the female to attaining non-knowledge, his responses to things are perfected. Within himself yet forgetting himself, within things yet forgetting things, uniting with the Dao in body—thus he sees life arising from that which gives life, nurturing that which nurtures itself. The Dao gives life to the myriad things—who claims possession or acts? Being fundamentally without action, who boasts or relies on their dominion over the world? Without the skill of carving or controlling, all is entrusted to nature. Thus it is said, "without domination." In this way, the hundred clans do not know, and the myriad things do not express gratitude—this is called profound virtue.

**Chapter Eleven: The Thirty Spokes**

Thirty spokes share one hub; it is where there is nothing that the usefulness of the cart lies. Clay is molded to form a vessel; it is where there is nothing that the usefulness of the vessel lies. Doors and windows are cut to make a room; it is where there is nothing that the usefulness of the room lies.

The *Imperial Commentary* says: Being and non-being are one in essence; utility arises from their interaction—this is called utmost spirit. Being and non-being have different appearances: being is the substance, non-being is the function. This is the movement of yin and yang, the principle of the myriad things. The use of the cart lies in transport, the use of the vessel in containment, the use of the room in emptiness. Wondrous function emerges from utmost non-being; transformation is concealed without encumbrance. Like a mirror without image, it reflects by following things. The utmost person employs the heart, ever understanding this.

Minister Yi says: Being and non-being merge into oneness—this is the full embodiment of the Dao, the utmost spirit. From non-being one reaches being, and through being one perceives non-being; thus, being and non-being take on distinct appearances. Non-being serves as the substance of being, while being serves as the function of non-being—such is the principle of all things. The turning of the cart, the storage of the vessel, the dwelling in the room—all rely on the function of non-being. The cart, the vessel, the room—all take being as their substance. Those who cling to form and regard it as their own possession will fall into mutual decay—such are the benighted. Knowing that non-being serves as the function of being is to grasp the mechanism of transformation. Comprehending the efficacy of wondrous function is to respond without limit, attaining spiritual understanding. Thus, being exists for benefit, while non-being exists for function.

The *Imperial Commentary* says: Being is solid, non-being is empty. Solidity possesses form, image, sound, and color, giving it substance; emptiness enables it to measure, respond, and adapt without exhaustion. Between heaven and earth, the Dao is revealed through vessels—thus, non-being does not discard being, while vessels derive their marvel from the Dao, so being must return to non-being. Wood bends and water moistens, fire burns and metal hardens, earth levels and spreads, giving rise to crops—this is being, on which people rely for benefit. What governs the movement of heaven, the position of earth, the progression of the four seasons, and the flourishing of the myriad things? This is non-being. The world does not perceive its advance, so its function is inexhaustible. The mutual generation of being and non-being—Laozi expounded upon these three to clarify them.

Minister Yi says: That which takes form is being, the benefit of things; that which is empty is non-being, the function of things. Being and non-being support each other to achieve success. The cart, the vessel, and the room are the most visible examples of things, so he uses these three to illustrate the utility of being and non-being.

**Chapter Twelve: The Five Colors**
The five colors blind the eyes; the five tones deafen the ears.

The *Imperial Commentary* says: The eyes contain the faculty of sight, which is inherent in nature. When it penetrates to clarity, it becomes wisdom, sufficient to resolve the doubts of the world. The ears contain the faculty of hearing, which is inherent in nature. When it penetrates to acuity, it becomes counsel, sufficient to comprehend the aspirations of the world. Those who perceive the Dao, above form, see solely with the spirit; beyond sound, they listen solely with qi. Since their seeing and hearing do not depend on the use of eyes and ears, how could mere sounds and colors obstruct them? The people of the world enslave their ears and eyes to the burdens of external things. Thus, their eyes are seduced by the five colors, their ears by the five tones, and their acuity and clarity decline—how can they distinguish blindness from deafness?

Minister Yi says: Those who perceive the formless attain it through spiritual vision. Those who harmonize with the soundless attain it through qi-listening. Vision does not rely on the eyes, so it penetrates to clarity. Hearing does not rely on the ears, so it penetrates to acuity. Acuity and clarity suffice for counsel and wisdom, while the ears and eyes extend to the realm of no-hearing and no-seeing—how could they be burdened by sounds and colors? When nature is swayed by emotion, one loses oneself to things. Expending spirit on sounds and colors, enslaving ears and eyes to seeing and hearing—thus, the abode of the spirit is depleted, and acuity and clarity decline. Hence, it is said: the eyes are blinded, the ears are deafened.

The five flavors dull the palate; galloping and hunting madden the heart.

The *Imperial Commentary* says: The Dao, when spoken, is bland, without flavor. The five flavors are what all people crave, yet rich flavors are truly poisonous—thus, they dull the palate. Human life: the body does not fill a fathom, yet the heart rivals creation. The heart of the sage moves to weave the ten thousand directions, and in stillness mirrors heaven and earth. The people of the world engage in hunting and thereby lose their innate goodness, unable to master themselves—is this not a great sorrow?

Minister Yi says: The great broth and pure wine are bland, without flavor—this is how one embodies the Dao. The Dao, when spoken, is bland, without flavor—this is how one speaks of the Dao. Preserving harmony and wholeness, one retains one’s celestial authenticity—there lies the supreme flavor. Pursuing the world’s cravings and indulging in the five flavors, one loses the true taste—hence, it is said: the palate is dulled. The heart roams in the origin of things, rivaling creation. When it moves outward, it suffices to weave the ten thousand directions; when it retreats in stillness, it suffices to mirror heaven and earth—this is because the heart returns to no-heart. Engaging in hunting, the spirit gallops outward without returning to harmony, the qi and blood labor, and the heart becomes mad—losing one’s nature to this extent is truly lamentable.

Rare goods hinder conduct.

The *Imperial Commentary* says: Profit nourishes people, while goods transform them—thus, mutual exchange brings mutual sufficiency. The sage does not value rare goods or strange objects, deeming common things precious, desiring that people remain content in their lot without encroachment. Valuing rare goods leads to severing the bonds of nature and life, coveting wealth and honor—what conduct can be preserved? Thus, it hinders conduct. Zhonghui’s praise of Tang said: “He did not amass goods or profit.” Confucius said to Zigong: “Ci, you do not accept fate and instead amass wealth.” Such is the way goods hinder conduct.

Minister Yi says: Those who understand where righteousness and fate lie recognize the limits of wealth and honor. Knowing one’s limits and abiding by them, how could one suffer the ills of plunder, competition, or envy? Rare goods are what the world values, not because they are truly useful, but because they are mistakenly deemed fine due to their rarity. Valuing their rarity leads to severing the bonds of nature and life in pursuit of obtaining them—thus, conduct is ruined. Who would consider this? Hence, it is said: they hinder conduct.

Therefore, the sage acts for the belly, not for the eyes—thus, he discards that and selects this.

The *Imperial Commentary* says: In the *Book of Changes*, Kun represents the belly, for it bears and contains with thickness.

The *Li* trigram represents the eyes, for they see outward and bring clarity. To bear with thickness and contain all is to accept without exception. To see outward and bring clarity is to fall short in some things. The sage takes the world as his measure, thus choosing this capacious belly rather than governing every affair and scrutinizing every thing. Hence, he discards those outward-seeing eyes.

The *Zhuangzi* says: "No thief is greater than virtue; when the mind has intentions, the mind has eyes—thus the sage discards them."

Minister Yi says: That which makes no distinctions and accepts all is the belly. That which sees and distinguishes is the eye. Thus, Kun, with its thickness, bears and contains as the belly, while Li, with its outward vision, brings clarity as the eyes. To contain is to accept without exception, aligning with the body of the Dao. To see clearly is to perceive what one cannot reach, establishing the self as an instrument—hence, the sage discards that and selects this.

Sounds, tastes, and desires for profit all lose one’s nature. Moved by external things, the emotions become enslaved. The eyes are the most numerous in this, so they are mentioned first. If the eyes are not blind, the ears not deaf, the mouth not erring, the mind not mad, and conduct not hindered, then the emotions are forgotten, the qi is whole, and the belly is full—thus, in the end, it is said: he acts for the belly, not for the eyes.

*Exegesis on the True Meaning of the* Dao and Virtue *by Emperor Huizong of Song, Fascicle Two, Concluded*
*Exegesis on the True Meaning of the* Dao and Virtue *by Emperor Huizong of Song, Fascicle Three*
*Composed by Gentleman for Court Service, Minister Zhang An*

**Chapter Thirteen: Favor and Disgrace**

Favor and disgrace are like alarm; value great calamity as one’s own body.

*Imperial Commentary* states: Favor comes from below, honor from above. Dwelling in favor as glory is disgrace. Occupying honor as profit invites no greater calamity. Those who esteem wealth cannot refuse emolument; those who prize renown cannot renounce fame. Clinging to power, one cannot share authority—grasping it brings trembling, relinquishing it brings sorrow. Is this not why favor and disgrace strike like alarm? Grief and anguish, ease and joy, assail the body in turn; fear of constraint, elation and dread, overwhelm the heart. Is this not why great calamity is valued as one’s own body?

*Minister’s Exegesis* states: Receiving favor from others and taking it as glory—what greater disgrace is there? Occupying honor in one’s time and making it one’s own profit—what greater calamity exists? Favor and disgrace arise from gain and loss; the coming of gain and loss startles the heart. Startled by gain and loss, one is moved by glory and disgrace—hence, it is said they are *like alarm*. Taking another’s honor as one’s own profit, firmly occupying it, one burdens the heart, becoming entangled in the calamities of deliberate action, unable to free oneself.

What is meant by *favor and disgrace*? Favor is inferior.
*Imperial Commentary* states: The dragon, as a creature, transforms at will, uncontrollable and untamable. If it can be domesticated, its will is bent toward domestication—this is the way of disgrace. The ancients who excelled as scholars regarded the three ranks of honor as insufficient to alter their integrity, and the emolument of ten thousand *zhong* as insufficient to sway their resolve. Abiding in favor yet mindful of peril, dwelling in fortune yet empty as a valley—what disgrace could there be? Coveting high position, desiring royal emolument, knowing advancement but not retreat, knowing gain but not loss—thus others hold one in contempt. Therefore, receiving favor from others is the way of inferiority.
*Minister’s Exegesis* states: Taking another’s favor as glory makes the recipient act in disgrace—this is the way of inferiority. Moreover, knowing advancement but not retreat, knowing gain but not loss—this is how one treads into sorrow and invites bodily harm, becoming contemptible to others. Is this not inferior? Gaining it as if alarmed, losing it as if alarmed—this is called *favor and disgrace are like alarm*.

*Imperial Commentary* states: Rank and insignia upon the body are not the principle of life and destiny. Things that come by chance are but lodgings—what comes as a lodging cannot be refused, so the perfected person does not rejoice in gain. What departs cannot be resisted, so the perfected person does not grieve over loss. Now, if one is displeased by its departure and delighted by its arrival, gain and loss burden the heart—how can one not be alarmed? Liuxia Hui served as chief judge and was thrice dismissed yet did not leave; Zheng Kaofu was thrice ennobled and crept along the wall—these differ from such a state.
*Minister’s Exegesis* states: Favor and disgrace arise from gain and loss; when gain and loss arrive, the heart is burdened, the spirit is startled—this is being alarmed by gain and loss, thus producing favor and disgrace. Without gain, there is no loss; without favor, there is no disgrace. Forgetting gain and loss, what is there to alarm? Without alarm, where are favor and disgrace?

What is meant by *value great calamity as one’s own body*?
*Imperial Commentary* states: Grasping profit and power, monopolizing rewards and punishments, wielding fortune and might, the world fears one as if divine, reveres one as if Heaven—this may be called honor. Yet the sage does not burden himself with honor, thus abiding long in wealth and honor without calamity. It is like a person whose limbs are maimed, senses dulled, form abandoned, wisdom forsaken—merged with the Great Thoroughfare, there is nowhere one does not find contentment. The people of the world exchange their nature for things, thus burdened by things and unable to forget power; the form burdens the heart, thus the heart is lost and unable to forget the form—this calamity is great indeed.
*Minister’s Exegesis* states: Those who burden themselves with honor forget their own worth and cling to another’s honor—who can escape calamity? Only the sage, free of self, treads the highest honor yet never burdens himself with it—this is how one long preserves honor without the calamities of the human realm. Engaging with the self brings the calamity of having a body; the heart is enslaved by honor, the body is encumbered by glory—this calamity is great.

*What is the reason I have great calamity? Because I have a body. If I had no body, what calamity would I have?*
*Imperial Commentary* states: In human life, the hundred bones, nine orifices, five viscera, and six bowels are all complete within—with whom do I share kinship? Recognizing and claiming them is delusion. Those who embody the Dao understand this, so Confucius said: *"If I hear the Dao in the morning, I could die in the evening."* Mencius said: *"Neither premature death nor long life sways me."* Yanzi said: *"Hui has sat in forgetfulness."* Life and death are indeed great, yet they bring no change to oneself—how much less the trifles of gain and loss, fortune and misfortune? Thus the ancients’ perfected persons did not burden their forms with profit, nor their hearts with form. Viewing the myriad things as equal to oneself, one finds nothing to choose—thus my body is not my own. Above, one roams with the Creator; below, one befriends those who regard death and life as one, beginning and end as equal—what calamity could I have? Moreover, favor is what the world honors, yet one deems it disgrace; honor is what people delight in, yet one deems it calamity. Truly, external things cannot be relied upon—the principle is inherently so. If one can possess them as if possessing nothing, then though favored, one is not disgraced; though honored, one is without calamity. Yi Yin did not dwell in success through favor or profit; Yao did not take position as joy—these nearly attained it.
*Minister’s Exegesis* states: Having a self, one has a body; having a body, one has calamity. Without a body, the self is fundamentally nonexistent—if I do not even possess myself, what care could there be? The form is not burdened by things, thus the form returns to formlessness; the heart is not burdened by form, thus the heart returns to mindlessness—this is how, above, one roams with the Creator, and I have no body.

Beneath, one befriends those who equalize life and death in their beginning and end—what care could there be? Seeing favor as disgrace, treading nobility while knowing calamity, then favor and nobility lie with others—what could one rely upon? Thus, one who values their person as the world may be entrusted with the world. One who cherishes their person as the world may be confided with the world.

*Imperial Commentary* states: The world is a great vessel—none but the Dao may set it in motion. The world is a divine vessel—none but the Dao may preserve it. The sage embodies the Dao, thus they govern the world through non-interference, and the world delights in their leadership without weariness. Those next in rank know to value their person and do not debase themselves to be enslaved by things—these may be entrusted. Those who know to cherish their person and do not debase themselves to be harried by things—these may be confided. Thus it is said: The essence of the Dao is for governing the self; its remnants are for governing the state; its dregs are for governing the world. Yet worldly lords risk their persons and abandon their lives to pursue things—is this not lamentable?

*Minister’s Exegesis* states: The sage embodies the Dao, and in the world, their stillness is guarded by the Dao, their movement is set in motion by the Dao—how could they burden themselves with the world’s affairs and instead find it a weight? The world delights in their leadership without weariness—what calamity could there be? If one has not yet attained this, yet knows to value their person through the Dao and does not lose themselves in base degradation, then they know self-respect—thus they may receive the world’s trust. If one knows to cherish their person through the Dao and does not follow things into self-abandonment, then they know self-love—thus they may receive the world’s confidence. The world’s trust and confidence are rooted in the Dao. Though one may not fully embody the Dao, knowing to value and cherish the self through the Dao, it is said: they *may* be entrusted and confided.

**Chapter Fourteen**
*"Look at it, yet it cannot be seen—this is called the Even."*

*Imperial Commentary* states: The eye governs sight, and sight discerns things. The Even is level and undifferentiated—beyond what sight can reach, thus it is called the Even. The Great Simplicity before form is qi.

*Minister’s Exegesis* states: To see is to employ clarity; to perceive is to have an object. With my clarity, I perceive that object—this is merely the reach of the eye’s power. When the myriad dharmas unite as one and self and other merge in mystery, beyond what sight can reach, what is there left to see?

*"Listen to it, yet it cannot be heard—this is called the Rare."*

*Imperial Commentary* states: The ear governs hearing, and hearing investigates things. The Rare is vast yet intermittent—beyond what hearing can grasp, thus it is called the Rare. The great sound is rare indeed.

*Minister’s Exegesis* states: Sound arises from things, responding to their stimuli. As they belong to existence, they may be heard. Yet within soundlessness, harmony alone is heard—thus it is said: the great sound is rare. When only the rare sound is heard, it is vast yet intermittent—thus it is called the Rare.

*"Grasp it, yet it cannot be held—this is called the Subtle."*

*Imperial Commentary* states: Subtle, oh so subtle—reaching formlessness, who could grasp it? The great image is without form.

*Minister’s Exegesis* states: What may be grasped and held clings to form and vessel. Without anything to grasp, one seeks to hold yet cannot—this is the great image. Within such formlessness, something alone is perceived—thus it is called the Subtle.

These three cannot be sought, thus they merge into one.

*Imperial Commentary* states: Before the Great Simplicity divides, who distinguishes high and low? The great sound is rare—who discerns purity and turbidity? The great image is without form—who measures great and small? The eye has no use for its clarity, the ear no application for its acuity, the body no exhaustion of its strength—the full embodiment of the Dao resides herein. It cannot be fathomed, nor can it be probed. Qi, form, and substance are complete yet not yet separated—thus they merge into one. Yet having become one, can it remain without speech?

*Minister’s Exegesis* states: To look, listen, and grasp is not nothingness; to not see, not hear, not hold is not existence. Called the Even, called the Rare, called the Subtle—it is as if existing, as if not. As if existing, as if not, it cannot be interrogated as one—thus it merges without gap. Alike in having from non-being, alike in non-being from having—this is the merging into one. Above, it is not bright; below, it is not dark.

*Imperial Commentary* states: That which is above form is unfathomable as yin and yang, profound and difficult to know—this is called the utmost spirit, thus it is not bright. *Bright* means luminous. That which is below form is one yin, one yang, distinct and numbered—this is called the utmost Dao, thus it is not dark. *Dark* means obscure. The *Book of Changes* says: "To spiritually enlighten it depends on the person."

*Minister’s Exegesis* states: Engaging with form and vessel, there is the distinction of yin and yang—thus above is bright and below is dark, a principle that is inherently so. Where the utmost spirit works its wonders, above one does not know its brightness. Where the utmost Dao operates, below one does not know its darkness. Neither bright nor dark, it does not depart from the one.

Endless, oh, it cannot be named—it returns to no-thing.

*Imperial Commentary* states: The body of the Dao is like the constancy of day and night, yet its depths of darkness and light cannot be fathomed—how could it be described in terms of appearance, sound, or color? Thus it returns to no-thing.

*Minister’s Exegesis* states: The one is the warp of the Dao. Uniting being and non-being, above and below, into a profound unity—this is the Dao’s unnameability. A cord is made by joining strands; *endless* speaks of its ceaselessness. One and again one—this is the meaning of the ever more mysterious. Returning to the state before things began, thus it is said: it returns to no-thing.

This is called the form of the formless, the image of no-thing—this is called the indistinct and vague.

*Imperial Commentary* states: The form of the formless, the image of no-thing—vague, oh, within it there is something; indistinct, oh, within it there is an image. Like the great void containing the myriad images, yet its beginnings and ends cannot be perceived.

As if a numinous intelligence acts of itself, yet none can fathom its workings—call it "having" yet not having, call it "not having" yet not not having. Like the sun and moon far removed from man: seek them with mirror and burning-glass, and water and fire come of themselves. Where, then, do water and fire truly reside? The form of the formless, the image of no-thing—it is just like this.

*Minister’s Exegesis* states: The form of the formless is present yet unseen; the image of no-thing is present yet unattainable. Having returned to no-thing, it is as if things never began. The form of the formless, the image of no-thing—these again circle back to no-thing. To call it "having" yet not having, to call it "not having" yet not not having—this is what is meant by "indistinct and vague." Meet it and you do not see its front; follow it and you do not see its rear.

*Imperial Commentary* states: It has no beginning, no end; thus, whether you meet it or follow it, there is nothing to be seen.

*Minister’s Exegesis* states: When bound to the mechanism of emergence and withdrawal, of beginning and end, there is distinction between front and rear. But the Dao returns to formlessness—who then can speak of front and rear? The Dao abides in no-thing—how can one grasp it as having front or rear? Its coming cannot be met, its going cannot be followed. Who knows its beginning? Who knows its end? Hold to the Dao of old to steer the being of today. To know the ancient beginning—this is called the discipline of the Dao.

*Imperial Commentary* states: The alternation of yin and yang is called the Dao. Those who follow heaven but reject earth may be blinded by the Dao’s movement and rely on their strong yang. Those who follow yin but reject yang may drown in the Dao’s stillness and halt at withered desiccation. Those who act for themselves abandon propriety; those who act for others abandon righteousness—how can this be the Dao of old? The Dao of old cannot be pressed for answers, yet it is not having; it is called "vague power" yet not not having. Holding to it to govern the world, one adapts and exhausts its benefits, rouses and exhausts its spirit, and there is nothing it cannot accomplish. The great constancy of the Dao is unchanging in this—is this not what is meant by "existing since ancient times"? Thus it is said: To know the ancient beginning—this is called the discipline of the Dao.

*Minister’s Exegesis* states: The Dao of old is simply the One. Holding to the One in application, one can steer all that is under heaven. "Today" refers to the time when affairs arise; "ancient" refers to the time before departing from the One. To know the ancient beginning is to grasp the great constancy of the Dao—thus it is called the discipline of the Dao.

**Chapter Fifteen: The Skill of the Ancient Scholar**

The scholars of old who were skilled in their art were subtle, wondrous, profound, and penetrating—so deep as to be unfathomable.

*Imperial Commentary* states: The scholars of old differed from those of today; those skilled in their art differed from the unskilled. Thus, subtlety unites them with the Dao, wonder makes them one with the spirit, profundity matches them to heaven, and penetration foreshadows sageliness. Their depth of application is such that it cannot be plumbed. The *Book of Documents* says: "The mind of the Dao is subtle"—thus subtlety is the Dao. The *Book of Changes* says: "The spirit is that which wondrously brings forth the myriad things"—thus wonder is the spirit. The *Book of Changes* says: "Heaven is profound, and earth is yellow"—thus profundity is the color of heaven. The *Commentary* says: "That which penetrates all affairs is called sageliness"—thus penetration is the work of the sage. The depth of water can be measured; the depth of a cave can be explored. But the scholars of old who were skilled in their art were subtle, wondrous, profound, and penetrating—names and realities did not enter them, yet their mechanism arose from their heels. Their depth was such that it could not be plumbed. Liezi dwelled in the gardens of Zheng for forty years, and no one recognized him. Laozi said to Confucius: "A good merchant stores his goods as if his treasury were empty; a gentleman of great virtue appears dull-witted"—was he not speaking of this?

*Minister’s Exegesis* states: "Ancient" refers to the beginning of things, when the Dao had not yet departed from the One. Those who succeeded the Dao were good—thus goodness is the emanation of the Dao. Today, however, is the time when affairs press upon us, and scholars take up their tasks accordingly. This is why the scholars of old differed from those of today. Only by emerging from the ancient beginning, succeeding the Dao with goodness, can one return to the Dao. Taking the Dao upon oneself, one can bear the Dao. Thus they could be subtle, wondrous, profound, and penetrating—so deep as to be unfathomable. Their subtlety uniting them with the Dao speaks of the Dao’s depth; their wonder making them one with the spirit speaks of the spirit’s depth. Matching heaven’s profundity and acting in accordance with nature speaks of the depth of principle. Foreshadowing sageliness in penetration and attaining the profound in action speaks of the depth of affairs. Those with shallow mechanisms can be measured in an instant; but for those whose depth is great, who can know them? Precisely because they cannot be known, we force descriptions upon them.

*Imperial Commentary* states: The height of heaven cannot be measured in an instant; the depth of earth cannot be plumbed in an instant. That it is said to be round in covering and square in bearing is merely an approximation of its appearance. Forcing descriptions upon it—how can one truly grasp its utmost?

*Minister’s Exegesis* states: So deep as to be unknowable, it admits no sound—who can compare or discuss it? Forcing descriptions upon it, one can only borrow analogies, as in "like a river reaching a marsh."

Cautious, as if crossing a river in winter; hesitant, as if fearing one’s four neighbors.

*Imperial Commentary* states: "Cautious" means anticipating trouble before it arises; "hesitant" means harboring doubt about one’s own affairs. Those of old who embodied the Dao made inner cultivation their task, not delighting in engaging with things. Fearful and vigilant, they responded only when unavoidable—like crossing a river in winter, holding fast without losing themselves. Like fearing one’s four neighbors, as the *Book of Changes* says: "With this, one fasts and abstains."

*Minister’s Exegesis* states: When things probe them, they respond only under pressure—not delighting in engaging with things. Anticipating trouble before affairs arise is called "cautious"; fearing after affairs arise is called "hesitant." Crossing in winter speaks of acting only when unavoidable, advancing with hesitation—not what one desires.

Fearing one’s four neighbors speaks of how what is to be feared presses closely upon me, never parting—so one remains vigilant and dares not indulge. Solemn in demeanor.

*Minister’s Exegesis* states: "Solemn" describes a bearing of dignified gravity; "demeanor" refers to its ever-shifting aspect. By its gravity, the *Record* likens it to one deep in thought; by its variability, *Zhuangzi* calls it "composed bearing"—solemn as a guest’s form, forgetting yet appearing serene.

Dispersing like ice about to melt,
*Minister’s Exegesis* states: Clinging to form obstructs being; roaming the origin of things returns one to nonbeing. To leave things behind and depart from the self—this is what it means to disperse. Like ice about to melt, this is how one attains mysterious sameness. Water congeals into ice, then melts back into water, never departing from its essence, perfectly fused and interpenetrating.

Thick as if uncarved wood,
*Minister’s Exegesis* states: Returning to the profound thickness of one’s nature and destiny—this is called "thick." Like wood in its wholeness, undivided into implements—this is called "uncarved."

Vast as if a valley,
*Minister’s Exegesis* states: When the heart returns to non-heart, it becomes as boundless as the Great Void—vast to the utmost, like an empty valley, capable of receiving and responding.

Turbid as if muddy.
*Minister’s Exegesis* states: Self and other merge indistinguishably, as one without separation—like a muddy stream, concealing filth and embracing impurity.

Who can still the turbid and gradually clarify it?
Who can rouse the tranquil and gradually bring it to life?
*Minister’s Exegesis* states: Stillness is not the act of moving toward stillness, yet movement is never without stillness. Movement is not the act of departing from stillness, yet stillness is never without movement. One who attains both movement and stillness finds the Dao in action. Thus, the ancients who served the Dao moved turbidly yet never departed from stillness, so they gradually clarified themselves, and nothing could muddy them. Their stillness was tranquil yet never ceased moving, so they gradually came to life, and nothing could make them stagnant. To harbor the intent of cessation is to be bound by things—how then could one respond without harm?

Those who preserve this Dao do not desire fullness.
*Minister’s Exegesis* states: Those who serve the Dao forget the heart in the Dao. If one clings to possession and prides oneself on it, though something remains, it is already meager—thus they do not desire fullness. Fullness means taking pride in what one has and becoming self-satisfied. Satisfaction leads to overturning—this is the inevitable principle of reason. How can one preserve it?

Only by not being full can one remain worn yet not newly made.
*Minister’s Exegesis* states: Fullness must lead to overturning, for self-satisfaction inevitably brings about renewal and completion. This is the principle of decay and ruin, the cycle of succession—who can preserve fullness? The new gives way to the old, completion gives way to loss, alternating and interdependent like the turning of a wheel.

Those capable of renewal and completion are those who turn toward affairs and act. Those capable of decay return to the Dao and act not. When things are newly completed, they are fit for use and turn toward affairs. When things reach decay, they are cast aside by time and return to the Dao. To decay without renewing or completing—what fullness can there be? This is how the Dao is preserved.

**Chapter Sixteen: Reaching the Utmost of Emptiness**

Reach the utmost of emptiness; guard the depth of stillness.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: Nothing is more precious than emptiness; nothing is more excellent than stillness. Emptiness and stillness are the root of the myriad things. Emptiness suffices to receive all actualities; stillness suffices to respond to all movements. *Utmost* is where the many converge and reach their limit. *Depth* is where effort is exerted and reaches its limit. To reach the utmost of emptiness and press toward its limit, to guard stillness and attain its depth—then, though the ten thousand forms be tangled, my heart remains ever clear; though the ten thousand changes be manifold, my heart remains ever tranquil. This is called the joy of Heaven. Those who do not embody the Dao cannot partake of it.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Emptiness is the convergence of the Dao. Stillness is the return of the Dao. Emptiness is not burdened by things, and so it becomes the dwelling of all actualities. Stillness does not mingle with things, and so it becomes the source of all movements. Only through emptiness and stillness can one serve as the mirror of Heaven and Earth, the illumination of the myriad things—so that the ten thousand forms and ten thousand changes cannot disturb it. *Utmost* is the central point of the Dao, where the many converge. *Depth* is the movement and action of the Dao, where effort reaches its limit. To govern emptiness and reach its utmost limit, to guard stillness and attain its deepest depth—if one does not reach this, actualities may obstruct the Dao; if one does not attain this, movement may violate one’s nature.

The myriad things arise together; I thereby observe their return.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: The changes of the myriad things lie at the end of the Dao. Those who embody the Dao dwell above the myriad things. Things have their origins in sprouting and their ends in returning. As they arise together and hasten toward the path of movement and emergence, I observe that the moving must become still, and the emergent must return. Thereby, I perceive the heart of Heaven and Earth—engaging with things without being transformed along with them. This is called observing their return.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: The myriad things are endowed with form between Heaven and Earth. Yin and yang marshal the qi to enter and exit, laboring under the mechanism of Heaven and toiling under the transformations of spirit. They hasten toward the path of living existence, arising together without cease. Only by observing stillness in movement and observing return in emergence can those who embody the Dao do so. Thus, to observe return amid the arising of the myriad things is to perceive the heart of Heaven and Earth—how much more so the myriad things!

The things, luxuriant and manifold, each return to their root.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: *Luxuriant and manifold* is the image of movement and emergence. The myriad things emerge from *Zhen*, meet in *Li*, then luxuriate and arise together, their blossoms and splendor issuing outward. Delighting in *Dui* and laboring in *Kan*, they shed their blossoms and turn toward substance, returning to the abode of their nature. *Destiny* is the root of nature, and nature is its root. *Essence* is the mother of spirit, and spirit is its child. When essence is whole, spirit flourishes; when nature is fully realized, one reaches destiny.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: *Luxuriant and manifold* is how things come to be. *Root* is where things return. Blossoms and splendor issue from the root and grow; returning to the abode of nature, they revert to their root. When the root is great, the branches flourish—thus the root is the destiny of things. The luxuriant and manifold return to the root, thereby returning to destiny. Returning to the root is called stillness; stillness is called returning to destiny.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: Flowing and moving, they generate things. When things are generated, their principles take form—this is called *shape*. The shape embodies spirit, each having its own pattern—this is called *nature*. That which is not yet formed has its allotment, existing thus without gap—this is called *destiny*. Destiny spans past and present, ever abiding; nature shifts through ten thousand forms yet remains unchanged. Does the person who preserves their form and life depart from wisdom? Thus, returning to quiescent stability, one knows that destiny lies within oneself—just as spring and summer inevitably give way to autumn and winter. Embracing nature and holding fast to spirit, one examines oneself within—this is called returning to destiny.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: *Luxuriant and manifold* is the blossom of movement, the division of form. *Returning to the root* is the utmost of stillness, the return of destiny. The myriad things receive destiny from the formless and take shape in the formed. Thus, there is destiny, then life; there is life, then nature; there is nature, then feeling. When feeling responds to movement, it flows into things. Flowing into things, it pursues the branches and harms the root, chases things and loses its nature—thus destiny is extinguished. Only when form is forgotten and one does not know whence it arises, when the heart is forgotten and one does not know whence it stirs—observing return amid the arising of things—is principle fully exhausted. When the luxuriant and manifold each return to their root, their nature is fully realized. When principle is exhausted and nature is fully realized, one returns to stillness and oneness, reverts to the utmost of nothingness—thus it is called returning to destiny.

Returning to destiny is called constancy.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: *Constancy* is the term opposed to change. Returning to destiny, the ten thousand changes cannot alter it. Without gap or cessation, it unites with the Dao, accords with Heaven and Earth, and merges with the Qi-Mother.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: *Constancy* is not seen as close, and so it has no beginning or end. It does not cling to form nor fall into calculation. It unites with the Dao and is not altered by change—this is the true constancy of the Dao. Without returning to destiny, one cannot attain it.

Knowing constancy is called clarity.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: Knowing the constancy of the Dao, one is not altered by things. Thus, the six penetrations follow one another, sufficing to mirror Heaven and Earth, sufficing to illuminate the myriad things.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: To know without relying on the heart, yet attain the constancy of the Dao—this is true knowledge. To see without relying on the eyes, yet perceive the formless—this is the self-awareness of nature. Thus, it is called clarity.

True knowledge is not altered by things, and clarity thus suffuses and illumines Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things. Not knowing constancy, reckless action brings misfortune.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: The sage knows the constancy of the Dao, and so his actions accord with principle, ever meeting with good fortune. Those who do not know constancy follow things in their flux, stumbling into calamity at every turn. Thus, they falsely perceive beauty and ugliness, opposing the Dao; they recklessly generate right and wrong, diverging from the Dao. Moreover, they cannot secure their destiny, hence misfortune.

The *Book of Changes* says: *Return* is not reckless. To be deluded and not know return—this is recklessness indeed.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Knowing the return of destiny, one knows constancy. Not knowing the return of destiny, one gallops outward, entangling with things, forgetting the root of one’s nature, destiny, virtue, and power, drowning in the branches of beauty and ugliness, right and wrong. Grasping at that which is real, one drifts into falsity, losing essence and forfeiting spirit, never knowing to return. Deluded and not knowing return, who can escape misfortune?

Knowing constancy, one is all-embracing.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: Knowing constancy, one does not harbor right or wrong, beauty or ugliness, and thus nothing is not embraced.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Knowing constancy, one returns to utmost emptiness, becoming one body with the Dao—this is the perfection of embrace.

Embrace leads to impartiality.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: Without a mind that embraces, one does not exclusively cherish one’s kin nor exclusively love one’s children—what partiality could there be?

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Embracing nothing, one darkly accords with the Dao. Self and other are not established—how could there be partiality?

Impartiality leads to kingship.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: When the great Dao prevails, the world is impartial. Thus, the world delights in exalting one without weariness.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Being impartial with the Dao, one becomes the destination of the world, hence the delight in exalting one without weariness.

Kingship leads to Heaven, Heaven leads to the Dao.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: One who unites Heaven, Earth, and humanity and stands at the center of Heaven and Earth is a king. That which is one and great, above and covering all without exception, is Heaven. That by which Heaven, Earth, and humanity are all guided is the Dao. Perfecting humanity, one becomes one with Heaven; embodying Heaven, one becomes one with the Dao.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: The world’s destination, yet not knowing its destination, is entrusted to spontaneity. Great and covering all without exception, one becomes one with Heaven. Forgetting the reason for being so, yet being so, one returns to nothingness—this is perfecting humanity and embodying Heaven to become one with the Dao.

The Dao endures; to the end of one’s days, one is not imperiled.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: The Dao is everlasting, without flaw through the ages. All things that attain it flourish. It is the gateway of a hundred sages, inexhaustible; it veils Heaven and Earth, unceasing. Thus, to the end of one’s days, one is not imperiled. Imperilment borders on misfortune; subtlety borders on good fortune. Without imperilment, there is no reckless action’s misfortune—none but those who know constancy partake in this.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Only the Dao endures; endurance is the constancy of the Dao. The Dao abides in nothingness, hence it endures. The self is bound to form, hence it perishes. Yet though it perishes, it is not imperiled—though dead, it has never truly died. How much less could there be the misfortune of reckless action?

**Chapter Seventeen: The Highest**

Of the highest, those below merely know of its existence.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: Governing the world with freedom, one harmonizes with the age and finds joy in it. Without the joy of delight, yet praise and intimacy do not reach one. Without the suffering of distress, yet fear and insult do not come. Nothing is done, yet all is spontaneous. Thus, those below merely know of its existence.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: In an age of perfect governance, the ruler practices non-action. Knowing only its outward signs, the world uniformly lives yet does not know why it lives, uniformly attains yet does not know why it attains. They merely know of their superior, yet never perceive the superior’s influence upon them.

Next, they cherish and praise it.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: Bestowing blessings upon the people, laws are transmitted through the ages. The world loves one as parents, hence they cherish it. A noble name arises like the sun and moon, hence they praise it. This is the governance of emperors and kings. The traces of cherishing and praise are manifest, and the great harmony of the Dao is diminished.

*Zhuangzi* says: Shun had a flaw, yet the people delighted in him. They sang at the Spirit Tower, speaking of how the people first attached themselves to King Wen.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: The governance of the seven virtues is enacted through action. There is kindness to be cherished, allowing attachment; there is virtue to be embraced, allowing praise. Though the traces of governance surpass a single era, the wholeness of the Dao and virtue has already dispersed into the realm of the artificial.

Next, they fear and insult it.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: Guiding with policies, ordering with punishments, hence they fear it. Rewarding the entire world does not encourage the good; punishing the entire world does not deter the wicked. When feudal lords inquire about the weight of the tripods, as the viscount of Chu did, or when vassals steal treasures and great bows, as Yang Hu did—these are the customs of a declining age, hence they insult it.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Governance not grasping the essentials attends to outward traces. Even when practicing benevolence and righteousness, the transformation of benevolence and righteousness cannot be fully realized. Thus, they fear its penal majesty and govern with legal institutions. Laws emerge, and deceit arises; decrees descend, and fraud is born. Feudal lords go so far as to inquire about the tripods; vassals go so far as to steal treasures. Hence, it is said they fear and insult it.

Thus, when trust is insufficient, there is distrust.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: Attachment is not with glue or lacquer; restraint is not with cords or ropes—this is utmost trust. The Shang people made oaths, and the people began to rebel. The Zhou people held assemblies, and the people began to doubt. Trust was insufficient, hence this.

Of the highest, those below merely know of its existence—then it is fitting, and they do not perceive it as trust. When they fear and insult it, they know the cunning of deceit, the subtlety of trickery, the ingenuity of opportunism, and thus there is distrust.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Governance not rooted in the Dao, with the superior not trusting the Dao, resorts to legal institutions to establish authority, expecting people to fear and not insult. Yet trust is insufficient.

When the superior’s trust is insufficient, those below respond with distrust, and day by day grow ever more deceitful and shallow—of great disorders, what could govern them? Thus, how solemnly they esteem speech!

The *Imperial Commentary* states: Viewing speech through the Dao, if the ruler of all under Heaven is upright, how could words be treated lightly? They are guarded and revered, never dared to be taken lightly. Thus, when words are spoken, they become the Way for all under Heaven to follow; when words arise from the self, all under Heaven is transformed.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: To esteem speech is not to treat it lightly. If speech itself is not treated lightly, how could actions be? When actions are not taken lightly, there is trust in the Dao. Because distrust leads to disorder, they esteem speech so solemnly.

When merits are accomplished and affairs are settled, the common people all say, “We did it naturally.”

The *Imperial Commentary* states: The achievements of emperors and kings are but the surplus affairs of sages. When the people find their food sweet, their clothing beautiful, their customs peaceful, and their occupations joyful, these surplus affairs suffice to complete the work of emperors and kings. Yet they say, “We did it naturally,” as if to ask, “What has imperial power to do with us?” This is called the governance of the highest.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: To engage in affairs and exhaust one’s spirit in shallow pursuits—though called governance, it daily descends into disorder, for it is not rooted in the Dao. To return to nature and life, allowing growth and completion to unfold naturally—though called non-action, what does imperial power have to do with it? To esteem speech is to govern without words and transform through the Dao. Thus, the achievements of emperors and kings, the affairs of the age, once accomplished and settled, leave the people unable to describe them. They all say, “We did it naturally,” unaware of the superior’s governance, knowing only that the superior exists.

**Chapter Eighteen: The Abandonment of the Great Dao**

When the great Dao is abandoned, there are benevolence and righteousness.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: Lose the Dao, and virtue follows; lose virtue, and benevolence follows; lose benevolence, and righteousness follows. Benevolence establishes others; righteousness establishes the self—yet how far they have strayed from the Dao! Han Yu, failing to grasp the sages’ intent regarding the Dao and virtue, claimed that *ren* and *yi* were fixed terms while the Dao and virtue were empty positions. His criticism of Laozi for belittling benevolence and righteousness reveals his own limited vision—what *Zhuangzi* called “the benighted people.”

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: The great Dao is nameless, where self and other dissolve, returning to perfect nothingness, leaving no room for sound. With benevolence and righteousness, one has already strayed far from the Dao. Virtue is divided: benevolence loves others but cannot yet forget them; righteousness establishes the self but cannot yet forget the self. When benevolence and righteousness stand to the right, the burdens of self and other remain. Turning toward affairs and actions, the Dao is abandoned.

When wisdom and intelligence emerge, there is great falsity.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: When the people’s wisdom and strength are exhausted, falsity follows.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: With the wisdom of knowledge and the intelligence of the heart, one seeks to control the changes of the world, acting and intervening—then the people, competing with cleverness, give rise to falsity and chaos.

When the six relations are not in harmony, there are filial piety and compassion. When the state is benighted and disordered, there are loyal ministers.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: Names arise from insufficiency.

*Master Zhuang* says: The filial son does not flatter his parents; the loyal minister does not fawn upon his lord—this is the height of ministerial and filial devotion.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: When the six relations are in harmony, one does not recognize what makes them filial or compassionate. When the state is well-governed, one does not recognize the loyalty of its ministers. Only in harmony and order do filial piety and loyalty become commonplace. Without filial compassion, the name of filial compassion arises. Only when there is disloyalty does one praise loyalty.

**Chapter Nineteen: "Abandoning Sagacity and Discarding Knowledge"**

Abandon sagacity and discard knowledge, and the people will benefit a hundredfold.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: The Dao endows them with nature, one and unadulterated. Departing from the Dao to establish virtue is called sagacity and knowledge. Once sagacity and knowledge are established, the world begins to produce lofty, intricate, and ostentatious conduct—startling the foolish and enlightening the benighted, praising Yao and condemning Jie. Thus, the benefits of sagacity and knowledge to the world are few, while their harms are many. Abandoning them to unite with the Dao, each rests in the integrity of their nature and destiny—such benefit is vast.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: The names of sagacity and knowledge depart from the Dao and divide from virtue. The people’s nature loses its true purity and strays from primordial simplicity, so sagacity and knowledge emerge. Sagacity takes transformation as its essence; knowledge takes discernment as its function. When superiors govern inferiors with sagacity and knowledge, the people contend to follow the traces of sagacity and knowledge, unaware of what they truly value. Chasing the branches and forgetting the root, they compound harm upon the people. When sagacity and knowledge have no use and retreat into abandonment, the people will naturally attain great compliance, forget what they once admired, and find sufficiency in their inherent nature—thus their benefit is vast.

Abandon benevolence and discard righteousness, and the people will return to filial compassion.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: Filial compassion is innate nature. Striving for benevolence and straining for righteousness replace one’s nature with them. Abandoning benevolence and righteousness, the people will revert to their nature and return to their origin—not only loving their own kin or cherishing their own children—how could filial compassion be lacking?

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Filial compassion is the substance of benevolence and righteousness. Once the names of benevolence and righteousness are established, the people’s nature is lost. Their nature is inherently sufficient in filial compassion, yet burdened by the names of benevolence and righteousness—earnest and scrupulous, names and reality entangle, disrupting nature. Only by abandoning the traces of benevolence and righteousness will the people wholly preserve their Heaven-given nature, sincerely engaging with one another and forgetting artificial distinctions—thus restoring filial compassion.

Abandon skill and discard profit, and thieves and bandits will cease to exist.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: Those who craft cunning schemes have no use for shame. Those with covetous hearts disregard righteousness—both are thieves and burglars.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: When skill is prized, one exhausts their wit and stops at nothing—what shame remains? When profit is esteemed, one values goods and insists on acquisition—what righteousness is regarded? Only when skill is abandoned will the people labor with their hands and find ease in sufficiency. Only when profit is discarded will the people practice restraint and have abundance—how then could thieves and bandits exist?

These three are deemed insufficient as cultural refinements, so they are made to belong to something greater.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: The former kings governed the world by human principles; by the Zhou, culture had become excessive. When it reached its decline, culture overcame substance. Culture was in surplus while substance was lacking. The world universally lost its plain and unadorned truth, daily sinking into the habits of private desire. At the end of the Zhou, Master Lao sought to remedy this corruption and return to the root. Thus, he cast aside ritual and propriety, abolished learning and scholarship, and even sagacity and knowledge were rejected. Did he truly believe benevolence, righteousness, sagacity, and knowledge were insufficient to govern the world? The Way of the former kings is like a cycle—when culture is excessive, nothing restores substance like simplicity. Thus, they are made to belong to something greater: "manifest plainness, embrace simplicity, reduce selfishness, and diminish desire."

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Sagacity, knowledge, benevolence, righteousness, skill, and profit—these three pairs preserve culture while destroying substance. Thus, the people lose their benefit, filial compassion is not restored, and thieves and bandits multiply. This arises from trust being insufficient while cultural refinements are complete—hence the need for abandonment. Abandoning these refinements prevents the world from losing its truth and drifting into falsity. Thus, Master Lao loosened the excesses of culture to return to the root, making them belong to something greater. "Manifest plainness, embrace simplicity, reduce selfishness, and diminish desire"—such is the belonging.

**"Manifest plainness"**

The *Imperial Commentary* states: The *Analects* says, "Painting comes after plainness." Plainness has not yet received color—manifesting plainness is pure and unadulterated.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Nature is originally pure and plain. When moved, it is stained by things and becomes mixed and inconsistent. When nature is tranquil, it is pure and unified, free from things—naturally manifesting thus, hence "manifest plainness."

**"Embrace simplicity"**

The *Imperial Commentary* states: The *Classic* says, "When simplicity scatters, it becomes vessels." Simplicity has never been severed—embracing simplicity is tranquil, unified, and unchanging. *Master Zhuang* says: "Being the same in having no desires is called plain simplicity." In plain simplicity, the people’s nature is attained.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Simplicity is the undivided, uncarved whole of wood. The people’s nature is complete in spontaneity, not yet turned toward things—unified and unchanging, preserved without loss—hence "embrace simplicity."

**"Reduce selfishness and diminish desire"**

The *Imperial Commentary* states: Self-promotion is selfishness, and nothing nurtures the heart better than diminishing desire. Reducing selfishness and diminishing desire settles the distinction between inner and outer, clarifies the return of truth and falsity, and completes virtue while restoring nature—thus the names of sagacity and knowledge vanish.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: When selfish desire prevails, one loses oneself in things. Reducing selfishness and diminishing desire turns inward, lightens external things, recognizes what is truly valuable, and preserves Heavenly virtue. With virtue complete and nature restored to its origin—what need is there for sagacity and knowledge?

*Explication of the True Classic of the Dao and Its Virtue by Emperor Huizong of Song, Volume Three (concluded)*
*Explication of the True Classic of the Dao and Its Virtue by Emperor Huizong of Song, Volume Four*
*Composed by Court Gentleman for Promotion, Minister Zhang An*

**Abandoning Learning Brings No Sorrow**

The *Imperial Commentary* states: Learning is for exhaustively investigating principle. Yet when one is devoted to learning to exhaust principle, pondering good and bad, examining order and chaos—can one be without sorrow? Learning is for attaining the Dao. Seeing the Dao, one abandons learning, diminishing again and again until reaching non-action that leaves nothing undone. Then, following the natural course of one’s nature and destiny, there is no circumstance that does not bring joy—thus, no sorrow.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Those who are complete in Heaven have no need for externals, hence they abandon learning. Right and wrong are boundless; deluded knowledge and false views discriminate endlessly—thus, much sorrow. When learning is abandoned, there is no need for thought and deliberation. Spirit is preserved and restored, no longer enslaved to cleverness and discernment. Following the truth of one’s nature and destiny, where is there not spontaneous joy?

*"Ah!" and "alas!"—how far apart are they? Good and evil—how distant are they?*

The *Imperial Commentary* states: "Ah!" and "alas!" share the same sound; good and evil arise from one nature. Petty wisdom, self-serving, divides them into two. The enlightened, with a broad view, know their root is not different. The sage’s governance of the world—in ancestral temples and court halls, when speaking with ministers—does not distinguish them thus. Suppressing evil and promoting good, fearing failure, is because what people fear cannot but be feared.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: "Ah!" and "alas!"—good and evil—are distinctions made by deluded emotions, differing by so little—what is there to compare? "Ah!" and "alas!" are both sounds that issue forth; good and evil both arise from nature. Sound and nature are one. Taking "ah!" as reverence, "alas!" as rudeness, good as right, evil as wrong—all belong to delusion. With penetrating insight, all is fundamentally the same—what difference remains?

*What people fear cannot but be feared.*

The *Imperial Commentary* states: The Dao stirs the myriad things yet does not share the sage’s sorrows. Fortune and misfortune fluctuate with the people—this is the realm of affairs. Those who embody the Dao are without sorrow; those who engage in affairs have fears. If one does not take heed of what people fear, how can one avoid calamity? Thus, the noble person cultivates vigilance and introspection. The *Book of Songs* says: *"In awe of Heaven’s majesty."*

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Embodying non-action, the Dao unites self and others—what sorrow or fear could there be? Engaging in the governance of the world, fortune and misfortune are shared with the people.
