Thus, what people fear cannot but be feared—one does not set oneself apart but shares the people’s joys and sorrows. To not fear what should be feared—how can one avoid calamity?

*Vast and boundless, it has no end.*

The *Imperial Commentary* states: The ten thousand changes of worldly affairs are tangled and ungovernable, difficult to conclude, without limit—so-called good and evil are not yet fixed. Only the enlightened know that all is one.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: The realm of affairs and actions, with emotions shifting endlessly, succeed and supplant one another without cease. The threads of turmoil arise without bound—vast and immense, without limit. Only those who are obscurely one forget self and others, deluded emotions instantly vanish, and they know that all is one.

*The multitude bustle and rejoice, as if feasting on a grand sacrifice, as if ascending a terrace in spring.*

The *Imperial Commentary* states: All things flourish with yang and congeal with yin. The bustling and rejoicing are the outward signs of flourishing. The multitude, having lost the stillness of their nature, are devoted to external pursuits—like delighting in rich flavors to nourish the body, like gazing upon lofty splendor to please the mind. Such indulgent ones drift ever farther from the Dao.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Worldly affairs and things change endlessly, their ten thousand threads arising without end, their origins without limit—vast and boundless. Those who study the Dao deeply reject them; only those who embody the Dao are unburdened. The sage governs the world, and what people fear cannot but be feared. Yet following this with *"vast and boundless, it has no end"* shows that even the sage regards affairs with awe. The multitude, pursuing things and losing their nature, forsaking stillness for activity, abandon their most precious inherent joy, chasing the trivial ends of affairs. They bustle and rejoice, unaware of their own burdens. Thus, in their external pursuits, they have not yet been ensnared, believing themselves to have found flavor and delighting in it—hence, *"as if feasting on a grand sacrifice."* Believing themselves to have found pleasure and amusing their hearts—hence, *"as if ascending a terrace in spring."*

*Alone I am desolate, without sign, like an infant who has not yet learned to smile.*

The *Imperial Commentary* states: The *Classic* says: *"Return to the state of the infant."* Zhuangzi says: *"Before reaching the stage of the smiling infant, who is there?"* The infant’s desires and concerns have not yet arisen; near and far are viewed as one. Desolate and still, harmony and compliance accumulate within, and brilliance does not manifest externally—thus, like an infant who has not yet learned to smile.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Alone, forgetting the self, wandering in the origin of nature, desolate and without things—signs have not yet appeared. Thus, like an infant who has not yet learned to smile, the mind is focused on the One, desires and concerns unarisen. Nature preserves its truth, not distinguishing near and far.

*Adrift, as if having nowhere to return.*

The *Imperial Commentary* states: The *Book of Changes* says: *"Riding the six dragons to govern Heaven."* Riding the current means following the times and responding to principle without leaning toward one side—thus, as if having nowhere to return.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Riding principle to adapt to the changes of affairs and things, without attachment or bias.

*The multitude all have more than enough.*

The *Imperial Commentary* states: Someone asked, *"Who are the multitude?"* The reply: *"Those who, born into wealth and honor, are greedy and covetous. The heart of extravagance prevails, while the resolve for frugality is faint—thus, all have more than enough."* In the Dao, this is called *"excess food and redundant actions."*

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: The multitude, grasping at things and falsity, obtain their flavors and joys—thus, they have more than enough.

*Alone I seem lacking.*

The *Imperial Commentary* states: One’s merit covers the world yet seems not one’s own.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: The sage abandons things and distances people, standing alone.

*How foolish is my heart—so pure and simple!*

The *Imperial Commentary* states: Confucius said: *"When I speak with Yan Hui, he never disagrees all day—like a fool."*

Pure indeed! The celestial mechanism lies unspread, yet silently accords with the Dao—this is called great wisdom.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Great wisdom appears foolish, for it employs no cunning; nothing is done through knowing. Thus, it is utterly pure, with no scheming heart arising. In perfect unity, it silently accords with the Dao.

The vulgar are bright and clear; I alone seem dim.
The vulgar are sharp and discerning; I alone am dull and muddled.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: Those who conform to common custom deem brightness as clarity, yet their clarity is small. They regard sharpness as wisdom, yet their wisdom is forced. Abandoning things and distancing oneself from others, one scorns the habits of the age—then, though seeming lost, one abides; though appearing dull, one is keen. How could the vulgar ever glimpse this?

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Brightness and sharpness—thus does the vulgar world employ petty wisdom for self-interest. Dimness and dullness, however, use no wisdom; self and others merge as one. Seeming lost, yet present; appearing dull, yet keen.

Vast as the sea, serene and still.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: Profound stillness and settled nature—this is the full embodiment of the Dao.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: The nature-sea lies deep and unfathomable, boundless and immeasurable—this is the essence of the Dao.

Boundless, as if without end.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: Ever-changing yet never abiding—this is the active function of the Dao.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Where there is form, there is cessation; where there is cessation, there is attachment, and no freedom. Where there is no form, there is no cessation; where there is no cessation, there is no clinging, and nothing is beyond reach—this is the function of the Dao.

All others have their uses; I alone am stubborn and coarse.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: Cinnamon is edible, so it is cut down. Lacquer is useful, so it is slashed. People know the use of what is useful, but none know the use of what is useless. All others have their uses—this is the use of the useful. I alone am stubborn and coarse—this is the use of the useless. The *Tradition* says: "The heart that does not follow the warp of virtue and rightness is called stubborn." In ancient times, the refined was called beautiful; the rustic, coarse. Stubbornness means no adornment of wisdom; coarseness means no display of beauty. The spirit-person thus remains unfit for use.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Falling into form and instrumentality renders one usable. What is usable is objectified by things. When things reach the point of adornment and beauty, they become even more useful and not discarded. Stubbornness means no adornment; coarseness means no beauty—this is why I am of no use to the world, and the world and I remain indifferent to one another.

I alone differ from others, valuing the search for nourishment from the Mother.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: Like a foal or calf following its dam, one seeks only the Dao. The Dao gives birth, nurtures, rears, and fosters all things—thus it bears the meaning of the Mother. Seeking only the Dao is what sets one apart from those who lose their nature to custom.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Born of the Dao, one daily constructs oneself through things, losing one’s true nature and forgetting to return to the root. Losing what gave one life, one fails to know what sustains one—this is why one values seeking nourishment from the Mother. Seeking from the Mother is returning to the Dao and recovering one’s nature. Feeding from the Mother is being nourished by the Dao and preserving one’s authenticity.

**Chapter Twenty-One: The Manifestation of Vast Virtue**

The manifestation of vast virtue follows only the Dao.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: "One yin, one yang—this is called the Dao." What things obtain to live is called virtue. The Dao is ever without name—how could it be described? Thus, its virtue is called numinous. Virtue has direction and form, and all things obtain it equally—thus the Dao is made manifest. Cultivating nature and returning to virtue, when virtue reaches its fullness, it reverts to the beginning. Therefore, it follows only the Dao.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: The Dao is subtle in its formlessness, fundamentally beyond description. Virtue follows the Dao, so the Dao is made manifest through virtue. What is called virtue is what is obtained from the Dao. When virtue reaches its fullness and reverts to the beginning, it merges once more with the Dao.

The Dao as a thing is only vague and indistinct.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: The body of the Dao is utterly formless, yet its function is wondrous. It is the source of all things, yet nothing is not the Dao. "Vague" means it has images that may be seen. "Indistinct" means it has patterns that may be discerned. Yet what is called "having" is doubtful, verging on nothingness. Thus, it is said, "the Dao as a thing."

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: The Dao as a thing is what is perfectly blended—this is called the thing of no-thing. Vague, yet having images, though its images are not visible. Indistinct, yet having patterns, though its patterns cannot be discerned.

Vague and indistinct, within it are images.
Indistinct and vague, within it are things.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: What is seen is called an image; what is formed is called a thing. Within the indistinct and vague, images and things are both present. Like the great void transforming into thunder and wind, like the great clod changing into water and fire—thus it accomplishes transformation, thus it moves as spirits. This is called the wonder of the Dao.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Vague and indistinct—neither being nor non-being. From indistinct to vague, within it are images—from nothingness to being. From vague to indistinct, within it are things—from being to nothingness. Thunder, wind, water, and fire are images and things, from nothingness to being, from being to nothingness. Thus they accomplish transformation, thus they move as spirits, and return to the ultimate Dao. Therefore, it is called the wonder of the Dao.

Dark and obscure, within it is essence.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: "Dark" is the utmost of seclusion. "Obscure" is the concealment of brightness. Within the dark and obscure lies the source of utmost yin, where the Celestial One first appears, and essence truly arises.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Essence arises from the Celestial One as the root of bodily life. Thus, following the discussion of images and things, it speaks of the dark and obscure. After the vague and indistinct, with images and things, it describes the essence of the Dao’s body. The dark and obscure hold essence—this is the beginning of all things’ generation.

Its essence is utterly real, within it is sincerity.
From ancient times to the present, its name does not depart.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: Essence is the utmost of celestial virtue, upright and not false—thus it is called utterly real.

One remains unchanging, thus it is called "having sincerity." Uninterrupted in its constancy, thus its name does not depart.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: One that does not depart is complete in celestial virtue—this is the utmost of truth. One that does not change responds to its mandate like an echo—this is the utmost of sincerity. From ancient times to the present, there is neither past nor present; its name does not depart—this is what is called the eternal name.

"By this I know the origin of the myriad things." How do I know the nature of the myriad things? By this.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: The transformations of the myriad things pass like days, ceaseless and unending. The utmost truth of essence abides, never departing. The sage values essence, thus able to observe the changes of the myriad things and know their causes.

Without thought, it is quiescent; without action, it is unmoving. Yet moved by resonance, it penetrates the patterns of all under heaven. Thought arises as its inception, and the traces of achievement manifest—without the utmost essence of all under heaven, who could attain this?

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Things perfected in beauty are called "origins." The beauty of the myriad things shifts and changes without constancy, thus the transformations of the myriad origins flow unceasingly. The truth of their essence remains one and undivided, abiding without departure—thus one may know the impermanence of the myriad things.

**Chapter Twenty-Two: "Bending, Then Whole"**

"Bending, then whole."
The *Imperial Commentary* states: Flowing with things and moving with their waves, one preserves one’s form and life without loss. *Zhuangzi* says: "Those who bend externally follow others as companions."

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Without intent toward things, forgetting form in oneself, one can follow the changes of the myriad things, yielding and bending without resisting them. Thus, nothing harms them, and life is preserved whole.

"Yielding, then straight."
The *Imperial Commentary* states: Forsaking excess without resentment, enduring hardship without lament, thus inwardly straight without losing correctness. *The Book of Changes* says: "The inchworm’s bending is to seek extension."

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Straightness is sought through extension. Yielding to the crookedness of things without asserting one’s own straightness, thus inwardly straight without losing correctness—this is not bending oneself to seek straightness. If one clings to one’s own straightness and cannot follow change, this is not the way to seek extension.

"Hollow, then full."
The *Imperial Commentary* states: Without hoarding, thus having surplus.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Hollow is like a sunken place where water gathers, thus always full. A small hollow is always full without hoarding, thus having surplus.

"Worn, then renewed."
The *Imperial Commentary* states: If winter’s closure is not secure, spring’s growth will not flourish.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Worn in winter, renewed in spring—such is the principle of things.

"Little, then gained; much, then confused."
The *Imperial Commentary* states: The essentials of the Dao are not burdensome. Hearing and seeing much is not as good as simplicity. Taking branches for the root leads to lifelong confusion—this is called great delusion.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: The Dao, virtue, nature, and destiny do not depart from attaining oneness. The affairs and things of the world arise in myriad threads. Thus, the sage embraces the One as the model for all under heaven.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: Being without duality in its nature, its generation of things is unfathomable. Only the utmost essence of all under heaven can become the utmost spirit. The sage embraces the One to guard it, not disturbing its essence. Thus, speech becomes the model for all under heaven, and action becomes the law for all under heaven.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Little gains the principle of nature and destiny; much confuses the changes of affairs and things. Thus, the sage embraces the One without departing from reality, embodying the Dao and exhausting nature, merging self and other in mystery, becoming the model for all under heaven.

"Not self-displaying, thus bright."
The *Imperial Commentary* states: Not obscured by one’s own views, thus nothing is not illuminated—hence, bright.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Those who display themselves are obscured by partiality. Those who do not display themselves unite into brightness.

"Not self-asserting, thus illustrious."
The *Imperial Commentary* states: Not partial to one’s own correctness but following what is correct, thus achievements are great, names are illustrious, and all under heaven submits—hence, illustrious.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Those who assert themselves lose others. Those who do not assert themselves can employ others. Employing others, they follow what is correct, and achievements become illustrious—hence, illustrious.

"Not self-boasting, thus meritorious."
The *Imperial Commentary* states: *The Book of Documents* says: "You alone do not boast, thus none under heaven can contend with you for merit."

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Boasting leads to arrogance, and others will not acknowledge one’s merit.

"Not self-aggrandizing, thus enduring."
The *Imperial Commentary* states: *The Book of Documents* says: "You alone do not aggrandize yourself, thus none under heaven can contend with you for ability."

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Aggrandizing oneself leads to ostentation, and others will not help one achieve ability.

"Only by not contending does none under heaven contend with them."
The *Imperial Commentary* states: All people seek to be first; only the self remains behind, daily receiving the world’s disgrace. In this way, one always abides in the place of non-contention—who can contend with them?

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Having a self leads to contention; forgetting the self, nothing contends with them.

The ancient saying "bending, then whole"—was this empty words? Truly whole, one returns to it.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: The sage’s movement is like water, engaging with things without losing wholeness. Their response is like a marking-line, following principle without losing straightness. Knowing hollowness leads to fullness, without the burden of arrogance. Knowing wear leads to renewal, without the traces of boasting. As if by nature’s own doing, without knowing who does it—attaining bending alone, thus whole and returning to it. One can preserve the body, exhaust one’s years, and not know their end. This is called the person of complete virtue—was this empty words?

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Emptying the self to wander in the world, attaining bending to respond to principle, following the changes of things in great compliance without resisting them—this is the wholeness of attaining the Dao. Not self-displaying, not self-asserting, not self-boasting, not self-aggrandizing, and ultimately not contending—all are means to attain bending.

Whose clarity shines forth, whose achievements endure long—so that none in the world can contend with them—may be called the perfection of sincerity, wholly returned to it. Perfected in sincerity and wholly returned to it, thus one can preserve the body, exhaust one’s years, and not know their end.

**Chapter Twenty-Three: Few Words, Naturalness**

Few words, naturalness.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: "Few" means standing alone above the myriad things without opposing them—what *Liezi* calls "doubtful solitude." Abandoning cunning and contrivance, following Heaven’s principle without attending to externals, thus words are natural.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: "Few" means being distant from things, not paired with them, thus able to stand alone above the myriad things without opposing them. "Natural" means that which arises without cause. That which has a cause is human artifice; that without cause is Heaven’s principle. Thus, abandon cunning and contrivance, follow Heaven’s principle, and do not attend to externals.

Therefore, whirlwinds do not last the morning; sudden rains do not last the day. Who brings these about? Heaven and Earth.

Heaven and Earth cannot even endure forever—how then could man?

The *Imperial Commentary* states: In Heaven and Earth’s creation of the myriad things, wind disperses them, entrusting all forms to self-transformation; rain moistens them, allowing all things to self-nourish. Thus, they neither augment life nor urge its completion, and the myriad things naturally flourish between Heaven and Earth—this is why they endure long. But whirlwinds and sudden rains bring the affliction of discord between yin and yang; if something compels them, things suffer harm, and so they cannot last. The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Wind’s transformation lies in stirring and scattering; rain’s benefit lies in moistening and nourishing. When their qi is harmonious and their timing responsive, they entrust the myriad things to self-transformation and self-benefit—thus they endure long. But whirlwinds and sudden rains prevail in force, arriving with violence, injuring the harmony of the two qi, and disordering the seasons’ commands—thus things suffer harm. If even Heaven and Earth, in bringing these about, cannot last the span of a morning or a day, how much less can man? This is why one must return to the fewness of words and naturalness.

Therefore, those who engage with the Dao: those of the Dao unite with the Dao; those of virtue unite with virtue; those of loss unite with loss. Those who unite with the Dao, the Dao also attains them. Those who unite with virtue, virtue also attains them. Those who unite with loss, loss also attains them.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: The few stand beyond all engagement, beyond the realm of sound, awakening alone through their nature, becoming one with the Dao and not sharing with things—how could virtue be named, or loss be a burden? Only by not knowing the naturalness of solitary transformation, and instead deeming the Dao difficult to know and difficult to practice, as if ascending to heaven and seemingly unattainable, does one begin to toil the mind and exhaust the body in engaging with the Dao—sometimes leaning toward one side, sometimes blinded by one corner, and the arts of the Dao are torn asunder by the world. Those of the Dao unite with the Dao; those of virtue unite with virtue; those of loss unite with loss. Yet if they do not attain their own attainment, then what they attain is precisely what becomes loss. The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: The Dao forgets both self and things, abiding in the great void, awakening alone and reverting to constant nothingness. The Dao is but a forced name—what is there to engage with? To have something to engage with, something to attend to, is to depart from the Dao and enter the realm of forms and vessels, becoming the artifice of man. Those who deem the Dao difficult to know and difficult to practice thus unite with it. With attainment and loss, they exhaust their minds in thought and their bodies in toil, spending their lives in engagement, enslaving their natural truth without knowing to return. Thus, in the three—Dao, virtue, and loss—each takes what it recognizes as the Dao, humbles itself to engage with it, and seeks what it unites with, each attaining its own attainment. Yet what they attain is precisely a return to loss, not merging with the few.

Where trust is insufficient, there is distrust.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: Trust is without delusion; delusion sees truth and falsehood. If one takes the Dao as true and things as false, then trust is insufficient, and so there is distrust. The followers of Huishi and Han Fei all derive their terms from the Dao’s greatness as opposed to smallness, and thus it can be named. The Dao’s subtlety is small and hidden; its center is great and manifest.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: The Dao’s essence is constant nothingness, and thus it has no name. Because it pervades all, it is called by the name "Dao." Because it encompasses all, it is forcibly named "great." "Great" means "passing on." The *Imperial Commentary* states: It moves without lingering, thus it is called "passing on." The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Great and without place it does not go, thus it is so. "Passing on" means "far." The *Imperial Commentary* states: It responds without exhaustion, thus it is called "far." The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Passing on without cease, it responds without exhaustion. "Far" means "return." The *Imperial Commentary* states: Returning to the root is called stillness; stillness is reverting to the mandate, thus it is called "return." The Dao’s central essence, when named as great, pervades and encompasses all without distinction—this is what is meant by "in speaking of the far, it is not hindered." When the moving is still and the acting ceases, it returns and repeats its Dao, not departing from its nature—this is what the *Book of Changes* calls "in speaking of the near, it is still and correct." The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Passing on without cease, it becomes far; far, it must return; returning, it reverts to the Dao.

Thus, the Dao is great; Heaven is great; Earth is great; the king is also great. The *Imperial Commentary* states: The Dao covers and bears Heaven and Earth. Heaven covers all without exception; Earth bears all without exception. The king stands between Heaven and Earth, and with them forms a triad—thus he is also great. The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Heaven and Earth’s covering and bearing are enveloped by the Dao. The king stands between them, assisting in Heaven and Earth’s proper work to nurture the myriad things. Heaven governs Earth; the Dao governs Heaven. Nothing is greater than these four. Within the realm, there are four great ones, and the king occupies one of them. The *Imperial Commentary* states: Descending from the Dao, there are directions and forms, thus it is called "within the realm." Still, he is sagely; in motion, he is kingly. Able to penetrate the three powers and connect the human Dao, he is thus king. Therefore, he shares the same essence as the Dao, shares the same work as Heaven and Earth, and is likewise called "great." The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: The Dao gives rise to things yet is not a thing; in relation to things, it is called "having things." The Dao’s central essence, therefore, along with Heaven, Earth, and the king, has not yet departed from the greatness within the realm.

Man models himself on Earth; Earth models itself on Heaven; Heaven models itself on the Dao; the Dao models itself on naturalness. The *Imperial Commentary* states: "Man" refers to the king. Heaven does not produce, yet the myriad things transform; Earth does not grow, yet the myriad things are nurtured; the emperor and king do nothing, yet the world achieves its work. What they model themselves on is simply the Dao’s naturalness. The Dao models itself on naturalness because it responds to things. Naturalness is not the Dao’s entirety; it emerges to respond to things, thus descending to model itself below. The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: When there is something to align with, and it naturally becomes so, then naturalness resides above things, emerging below non-things—it is not the Dao’s entirety. Only because the Dao emerges to respond to things does it model itself below.

The four each model themselves on something, yet there is no before or after—none know their beginning.

**Chapter Twenty-Six: Weight as the Root of Lightness**
Weight is the root of lightness; stillness is the lord of restlessness.
Thus the noble person travels all day yet does not part from their heavy supplies.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: Weight does not sway or lose its hold, thus it is the root of lightness. Stillness does not move recklessly but maintains control, thus it is the lord of restlessness. Holding fast to stillness and weight, one seldom loses their way. The noble person travels all day without parting from their heavy supplies, and so treads perilous paths without harm.
The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Weight can bear lightness, but lightness cannot bear weight—thus weight is its root, and lightness may be lifted. Stillness can govern restlessness, but restlessness cannot govern stillness—thus stillness is its lord, and restlessness may be settled. The noble person travels all day without parting from their heavy supplies, meaning they are never without weight—how could they act lightly? Weight is difficult to move; when difficult to move, thoughts are unified; when thoughts are unified, they are comprehensive; when comprehensive, there is no peril in affairs. Heavy supplies sustain one on the road, thus they cannot be abandoned. When one’s inner self is weighty, external things become light—thus the desires of the ears and eyes cannot disturb, power and prestige cannot sway, and life and death cannot alter. Even when treading perilous paths, what cause for concern is there?
Though there be splendid sights, one dwells in tranquility, transcendent.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: Splendid sights belong to external things; tranquil dwelling belongs to the self. Only when the self is at peace can things be enjoyed.
The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Those who delight in external things are drawn outward, their hearts wandering among splendid sights. Those who cultivate inner harmony remain composed, their bodies at ease in tranquil dwelling. Thus, though splendid sights exist, they cannot compare to the transcendence of tranquil dwelling.
How could the lord of ten thousand chariots treat his person lightly before the world?
The *Imperial Commentary* states: The world is a great thing. One who possesses a great thing trembles as if driving six horses with rotten reins—thus one cannot treat their person lightly before the world.
The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: The ruler bears the weight of the world in his single person—thus he cannot treat his person lightly before the world. Lightness loses ministers; restlessness loses the lord.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: Without weight, there is no awe, thus one loses ministers. Without leading by example, one provokes transgression, thus one loses the lord.
The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Weight and stillness establish control, and the Way of ruler and minister is preserved.

**Chapter Twenty-Seven: Skillful Action**
Skillful action leaves no wheel tracks.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: What abides is spirit; what passes is transformation—thus there are no wheel tracks.
The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: To arrive without traveling is skillful action. The Dao moves through the world of its own accord, while I do nothing—thus there are no wheel tracks. In an age of perfect virtue, worthies are not esteemed, abilities are not employed; the ruler is like the tip of a branch, the people like wild deer. When spiritual transformation is unknown, what tracks could there be?
Skillful speech bears no flaw or blame.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: Harmonizing with the natural boundary, following its unfolding—thus there is no flaw or blame. The greatness of words and deeds begins with deliberation and ends in transformation. Only the sage can perfect their words and deeds, achieving the subtlety of transformation. Thus their actions leave no traceable tracks, and their words bear no burdensome flaws.
The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: To be understood without speaking is skillful speech. Without speaking, there is no flaw or blame. The sage withdraws their brilliance behind tassels of floss, entrusting sight and hearing to the world. Reverent in their southern-facing seat, affairs are accomplished among the hundred clans. What does Heaven say? The four seasons proceed, the hundred things are born.
Skillful reckoning requires no counting rods.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: Penetrating the One, all affairs are complete—how much more so for what lies beyond calculation? Thus, without counting rods, the myriad transformations are as clear as counting one or two.
The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Profoundly attuned to unity, following Heaven’s principle, they respond to the myriad complexities with clarity—what need is there for counting rods? This is the excellence of reckoning.
Skillful closure requires no bolts or bars yet cannot be opened.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: Guarding the spirit, withdrawing into secrecy.
The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Withdrawing sight and returning hearing, resting in the brilliance of inner illumination, the spirit returns to qi, and qi unites with the Dao—this is skillful closure within oneself. Preventing wrongdoing before it arises, cutting off evil before it sprouts, so that the hearts of the unworthy have no means to arise—this is skillful closure in external things.
Skillful binding requires no cords or knots yet cannot be undone.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: To rely on cords and knots for firmness is to pare away one’s nature. If one does not speak yet nourishes others with harmony, stands alongside them yet transforms them, then bonds are firm without knots—who could undo them?
The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: When the ruler binds the world with perfect trust, and the world is satisfied in trust, so that the old find peace and the young find care—who could undo this?
Thus the sage is ever skillful in saving people, and so there are no abandoned people. Ever skillful in saving things, and so there are no abandoned things.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: Skillfulness is the continuation of the Dao. When one is submerged in the Dao, there is no skillfulness to be named. Once the name of skillfulness is established, the Dao emerges to benefit the world. The sage embodies the Dao to aid the world, thus possessing these five skills and extending to people and things without abandonment. Yet the sage’s love for people and benefit to things, allowing things to fulfill their lives and people to delight in their natures, is not by design. Returning to the One without trace, they simply follow what is constant.
The world has lost the Dao. The world has entirely lost its tranquil, still, and constant nature, daily sinking into the realm of care and exhaustion—who but the sage can save it?
The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: The sage embodies the Dao in non-action, spiritually transforming the world. Rooted in the five skills, they save people before illness arises and save things before harm occurs. Yet they do not display the traces of their saving, and the people do not know by what power they are aided—each simply rests in their life. This is called inheriting illumination.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: To inherit is to bring forth without display. Inheriting illumination, one’s light shines yet does not dazzle.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: I do not reveal the means by which I act, and thus people do not know how it comes to be—this is to inherit the five virtues of the superior, called inheriting illumination. Therefore, the good person is the teacher of the not-good person, and the not-good person is the resource of the good person.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: "Resource" speaks of its benefit. Only when there is the not-good does one know the goodness of the good as beneficial.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Only with goodness does one see the not-goodness of others, thus the good person becomes the teacher of the not-good person. Only with not-goodness does one see the goodness of others, thus the not-good person becomes the resource of the good person. If one does not value their teacher nor cherish their resource,

The *Imperial Commentary* states: When all under heaven know the good as good, the not-good ceases to be. Good and not-good, self and other are both forgotten. With no room for intention, what need is there for valuing or cherishing? This is how the sage achieves great unity with all things.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: When good and evil are clear, emotions and deceit arise, and all under heaven becomes ungovernable. Thus, only when valuing and cherishing are absent do good and evil both fade, self and other become one reality, and the mysterious virtue is preserved whole. Though wisdom may lead to great delusion, this is the essential subtlety.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: The essential subtlety of the Dao does not obscure the many goods, nor does it employ wisdom. The seven sages all lose their way, with no path to inquire after—this principle accords with that.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Where there is knowledge, there are things, and one fails to know the subtlety of no-thing. Though called wisdom, it is great delusion regarding the Dao. The subtlety of the Dao is rooted in this, thus it is called essential.

*Exegesis on the Authentic Meaning of the Dao and Its Virtue by Emperor Huizong of Song, Scroll Four Concluded*
*Exegesis on the Authentic Meaning of the Dao and Its Virtue by Emperor Huizong of Song, Scroll Five*

**Exegesis by Gentleman-Official Zhang An**

**The Daoist Classic, Chapter Twenty-Eight: "Know the Male, Keep to the Female"**

"Know the male, keep to the female, and be the ravine of the world." As the ravine of the world, constant virtue does not depart, and one returns to the state of an infant.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: The male overcomes things with strength, while the female is merely gentle and still. The sage’s wisdom knows how to overcome things, yet abides in softness and stillness. All things turn toward him, drawing sustenance without exhaustion—thus he is the ravine of the world. Just as a ravine lies low and all waters flow toward it, so too does he hold fast to humility rather than strength. Then virtue and nature remain united without separation, which is called wholeness of power. Hence it is said: "Constant virtue does not depart, and one returns to the state of an infant." The infant’s qi is harmonious and not violent; its nature is pure and not yet scattered. Mencius said: "The great man does not lose the heart of a newborn child."

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: The male, being yang, moves and is strong; the female, being yin, is still and soft. Movement tends toward action, while stillness is rooted in holding fast. Moving without losing stillness—thus one knows the male and acts in due time. Only stillness can govern movement, so one guards the female and does not overcome with strength. The female is still and yields to others, becoming the refuge of all movement. Like a ravine, it receives streams and sustains things without showing depletion. Virtue reaches constancy, unified and unbroken—this is the ultimate stillness. "Returning to the state of an infant" means virtue reverts to its origin.

"Know the white, keep to the black, and be the model for the world." As the model for the world, constant virtue does not err, and one returns to the boundless.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: White illustrates the manifestation of virtue; black illustrates the return to the Dao. The sage illuminates his bright virtue yet silently unites with the Dao, without a single flaw. The world takes him as its standard and delights in following him without weariness—thus he is the model for the world. Upright and not reckless, steadfast as the four seasons, without deviation or error, such a one is difficult to exhaust or fathom, never having a limit. Hence it is said: "Constant virtue does not err, and one returns to the boundless." The *Book of Documents*, in the *Great Plan*, speaks of royal governance as "returning to its proper limit," while Laozi speaks of being the model for the world as "returning to the boundless." *Ji* (limit) means the center. "Proper limit" refers to virtue manifest in affairs, with the center as its utmost. "Boundless" refers to virtue’s return to the Dao, which cannot be attained.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: White, as a color, is unadorned; when illuminated by heaven’s light, it shines brightly. Black, as a color, is unchanging; returning to profound obscurity, it silently accords. Thus, black is what one guards, while white must not be unknown. Knowing the white yet guarding the black, one empties the self and does not rely on one’s own brightness, achieving unity and thereby uniting with the Dao. Taking this as a model, one endures without error, like the cycles of cold and heat, where heaven’s workings are not forced. Thus, "returning to the boundless"—to call it a "proper limit" is to remain centered; to call it "boundless" is to reach everywhere. Reaching everywhere is the return to the Dao.

"Know glory, keep to disgrace, and be the valley of the world." As the valley of the world, constant virtue is fulfilled, and one returns to simplicity.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: Beyond nature and destiny, there is nothing that is not a thing. The people of the world regard gain as glory and loss as disgrace, prosperity as glory and restraint as disgrace. Only the sage can view glory and disgrace as one, without a mind for seeking or conforming. Yet he does not strive for extravagance but takes restraint as his principle—this is emptiness alone. Thus he is the valley of the world. The valley is empty and can receive; it responds without hoarding. When virtue reaches this point, it is complete and cannot be surpassed. Hence it is said: "Constant virtue is fulfilled." Simplicity is the whole body of the Dao. Returning to simplicity, one can fully embody the Dao. Kings and dukes call themselves "solitary," "widowed," and "barren," yet embracing simplicity, the world submits.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: The sage empties himself to respond to the world. Knowing that things bring glory, he does not see what is desirable. His guard is in disgrace; he does not precede things or initiate movement, merely responding to them. Thus he is the valley of the world. His body is empty within, his responses inexhaustible. When virtue reaches this point, it suffices to nourish all things without showing beginning or end—this is the fulfillment of virtue. Simplicity is the wholeness of things, the body of the Dao. Only by fully embodying the Dao can one return to simplicity. When simplicity disperses, it becomes vessels.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: That which is above form is called the Dao; that which is below form is called vessels. There are forms and names, divisions and responsibilities. The Dao is whole; heaven and humanity unite as one. Vessels are dispersed; heaven and humanity separate into two.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Things are simple when whole; when put to use, they become vessels. When simplicity disperses, the wholeness of the Dao is divided; when vessels are made, things are put to use. The sage employs them as officials and leaders.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: The wholeness of the Dao, the sage uses to govern the self. The dispersion of the Dao, the sage uses to govern the world. There are forms that can be named, divisions that can be upheld—thus he establishes offices and leads subordinates, and the world is ordered. This is called "official and leader." The *Book of Changes* says: "Knowing the subtle and the manifest, knowing the soft and the hard—this is the aspiration of ten thousand men." This is of the same meaning.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: When the Dao is whole in simplicity, nature is one. When vessels differ in use, it is the distinction of materials. The sage establishes offices and leads subordinates, becoming their official and leader, in order to skillfully employ vessels. Thus, "great regulation is not cut apart."

The *Imperial Commentary* states: Transforming and shaping it lies in change; carving and sculpting myriad forms is not craft.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: The sage employs people according to their abilities, making them leaders of the hundred officials. Yet the sage governs the world through non-action, without exerting force.

**Chapter Twenty-Nine: The Will to Manifest**

Those who would take the world and act upon it—I see they will not succeed.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: The world is a great vessel. He who possesses this great vessel cannot be treated as a vessel. To treat it as a vessel yet not be bound by it—this is how one masters all vessels. He who understands that the master of vessels is not a vessel himself, and thus does not act upon the world, may be entrusted with the world. Those who would take the world and act upon it rely on cunning and force, straying far from the Way. Thus the sage governs through the transformative power of the Dao, not through cunning or force. The Qin’s downfall through coercion—was this not its meaning?

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Where there is grasping, things may be seized; where there is action, things may be undone. The world is called the divine vessel—it cannot be taken by those with intent, nor mastered by those with strength. The sage, free of intent and returning to non-action, is thus joyfully upheld by the world without weariness.

**The World as Divine Vessel**
The *Imperial Commentary* states: Bound by form and number, confined by direction and structure, limited between heaven and earth—this is a vessel. Yet it stands beyond measure, moves without fixed direction. Those who act upon it fail; those who cling to it lose. Thus it is called the divine vessel.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: It is called divine because it has no form; it is called a vessel because it is not formless. Whether form or formless, neither can be grasped—only those who embody the Dao attain it. Only those who embody the Dao attain it; it cannot be seized by cunning or force.

**Non-Action in Governance**
The *Imperial Commentary* states: To govern the myriad things and direct all movement, there must be one who is not a vessel—only then can the world be ordered. Thus it is said: the ruler must practice non-action and employ the world.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: To be bound by vessels is to be used by those who are not vessels. To act through non-action is to have the world act for you.

**The Perils of Action and Clinging**
*Those who act upon it fail.*
The *Imperial Commentary* states: To be capable of action but not of non-action is to be ensnared by cunning. As *Zhuangzi* says: "Excessive knowledge leads to failure."

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: To act through force is to exhaust one’s strength, leading to failure.

*Those who cling to it lose it.*
The *Imperial Commentary* states: The value of the Dao lies in timing. To cling without adapting is to lose the movement of the times—this is to violate the Dao.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Time shifts without pause; the Dao transforms with it. To cling without understanding is to lose the advantage of the moment.

Thus, some things move while others follow, some exhale while others inhale, some are strong while others are weak, some are upheld while others collapse.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: The principles of the myriad things: some move while others follow, like the sun and moon’s alternation. Some exhale while others inhale, like the four seasons’ succession. Some are strong while others are weak, like the waxing and waning of the five phases. Some are upheld while others collapse, like the blossoming and withering of plants. Subject to time and bound by fate, they cannot escape entanglement—only the sage is free from such burdens, standing independent above the ten thousand things. To come and go alone is called "possessing singularity." One who possesses singularity is called "most noble." Thus, in wielding the divine vessel, there is always abundance. Though the forms of things vary, the sage’s heart remains constant.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Where there is movement, there is following; where there is exhalation, there is inhalation; where there is strength, there is weakness; where there is upholding, there is collapse—this is the inherent nature of things. Only the sage, unburdened by things and standing above them, does not shift with their changes but skillfully wields the divine vessel. Therefore, the sage eliminates extremes, extravagance, and excess.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: The sage observes the transformations of the myriad things and knows the many burdens of excess. Thus, he does not seek to augment life, indulge nature, or overreach. He roams within the bounds of sufficiency. How then could he suffer the failure of those who act or the loss of those who cling? Thus it is said: ornate embellishments and ostentatious displays are of no benefit to governance.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: The sage understands the inherent principles of things—how movement and following, exhalation and inhalation, strength and weakness, upholding and collapse are never uniform. Thus, he knows that extremes lead to change, extravagance to distress, and excess to decline. Therefore, he strives to eliminate them, freeing himself from the burdens of excess.

---

**Chapter Thirty: Aiding the Ruler with the Dao**

One who aids a ruler with the Dao does not use military force to dominate the world.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: The deployment of armies and weapons is the least of virtues. Such means existed even among the ancients, but they were not primary. One who aids a ruler with the Dao focuses on the root—thus, he does not use military force to dominate the world.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Weapons are instruments of misfortune, used to assist virtue only when unavoidable. Thus, they are the least of virtues, not the Way of Yao and Shun, and should not be displayed before the king. Therefore, one must aid the ruler with the Dao.

*Such affairs tend to rebound.*
The *Imperial Commentary* states: As Mencius said, "It will return to you."

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Strength inevitably weakens—this is the nature of things. How much more so when one seeks dominance over the world through force? Can such strength endure?

*Where armies camp, thorns and brambles grow. After great wars, lean years follow.*
The *Imperial Commentary* states: Below, they plunder the people’s strength, so thorns and brambles grow. Above, they defy the seasons, so lean years come. As the *Book of Songs* says: "Pacify the ten thousand states, and abundant years will come." To pacify the ten thousand states is to harmonize the people; when the people are harmonious, the harmony of heaven and earth responds.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Where armies camp, they destroy the people’s livelihoods, so thorns and brambles grow. After great wars, the killing energy harms the harmony of heaven, so yin and yang fall into discord, and lean years arrive.

Thus, the wise achieve their aims and stop—they do not dare to seek dominance through force.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: To seek what is possible, to achieve completion, to expend little effort and gain much—this is the Way of the sage. To overcome others through force is called a "fierce virtue." Thus, victory in war lies in harmony, not in numbers.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: To be resolute in forceful conquest is what the common people call "achievement." To dare not seek dominance but to achieve without arrogance, boasting, or pride—this is to act only when unavoidable.

Fruition without arrogance, fruition without boasting, fruition without pride, fruition only when unavoidable—this is fruition without force.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: Among those who act only when unavoidable, the Way of the sage is found. Thus, armies achieve good fortune through harmony, avoid misfortune through righteousness, respond only when unavoidable, and seek only the completion of their task. Those who boast of themselves do not endure; those who praise themselves achieve nothing; those who are prideful are unworthy of regard. Embodying these four principles is how one achieves without force.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Fruition without arrogance suffices to perfect one’s abilities. Fruition without boasting suffices to accomplish great deeds. Fruition without pride ensures victory without excess. Fruition only when unavoidable ensures action without recklessness. Such is the nature of true fruition—how then could force be necessary?

All things, when they reach their peak, begin to decline.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: The transformations of summer’s growth and autumn’s decay are plain to see.
The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: What flourishes must inevitably wane—such is the nature of things. This is called "not the Way."
The *Imperial Commentary* states: The Way has no beginning or end; it does not transform with things.
The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: The Way employs softness and weakness, thus enduring unchanged through past and present. Things that burden themselves with strength are born contrary to the Way.
What is not the Way perishes early.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: To stand apart from the Way is to know the difference between strength and decay.
The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Forms do not arise without the Way; to stand apart from it is to perish early.

**Chapter 31: On Fine Weaponry**
Fine weaponry is an instrument of ill omen—some things despise it, and thus those who embody the Way do not dwell with it.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: Auspicious affairs bring good fortune; weapons are instruments of misfortune, and thus are called "inauspicious." When weapons are sheathed, they are wielded only in due time. Those who embody the Way display virtue and do not parade their arms, and thus do not dwell with them.
The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Where armies camp, thorns and brambles grow; after great wars, lean years follow. Such is the inauspicious nature of these instruments—thus they are despised, and those who embody the Way do not dwell with them.

Therefore, the noble person values the left in times of peace and the right in times of war. Weapons are instruments of ill omen, not the instruments of the noble person.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: The left is yang and governs life; the right is yin and governs killing. Yang is virtue; yin is punishment. The noble person values virtue and fears punishment, and thus weapons are not their instruments.
The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Yang’s power lies in giving life, thus the left is valued. Yin’s function is killing, thus the right is valued. To exalt killing is to make an instrument of ill omen. To exalt virtue is to make the instrument of the noble person.

When weapons must be used, tranquility is paramount—thus they are not glorified. To glorify them is to delight in them. To delight in them is to delight in killing.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: To suppress violence and rescue the disordered, one acts only when compelled—thus it is unavoidable. Without the intent to conquer, tranquility is paramount. Without certainty in conquest, weapons are not a source of joy—thus they are not glorified.
The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Killing to end killing—how could it be otherwise? Weapons are not a source of joy—thus they are not glorified. Not glorifying them, tranquility is paramount.

Those who delight in killing cannot realize their ambitions in the world.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: When a ruler embraces benevolence, none in the world can oppose him. To secure what endangers others and profit from their misfortune, to delight in what brings about their ruin—this is where resentment gathers and disaster converges.
The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: To delight in killing is the height of unbenevolence. Unbenevolence leads to a thirst for slaughter, leaving people with nowhere to turn for life—thus it becomes the source of resentment and calamity.

In auspicious affairs, the left is exalted; in inauspicious affairs, the right is exalted. Thus, the deputy general stands to the left, while the supreme general stands to the right—meaning that those in positions of power are treated with the rites of mourning. When many are killed, one weeps in sorrow. When victory is won, it is treated with the rites of mourning.
The *Imperial Commentary* states: The *Book of Changes* describes armies as a poison upon the world. Even in victory, some are inevitably poisoned—thus those in positions of power and those who triumph in battle are treated with the rites of mourning.
The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: The benevolent see no difference between self and other, near and far—they regard all with equal compassion. Thus, they despise fine weaponry as instruments of ill omen and treat armies with mourning rites. When many are killed, they weep in sorrow; when victory is won, they observe it with mourning rites—how could they do otherwise?

**Chapter 32: The Way Is Eternally Nameless**

**Imperial Commentary** states: The Dao is the beginning of Heaven and Earth—how could it be named?

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: That which gives birth to Heaven and Earth is the Dao. The Dao eternally abides in the nameless—what name could it possess?

Though the uncarved block is small, none under Heaven can subjugate it.
**Imperial Commentary** states: The uncarved block is an analogy for the Dao’s undivided wholeness. When form and name descend, the great governs the small. The Dao’s undivided wholeness does not depart from inherent nature. Though small, it discerns all things—what Zhuang Zhou called "the true ruler abiding within."

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: Before the Dao scatters into forms and vessels, it is called the uncarved block. Subtle and small, the myriad things none do not revere it—who could subjugate it?

Should lords and kings hold fast to it, the myriad things will of themselves submit.
**Imperial Commentary** states: When the Dao suffices as the master of things, they will of themselves submit. Zhuangzi said: "In pristine simplicity, the people’s nature is preserved. They serve the myriad things without recourse to coercion or punishment—this is nearly it."

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: The Dao’s uncarved block is the undivided whole. Should lords and kings attain and hold fast to it, establishing it as the norm for all under Heaven, things will come of themselves without summons.

Returning to the nameless is the Dao. Turning toward the existent is the realm of things. Thus, the Dao is the master of things, and things submit to the Dao and return to it—therefore they are called guests.

Heaven and Earth unite to descend sweet dew. No one commands it, yet it spreads evenly of itself.
**Imperial Commentary** states: The Dao in its pure simplicity, held fast and never lost, not only causes things to submit of themselves. Above, it reaches Heaven; below, it coils through Earth. Above and below, it flows in unison with Heaven and Earth, so that communication and harmony are achieved, and the myriad things all receive its blessing. Sweet dew is the harmonious qi of Heaven and Earth. The *Tradition* says: "The virtue of emperors and kings reaches above to the Great Clarity, below to the Great Tranquility, and in between to the myriad spirits—then sweet dew descends."

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: When lords and kings attain the One and hold fast to it, they thereby harmonize the boundary between Heaven and humanity, leaving no gap. Thus, the mutual resonance of Heaven and Earth, the harmony of yin and yang, and the descent of sweet dew spread evenly throughout all under Heaven—who causes this to be so?

Once names are established, names too come into being. One must also know when to stop. Knowing when to stop is how one avoids peril.
**Imperial Commentary** states: In the sequence of the Great Dao, after five transformations, forms and names may be invoked. Once forms can be named, the Dao declines and virtue wanes. Simplicity scatters, and the uncarved block dissolves—none can halt it. The humane of the age lament the world’s calamities with anxious eyes, while the inhumane sever the bonds of their nature to glut themselves on wealth and honor. The sage is not thus. When names are first established, he does not follow things in their flux. Serene and self-sufficient, who could endanger him? Thus it is said: knowing when to stop, one avoids peril.

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: The Dao, whole in the One, is the uncarved block. When the uncarved block first establishes names, names arise from the One. From the One to the ten thousand, no thing lacks a name. Rectifying names and following principle—this is how one knows when to stop. To cling to names and become mired in existence, to drift with things in their changes, to proceed without return—this is to invite the calamities of worldly entanglement, to forget the truth of nature and life, and to lose one’s place of security. How could one not be imperiled?

The Dao’s presence in all under Heaven is like streams and valleys flowing into rivers and seas.
**Imperial Commentary** states: All under Heaven is one nature. The Dao’s presence in all under Heaven unites through nature, just as streams and valleys gather into rivers and seas through water. Those of the same kind attain; those of like categories respond. When the sage presides over the world, what does he do? He follows nature, and that is all.

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: Nature is one, emerging from the Dao’s great wholeness. Thus, nature and the Dao are both obscured in the One. Scattered, they become streams and valleys; gathered, they become rivers and seas—what difference is there in the water?

**Chapter 33: He Who Knows Others Is Wise**
He who knows others is wise...

**Imperial Commentary** states: The *Commentaries* say: Wisdom is like the eye—it can see a hundred paces away but cannot see its own lashes. Discerning others’ crookedness or uprightness, distinguishing black from white, is the work of wisdom in knowing others.

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: To know others through one’s private perceptions and grasp their true nature is called wisdom. Though wisdom arises from Heaven, human effort plays a part. Thus, one remains blind to one’s own perceptions. He who knows himself is enlightened.

**Imperial Commentary** states: The *Book of Changes* says: "Returning to oneself is self-knowledge." The *Commentaries* say: "Inward vision is called enlightenment." Wisdom in knowing others engages with the external, entangling the mind in the world. Returning to self-knowledge is stillness that reverts to the root, seeing oneself alone—this is the mirror of Heaven and Earth, the illumination of the ten thousand things.

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: Knowing things leads to chasing illusions; knowing oneself returns to the root. Returning to the root is emptiness, unity, and stillness. Only in stillness is there clarity—sufficient to mirror Heaven and Earth, to illuminate the ten thousand things.

He who overcomes others is strong; he who overcomes himself is mighty.

**Imperial Commentary** states: The perfected person values virtue over strength, strives to overcome the self rather than others. Cunning the wise into foolishness, emboldening the brave to cowardice—this is overcoming others, relying on force. Overcoming one’s private desires, nurturing uprightness without harm—this is overcoming the self. Outwardly, one stands alone without fear; inwardly, one withdraws from the world without distress. Going anywhere, one is invincible—this is what it means to be mighty.

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: Overcoming others relies on force; overcoming the self relies on might. Overcoming others means others may one day not be overcome; overcoming the self means nothing can oppose it—hence, it is called mighty. When desires reach their peak, when profit and harm grind against each other, when power and might descend, when wealth and nobility tempt, when poverty and lowliness press, when life and death compel—nothing sways the heart. This is what is meant by overcoming the self.

He who knows contentment is rich.

**Imperial Commentary** states: To possess myriad differences is called richness. He who knows contentment focuses on inner wandering and finds sufficiency within. All things are complete; kingdom and wealth are as one.

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: He who does not know contentment endlessly seeks with insatiable desire, never finding satisfaction. Finding sufficiency within and needing nothing from without—what richness could surpass this?

He who acts with perseverance has resolve.

**Imperial Commentary** states: Unceasing self-striving is the resolve for the Dao.

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: Strength in overcoming the self, acting without cease—such is the resolve for the Dao.

He who does not lose his place endures.

**Imperial Commentary** states: Standing firm without changing direction, one endures in the Dao. Those who shift with time and move with things endure but briefly.

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: Embracing the One without departure, guarding qi without disorder, spirit whole and wandering in the origin of things—thus one returns to the constancy of the Dao.

He who dies yet does not perish is long-lived.

**Imperial Commentary** states: Life has its sprouting; death has its return. Tracing origins and reverting to endings, one understands the discourse on life and death. The sage comprehends the Dao of day and night, knowing that death is not fundamentally different from life. Thus, when the form dissolves, the spirit does not perish—it merges with Heaven and Earth, its limit unknown. What is this if not long life? The meaning of this chapter begins with knowing others to exhaust principle, centers on knowing contentment to fulfill nature, and concludes with non-perishing to reach destiny. Then creation lies within oneself. Who but one who transcends past and present, entering the realm of neither death nor life, could achieve this?

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: Where there is life, there is death; where there is death, there is life. Life and death are like the changes of morning and evening. All things that enter and exit transformation are thus. The sage understands the transformations of life and death, comprehends the principles of day and night. Though the body follows change, the awakened nature remains serene, its limit unknown. The deluded and ignorant, clinging to confusion, though alive, are as if dead. Those who perceive broadly, forgetting the form, keep spirit whole in the One. Seeing no end or beginning, though one may speak of death, who knows its perishing? Thus, it is called long life.

**Chapter 34: The Great Dao Flows Everywhere**
The great Dao flows everywhere—it may be grasped to the left or right.

**Imperial Commentary** states: Flowing without attachment, thus movement and stillness are not lost, coming and going are inexhaustible. Left or right—nothing is beyond its reach.

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: The Dao is present in all things, thus it flows without restriction. Left or right—what attachment could there be?

The ten thousand things rely on it to live, yet it does not refuse. Its work complete, it does not dwell.

**Imperial Commentary** states: Those who depart rely on it; those who seek receive from it. The ten thousand things take form and transform, gain wisdom and strength, yet it does not claim its work. Like the four seasons, when its work is done, it departs.

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: Zhuangzi said: "Without the Dao, forms do not arise." Thus, the Dao is what the ten thousand things rely on to live. The Dao gives life to the ten thousand things yet never refuses. It gives life to what naturally arises, completes what naturally comes to be—what work is there to claim? It clothes and covers the ten thousand things yet does not act as their master, thus it is ever without desire—it may be called small.

The ten thousand things return to it yet do not know it as their master—it may be called great.

**Imperial Commentary** states: When the Dao returns to utmost subtlety, it is small, distinguishing itself from things. When it manifests in utmost change, it is great, interacting with things. Distinguishing itself, it is ever without desire; interacting with things, the ten thousand things return to it. It blankets the ten thousand things yet does not display its governing power—thus it does not act as master. It animates the multitude yet none glimpse its path of return—thus they do not know it as master. The Dao cannot be named as small or great. What can be named is only the Dao’s reach toward things.

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: The Dao pervades the ten thousand things yet hides in utmost nothingness, never emerging to master them—thus it is said not to act as master.

Ever without desire, it distinguishes itself from things and returns to utmost subtlety—thus it is named "small." The ten thousand things dance in the Dao and return to it, yet never know how it acts as master. Interacting with things, it manifests in utmost change, yet things cannot comprehend it—thus it may be named "great." That which does not act as master is the Dao; that which does not know the master is things. Things are insufficient to know the Dao, and what can be seen of it is only its boundless greatness.

Therefore, the sage never acts as great, and so accomplishes greatness.

**Imperial Commentary** states: One whose merit blankets the world yet seems not to originate from oneself—thus their enterprise is great and all-encompassing. Mencius said: "Great and transformed—this is called sageliness." Being great yet able to transform, how could there be the burden of striving for greatness? This is how one accomplishes greatness.

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: The Dao, by not acting as master, causes the ten thousand things to return to it without knowing—thus it may be named "great." Therefore, the sage does not exalt themselves, and so accomplishes greatness, for they embody the Dao.

**Chapter 35: Holding to the Great Image**
Hold to the great image, and all under heaven will come.
**Imperial Commentary** states: The image is like the celestial bodies suspended in the sky—it acts not, yet moves with vigor; it speaks not, yet displays its patterns. The sage’s governance of the world abides in non-action, practices the teaching without words, and the people turn to them as to parents. Thus it is said: "Hold to the great image, and all under heaven will come."

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: "Great" implies form; "image" implies likeness—hence the use of "hold." The sage embodies the Dao in non-action, so what is there to hold? Yet in emerging to order the world, the Dao’s movement and the patterns it displays indeed form an image. Holding to this image without revealing its traces, all under heaven are transformed by the Dao—thus they become the world’s destination. This image originates in the Dao, so it is called "great."

Coming yet unharmed,
**Imperial Commentary** states: Yin and yang harmonize in stillness; ghosts and spirits are not disturbed; the multitude of beings are not injured; the ten thousand things do not perish prematurely. Though the people possess knowledge, they have no use for it—what harm could there be?

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: When all under heaven come, those who follow in traces are many. Governing through traces is what brings harm. The sage acts not from above yet fulfills the natural course of the ten thousand things. All beings grow spontaneously, unaware of their origin; all attain fulfillment, unaware of how they receive it. Thus between heaven and earth, whether in the hidden realms of yin and yang and ghosts and spirits or in the manifest realms of the multitude of beings and the people, each follows its nature. There is no place they go that is not beneficial—thus it is said "unharmed."

Secure, peaceful, and at ease.
**Imperial Commentary** states: Secure, then there is no fear of danger or ruin; peaceful, then there is no peril of steep slopes or uneven ground; at ease means unobstructed and well-ordered.

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: Coming yet unharmed, there is no place they go that is not naturally secure. Secure, they attain peace and align with the Dao—this is the Dao’s state of ease. Thus, security leads to peace, and peace leads to ease.

Music and delicacies make the passing traveler stop.
**Imperial Commentary** states: Those delighted by sounds and flavors are the world’s people, entangled by things and unable to free themselves. Things bind them, so they stop.

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: Music moves people through sound; delicacies please the mouth through taste. By what they find beautiful, they influence others, and the heart is detained—so unable to free themselves.

The Dao’s spoken words are bland, seemingly without flavor.
Looked at, they cannot be seen; listened to, they cannot be heard; used, they cannot be exhausted.
**Imperial Commentary** states: What can be tasted has been tasted, yet that which tastes the taste has never appeared—thus it is bland, seemingly without flavor. What can be seen as color has been displayed, yet that which colors the color has never manifested—thus looked at, it cannot be seen. What can be heard as sound has been heard, yet that which sounds the sound has never been uttered—thus listened to, it cannot be heard. Such is it: it can be bitter or sweet, black or yellow, gong or shang. Without knowledge, yet nothing is unknown; without ability, yet nothing is unachievable—thus used, it cannot be exhausted.

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: The utmost Dao is without flavor, without sight, without sound, yet returns to utmost nothingness. Responding to being from non-being, it arises from essence to function—thus used, it cannot be exhausted.

**Chapter 36: When About to Contract It**
When about to contract it, you must first expand it.
When about to weaken it, you must first strengthen it.
When about to abolish it, you must first exalt it.
When about to take it away, you must first give it.
**Imperial Commentary** states: Yin and yang illuminate each other, alternately covering and ordering one another. The four seasons succeed each other, alternately generating and destroying. The principles of the ten thousand things and the traditions of human relations—whether in their gathering or scattering, their flourishing or decline, their sudden rise or their waning and waxing—always emerge from the subtlest beginnings, and none perceive their signs. Only the sage who studies these subtleties attains foresight of good fortune; the worthy merely approach this ability.

Yang flourishes in summer, yet yin is born at noon; yin congeals in winter, yet yang is born at midnight. Goujian sought to exhaust Wu, so he urged them to attack Qi; Zhibo sought to raid Chouyou, so he presented them with broad chariots. This is how the sage treads on frost and knows the coming of solid ice—observing the ebb and flow of fullness and emptiness, not confined to their forms. Thus, the brave cannot weaken them, and the wise cannot seize from them.

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: The movement of yin and yang, the principles of the ten thousand things—whether in their flourishing or decline, strength or weakness, fullness or waning, success or failure—revolve endlessly, always with subtle signs emerging from the most minute. The sage studies these signs and attains foresight—thus they achieve subtle clarity. Therefore, firmly expanding it contracts its expansion; firmly strengthening it weakens its strength. If their rise is unjust, then firmly exalting it will cause its power to abolish itself. If their taking is improper, then firmly giving to them will cause their possession to be seized of itself. Thus, when hardness and strength lie with the other, and softness and weakness with oneself, the strong will surely break—this is the natural principle.

The sage responds to the myriad changes, accomplishes the world’s affairs, yet none know how this comes to be—it is by this Way. This is called *subtle clarity*.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: That which has not yet shown signs is *subtle*, yet its principle is most evident. Yang Xiong said: "When water stills, the depths become clear; when trees wither, the branches fade. The worthy perceive it, yet the multitude does not."

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Within the formless, one alone discerns what is present—so subtle is this that the principles of the ten thousand things lie hidden within it. By studying the subtle and attaining foresight, by delving into the profound and grasping it, one achieves utmost clarity. The worthy one’s function is one with creation, yet how could the multitude comprehend it? The contraction and expansion, the weakening and strengthening, the rise and fall, the giving and seizing—such is *subtle clarity*. The soft overcomes the hard; the weak overcomes the strong.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: Only the sage can amass countless small victories to achieve great triumph. The *Classic* says: "Nothing in the world is softer or weaker than water, yet nothing surpasses it in attacking the hard and strong." The *Outer Chapters* of the *Zhuangzi* discuss the mutual admiration of the *kui*, the louse, and the wind: "Point to me, and you surpass me; follow me, and you surpass me. Yet only I can snap great trees and topple grand houses."

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: To overcome hardness with hardness is to ensure that hardness breaks; to restrain strength with strength is to ensure mutual harm. The soft does not contend, yet nothing contends with it. The weak does not oppose, yet nothing opposes it. Thus, hardness and strength are instead overcome by softness and weakness—such is the principle and the natural tendency.

Fish cannot leave the depths; a state’s *sharp instruments* must not be shown to others.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: The depths are where fish conceal themselves; *sharp instruments* are how a state controls others. A fish that swallows boats, once stranded and bereft of water, may be tormented by ants—thus it cannot leave the depths. If a ruler displays rewards, ministers will exploit his favor; if he displays punishments, they will seize his authority. Rewards and punishments are tools of governance, yet even these must not be shown—how much less the Way of governance? The sage wields *sharp instruments* without displaying them, not by relying on strength, but by possessing a wondrous Way. He exhausts the realm without acquiring a reputation for wisdom, subdues the ten thousand things without earning merit for courage. He does not strive to conquer things yet attains the constant Way of victory.

Yang opens and yin closes; transformations are endless. He steers the ministers, governs the world, yet none can fathom him. Thus, he controls others without being controlled by them. The root lies above; the essential lies with the ruler—then the world is well-ordered.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Fish conceal themselves in the deep abyss; if they leave it, they are controlled by others. A state conceals its *sharp instruments*; if it displays them, others may fathom and thus cannot be controlled. The sage operates the mechanism of transformation in unfathomable ways, wielding supreme authority to adapt to change. He governs the world, commands the myriad movements, yet his divine virtue and wondrous Way remain beyond the reach of all—none can glimpse their bounds. Even his reputation for wisdom and merit for courage cannot be described, so how could the tools of governance, rewards and punishments, ever be inverted and wielded against him?

**Chapter 37: The Dao Constantly Takes No Action**

The Dao constantly takes no action, yet there is nothing it does not do.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: Still and unmoving, yet when it responds, it penetrates all affairs under heaven.
