The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: The Dao constantly takes no action—this is the essence of the Dao. Yet there is nothing it does not do—this is the function of the Dao. The Dao does not act, yet naturally accords with the ten thousand things, accomplishing all without exception.

Should the lords and kings be able to hold to it, the ten thousand things will transform of themselves.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: When a mirror’s water meets forms, it does not employ cleverness, yet the square, round, curved, and straight cannot escape it. If lords and kings hold to the Dao to govern the world, they enter the realm of non-action, yet their actions arise from non-action. They bestow transformation upon the ten thousand things, and the ten thousand things are transformed by it—like nature acting of itself, unaware of who acts. Thus, it is called *self-transformation*.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: How does Heaven speak? The four seasons proceed, and the myriad things are nurtured. If lords and kings embody the Dao and emulate Heaven, governing through non-action, they do not abandon what must be done. They appear to accomplish all without acting, for they follow the principles of things and ride the timely moment. What do *I* do? The Dao guards it for me. I transform the ten thousand things with the Dao, and the ten thousand things are transformed by the Dao, yet none know who acts—thus it is called *self-transformation*.

If, in the midst of transformation, desire arises, I will suppress it with the nameless uncarved block.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: Mencius said: "Those who wait for King Wen before they rise are ordinary people." The people merely follow the ruler’s transformation. If desire arises, they depart from the Dao to pursue goodness, act with perilous virtue, abandon their nature to follow their hearts—this is insufficient to stabilize the world. Only the Dao is nameless, simple and undivided, so those who act are suppressed by it. Nothing is better than loyalty to rescue the hasty—this is why.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: When the ruler transforms the world with the Dao, the ten thousand things are transformed by the Dao the ruler holds, yet none know how they are transformed. They will return to the constancy of nature and destiny, all alike arising from the realm of non-action—thus it is called *transformation*. To act is to engage in action; if desire arises, it will incline toward action and flow into artifice. With the nameless uncarved block, I steady their resolve, extinguish their delusive desires, and restore them to the wholeness of Heaven’s nature.

The nameless uncarved block will also have no desires.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: Ji Zhen’s "non-action" is but one aspect of things; among the ancient arts of the Dao, some resided in this. Yet few can fully embody the form of Heaven and Earth—thus, it will also have no desires. This is how Laozi dispels confusion and removes obstructions.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: The Dao is a forced name. To forget the totality of what one guards, to loosen the heart that desires to act—this is the nameless uncarved block. The nameless uncarved block will also have no desires, so that even the desire for non-desire is forgotten.

To forget what one has forgotten is to return to the state of no-thing.
When self and things are originally the same, what desires could there be?
Through non-desire one attains stillness, and the world will of itself become upright.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: When water is still, it levels even the most precise measuring tool—thus the great craftsman takes it as his model. Through non-desire one attains stillness, and so does not lose the upright; this begins with the self, and thus the world will of itself become upright. The *Book of Changes* says: "The Qian Dao transforms and changes, each rectifying its nature and destiny." The Qian Dao transforms and changes—this is non-action; each rectifies its nature and destiny—this is through non-desire one attains stillness, and the world will of itself become upright. To govern the world with the Dao, reaching the point where each rectifies its nature and destiny—this is called the ultimate governance.

The *Minister’s Exegesis* states: Without desire, the heart returns to nothingness; through stillness, emptiness unifies and becomes upright—first the self is rectified, and thus the world will of itself become upright. When the world reaches the point where each rectifies its nature and destiny, this is the ultimate achievement of Daoist governance.

*Exegesis on the True Meaning of the Dao and Its Virtue by Emperor Huizong of Song, Scroll Five Concluded*
*Exegesis on the True Meaning of the Dao and Its Virtue by Emperor Huizong of Song, Scroll Six*

Ministerial Exegete, Gentleman for Court Service, Zhang An’s Exegesis on the *Virtue Classic*

The Imperial Commentary states: The Dao has no fixed form or substance, while virtue may be complete or deficient. When united with the Dao, virtue is without virtue and may be named; when distinguished from virtue, it has names that may be discerned. Benevolence, righteousness, propriety, and wisdom are received according to measure and applied according to the times—these are merely aspects of virtue. Those who embody the Dao differ from this, and thus are placed in the latter section of the classic.

The Minister’s Exegesis states: The Dao is but a forced name, returning to constant nothingness, and thus has no fixed form or substance. Virtue is that which is obtained, inheriting the Dao’s loss, and thus may be complete or deficient. When virtue is without attainment, merging with unified governance, then virtue and the Dao blend into namelessness. When virtue is attained through self-interest, then virtue and the Dao become distinctly discernible. Those who attain it as transformation are benevolent; those who attain it as principle are righteous; those who embody it as ritual are proper; those who embody it as knowledge are wise. Virtue’s reception and application have names that may be discerned, yet they drift far from the Dao. Only those who fully embody virtue truly attain nothing, and thus never depart from the Dao.

**Chapter Thirty-Eight: The Highest Virtue Does Not Act as Virtue**

The highest virtue does not act as virtue, and thus possesses virtue.

The Imperial Commentary states: That by which things obtain life is called virtue. When all equally attain it, silently harmonizing with the Dao, erring without regret and acting rightly without self-satisfaction—this is called *not acting as virtue*. Confucius did not dwell in his sageliness but acted as a sage in due time, and thus possessed virtue.

The Minister’s Exegesis states: Things are born of the Dao, each attaining it in its own way. When virtue is complete in nature, it merges with the Dao as one, never having differed from it. Without attaining the Dao, the Dao naturally attains itself—thus virtue pervades the myriad things without encumbrance.

The lesser virtue does not lose virtue, and thus is without virtue.

The Imperial Commentary states: Claiming and possessing it, acting with self-interest to lose the Dao—what virtue is there in this?

The Minister’s Exegesis states: Taking virtue as attainment and attainment as grasping, holding to it without loss—this is far removed from the Dao.

The highest virtue acts without acting.

The Imperial Commentary states: Without thought, one attains; without effort, one hits the mark; without action, one reaches the goal—this is the highest virtue.

The Minister’s Exegesis states: Without acting, one attains nothing of the Dao; attaining nothing of the Dao, one returns to ultimate nothingness, and thus acts without acting.

The lesser virtue acts with purpose.

The Imperial Commentary states: Without thought, one does not attain; without effort, one does not hit the mark; without action, one does not reach the goal—this is the lesser virtue. Is this not the division between the sage and the worthy?

Transcending form and relinquishing knowledge, one merges with the great unity. Benevolence, righteousness, propriety, and wisdom—though one seeks to simplify them, they cannot be discarded, and thus one acts without acting. Bending to rituals and music, uttering sighs of approval and reproach, one comforts the hearts of the world. Attaining what others attain without attaining it oneself—thus one acts with purpose.

The Minister’s Exegesis states: With action comes attainment, and with attainment of the Dao, one cannot remain without acting, for circumstances compel it.

The highest benevolence acts without acting.

The Imperial Commentary states: Yao and Shun’s benevolence was innate, covering the world without seeking profit—thus they acted without acting.

The Minister’s Exegesis states: Virtue admits all without exclusion, and benevolence flows forth without contrivance—thus it acts without acting.

The highest righteousness acts with purpose.

The Imperial Commentary states: Measuring the enemy and assessing what is fitting is called righteousness. Establishing oneself and regulating affairs—can one remain without acting?

The Minister’s Exegesis states: With oneself to establish and affairs to regulate, where benevolence is insufficient, the Dao lacks no principle—thus righteousness’s achievements are manifest and cannot cease.

The highest propriety acts, yet none respond, so one rolls up one’s sleeves and forces compliance.

The Imperial Commentary states: Ritual is for interacting with others, demonstrating to people the patterns of benevolence and righteousness—its uses are many. Yet if none reciprocate after one has exhausted one’s offerings, then resentment and contention arise, leading to disorder. In the Spring and Autumn period, a single unanswered word or an improper bow could cause two states to shed blood—rolling up one’s sleeves and forcing compliance is but a minor calamity. The sage values benevolence and devalues righteousness; ritual is but a means to practice them, not the proper path. Thus, the highest benevolence is akin to virtue, the highest righteousness acts with purpose, and the highest propriety meets with no response.

The Minister’s Exegesis states: Ritual bends to prevent affairs and establish regulations, manifesting the patterns of benevolence and righteousness through deportment. Though named a tool to halt transgression, it still demands reciprocity and precedence in exchange. Its complexity breeds mutual reproach—this is truly the root of contention and chaos. Thus, when the Dao is lost, virtue follows; when virtue is lost, benevolence follows; when benevolence is lost, righteousness follows; when righteousness is lost, propriety follows. Ritual is the thinning of loyalty and trust, and the beginning of chaos.

The Imperial Commentary states: The Dao cannot be attained, so when the Dao is lost, virtue follows. Virtue cannot be perfected, so when virtue is lost, benevolence follows. Benevolence can be enacted, and enacting it approaches righteousness—thus when benevolence is lost, righteousness follows. Righteousness can be diminished, and when diminished, it is adorned with ritual—thus when righteousness is lost, propriety follows. By the time propriety arises, one has drifted ever farther from the Dao, and what is lost grows ever more numerous. No two things flourish simultaneously—this is like yin and yang. Principle alternates between yielding and contending—this is like might and virtue. Where substance is abundant, appearance is meager—this is like the ritual between father and son. From this, we see that where ritual is elaborate, substance must decline. When substance declines, pretense follows, and contention and chaos arise. Thus, ritual is the thinning of loyalty and trust, and the beginning of chaos.

The Minister’s Exegesis states: Attaining the Dao is the Dao’s loss. Acting through benevolence is virtue’s loss. Mutual dependence and mutual sustenance are transformation’s loss. Establishing regulations and boundaries is righteousness’s loss. Form obscures essence, and breadth drowns the heart—thus loyalty and trust thin. What ritual regulates is external; what it attends to is superficial; what it constrains is its elaborate patterns—thus what it originates from is but the tip. This is how emptiness and pretense daily increase, becoming the beginning of chaos.

The one who relies on foreknowledge is the flower of the Dao and the beginning of folly.

The Imperial Commentary states: The Dao descends and emerges, and from this emergence, knowledge arises. Using knowledge as a chisel, one carves and sharpens it, exhausting the spirit and recklessly conjecturing—this is called foreknowledge. Foreknowledge pursues the tip while forgetting the root, thus it is the flower of the Dao. The heart labors and knowledge grows ever more entangled, thus it is the beginning of folly. Even if one’s guesses often hit the mark, this is why Confucius despised Zigong.

The Minister’s Exegesis states: The Dao takes not-knowing and not-recognizing as its root and values acting without reliance on knowledge; thus, wisdom and craft are but its tip. The flower is the tip of grasses and trees—when the flower flourishes, the root must decline. To attend to wisdom and craft, to anticipate affairs with foreknowledge, is to drift far from the root, thus it is the flower of the Dao. To regard knowledge as wisdom is the very reason it becomes the beginning of folly. Therefore, the great man abides in thickness, not thinness; dwells in substance, not its flower. Thus, he discards that and selects this.

The Imperial Commentary states: That which lies in "that" is what the Dao abandons; that which lies in "this" is what the Dao esteems. What the Dao esteems is thick and not thin, substantial and without flower. Unless one’s wisdom suffices for self-knowledge, returning to the original nature and not drifting into the tip-habits of things, who could achieve this? The *Book of Changes* says, "Sincere return brings no regret," for the center is where one examines oneself. "Sincere" is the utmost of thickness. That which is thick in man from birth is his nature. To return to one’s nature is to abide in thickness—this is how the great man embodies the Dao and models virtue.

The Minister’s Exegesis states: "This" is what lies within me—it is the root of the Dao. "That" is what lies in things—it is the tip of the Dao. The root is spoken of as thick and substantial; the tip is spoken of as thin and flowery. Selecting "this" is how one abides in thickness and dwells in substance; discarding "that" is how one does not abide in thinness nor dwell in its flower. Knowing what to discard and what to select—this is called the great man.

**Chapter Thirty-Nine: Those of Old Who Obtained the One**

Those of old who obtained the One:
Heaven obtained the One and became clear;
Earth obtained the One and became tranquil;
The spirits obtained the One and became numinous;
The valleys obtained the One and became full;
The myriad things obtained the One and came to life;
The lords and kings obtained the One and became the norm for all under heaven.
Their attainment was the same One.

The Imperial Commentary states: *Zhuangzi* says, "When one is through with the One, the myriad affairs are complete." Governing by the One leaves no room for duality; embracing the One leaves no room for departure; guarding the One leaves no room for change. To know the One is to know all things; not to know the One is to know nothing. Those of old who obtained the One embodied the utmost essence of all under heaven—nothing could match it. Thus, that which stands firm above is pure and unadulterated; that which rests stable below is still and unchanging. The most recondite and formless are the spirits—they obtain the One and are not obscured. The most empty and responsive are the valleys—they obtain the One and are not exhausted. The myriad things transform essence into form, thus they obtain the One and live. The lords and kings alone govern the many, thus they obtain the One and become the norm for all under heaven. From heaven and earth to the lords and kings, though their positions differ above and below, and their natures scatter between the hidden and the manifest, yet what makes heaven clear, earth tranquil, and lords and kings the norm for all under heaven is nothing sought from without nor imposed from outside. It is simply their attainment of the One. Thus it is said, "Their attainment was the same One."

The Minister’s Exegesis states: "Those of old" refers to antiquity; "now" refers to the present. Antiquity speaks of a time not far removed from the Dao, when heaven, earth, spirits, valleys, and lords and kings all took the Dao as their root. Thus, speaking of the One in terms of "those of old" refers to the uncarved block of the Dao. Utterly empty, undifferentiated, and perfectly whole, its essence undispersed, its qi returned to the primordial—thus heaven obtained it and was pure and unadulterated, its substance clear. Earth obtained it and was still and unchanging, its substance tranquil. The most recondite spirits obtained it and their numinosity was not obscured. The most empty valleys obtained it and their fullness was inexhaustible. The myriad things obtained it and their essence was whole, their life complete. The lords and kings obtained it and became the norm for all under heaven. All took the Dao as their root—thus their attainment was the same One.

If heaven had nothing by which to be clear, it would fear splitting;
If earth had nothing by which to be tranquil, it would fear bursting;
If the spirits had nothing by which to be numinous, they would fear fading;
If the valleys had nothing by which to be full, they would fear drying up;
If the myriad things had nothing by which to live, they would fear perishing;
If the lords and kings had nothing by which to be the norm and esteemed their high position, they would fear toppling.

The Imperial Commentary states: Heaven’s duty is to cover and give life; earth’s duty is to shape and bear. If it splits, it can no longer cover; if it bursts, it can no longer bear. Spirits rely on man to act—if they fade, they have nothing to show. Valleys receive but do not store—if they dry up, none can respond to them. When essence and qi gather, they become things—this is why they obtain the One and live. When they scatter, their wandering souls transform—this is why they lose the One and perish. Only the norm can govern the myriad changes and stand independent above the myriad things. If one has nothing by which to be the norm yet esteems one’s high position, one will be insufficient to preserve oneself—how could one not topple?

The Minister’s Exegesis states: Heaven is clear through the One—without it, it would split and be unable to broadly cover. Earth is tranquil through the One—without it, it would burst and be unable to deeply bear. Spirits are numinous through the One—without it, they would fade and have nothing to show. Valleys are full through the One—without it, they would dry up and be unable to respond. The myriad things live through the One—without it, they would perish. Lords and kings are the norm through the One—without it, they would topple. Thus, the noble takes the humble as its root; the high takes the low as its foundation.

The Imperial Commentary states: The humble is what the noble relies upon for stability; the low is what the high arises from. The common people see the tip, but the sage probes the root; the common people see the outcome, but the sage examines the subtle. Thus, he always attains the One.

The Minister’s Exegesis states: The noble who forgets the humble courts collapse; the high who forgets the low courts danger. Thus, lords and kings refer to themselves as "the solitary," "the few," and "the unworthy." Is this not taking the humble as their root? Is it not?

The Imperial Commentary states: "Solitary," "few," and "unworthy"—these are names of lowliness, yet lords and kings adopt them as their titles, knowing their root and nothing more. Is this not why lords and kings remain noble and exalted without peril?

The Minister’s Exegesis states: Lords and kings know their root lies in lowliness, so they style themselves "the solitary," "the few," and "the unworthy," never burdening themselves with claims of nobility or loftiness. Thus they preserve their true nobility. Therefore, those who seek frequent praise gain no praise at all.

The Imperial Commentary states: One who exalts himself to surpass others, who vaunts his nobility to belittle others, who clings to strength without knowing to guard with softness, who clings to whiteness without knowing to guard with blackness—such a one seeks praise in the world and attracts excessive acclaim, but praise beyond what is deserved soon draws baseless slander. Thus, he gains no true praise.

The Minister’s Exegesis states: One who knows the One knows the root, and so can humble and lower himself, concealing his utmost nobility. If one exalts and esteems himself, seeking praise repeatedly, how can he escape baseless slander? He does not desire to gleam like jade or stand alone like stone.

The Imperial Commentary states: Jade is prized and stone is base, each fixed and unchanging. The sage rides the moment and responds to things without stagnation; though the myriad changes are ceaseless, his heart remains constant in the One. This is true attainment of the One, and thus he cannot be deemed noble or base. Mencius said: "What is detestable about clinging to the One is that it clings to one while abandoning the hundred." Not to desire to gleam like jade or stand alone like stone—none but the sage who knows transformation can reach this, and this is called supreme virtue.

The Minister’s Exegesis states: Jade and stone are by nature fixed in the One and unchanging. To be one and unable to change is the way of things, not what is meant by attaining the One. One who attains the One bends to respond to the myriad changes, endlessly adapting without stagnation, yet his heart never strays from the One. Who then can deem him noble or base? To be prized like jade or scorned like stone—jade and stone cannot change, so their nobility and baseness are already fixed. This is not what it means to be permeated by the One. Thus, it is said: do not desire this.

Chapter Forty: Reversion is the movement of the Dao
Reversion is the movement of the Dao; weakness is the function of the Dao. The myriad things under heaven are born of being; being is born of non-being.

The Imperial Commentary states: The principles of heaven and earth move and rest in mutual dependence; strength and weakness complement each other. The teeming things, each returning to its root, have already gone forth and reverted to utmost stillness. Yet when stimulated, they penetrate all causes under heaven, and movement becomes none other than the self. Thus it is said: reversion is the movement of the Dao. The soft overcoming the hard, the weak overcoming the strong—here lies the marvelous function of the Dao. Zhuangzi said: "To accumulate many small non-victories into a great victory—only the sage can do this." Thus it is said: weakness is the function of the Dao. The progression of the four seasons withdraws and stores in winter, then flourishes and blooms in spring. Water’s nature is utterly soft, yet in attacking the hard and strong, none can surpass it. Does this not illustrate the point? Being and non-being give rise to each other like a cycle. Thus, non-being moves and generates being; being reaches its limit and returns to non-being, like east and west opposing yet unable to exist without each other. Those deluded by "non-action" or drowned in "some agency"—can this be the Dao?

The Minister’s Exegesis states: The Dao takes emptiness and stillness as its norm, so it regards movement as reversion. Movement departs from stillness yet returns to stillness—this is returning to the root and reverting to the origin, the movement of the Dao. The Dao takes non-contention as its victory, so it takes weakness as its function. The soft overcoming the hard, the weak overcoming the strong—here the marvelous function of the Dao resides. The myriad things under heaven arise from utmost non-being, are born as being, manifest in being, then return to non-being. Non-being in turn generates being—thus being and non-being revolve in mutual generation, unable to exist without each other.

Chapter Forty-One: The Superior Person Hears of the Dao
The superior person hears of the Dao and diligently practices it.

The Imperial Commentary states: The scholar is one who is devoted to the Dao. The superior person hears of the Dao and, through sincere accumulation and enduring effort, practices it with unceasing sincerity.

The Minister’s Exegesis states: The superior person is one whose aspiration for the Dao is steadfast and whose faith in it is profound, thus exerting effort in practice to attain realization.

The middling person hears of the Dao and remains in a state of uncertainty, now present, now absent.

The Imperial Commentary states: The middling person harbors doubt. When doubt arises, the will becomes divided, and in relation to the Dao, one moment departs from it, the next returns to it.

The Minister’s Exegesis states: The middling person’s perception of the Dao is unclear, hence their faith in it is not steadfast.

The inferior person hears of the Dao and laughs greatly at it—without such laughter, it would not be the Dao.

The Imperial Commentary states: The inferior person lacks sufficient faith and wisdom to grasp or comprehend it, thus they laugh.

The Dao possesses no form, color, sound, or flavor that can be apprehended, and thus it lies far beyond the desires of the ears, eyes, nose, and mouth. Zhuangzi said: "Great sounds do not enter the ears of the vulgar; lofty words do not lodge in the hearts of the multitude."

The Minister’s Exegesis states: The inferior person is ensnared by things and turns away from the Dao, mistaking illusory phenomena for reality and regarding the supreme Dao as empty and false. Hearing of the Dao’s greatness, they laugh and do not believe. Yet the Dao is not diminished by the laughter of the inferior person, nor would it be the Dao if it were.

Thus the established saying goes:
The luminous Dao appears obscure.
The Imperial Commentary states: Like the light of the sun and moon illuminating the earth below—this is brightness. Yet the profound wisdom of the origin does not display itself; it conceals its radiance and does not shine forth, thus appearing obscure.

The Minister’s Exegesis states: Bright as the light of the sun and moon, obscure as the darkness of the valley—brightness returns to non-brightness, for it does not employ brightness, thus appearing obscure.

The level Dao appears uneven.
The Imperial Commentary states: Though paths differ, they converge in the same destination; though policies vary, they share a single purpose.

The Minister’s Exegesis states: Levelness is true evenness, yet unevenness seems to halt progress. The Dao takes non-levelness as its levelness, thus appearing flawed.

Advancing in the Dao appears as retreat.
The Imperial Commentary states: Yan Hui advanced by retreating; Zhuangzi spoke of "sitting in forgetfulness."

The Minister’s Exegesis states: The practice of the Dao is called "diminishing," thus it appears as retreat.

Supreme virtue resembles a valley.
The Imperial Commentary states: Empty yet responsive, responding without exhaustion; empty yet receptive, receiving without hoarding. The *Classic* states: "Be a valley to the world, and constant virtue will be sufficient."

The Minister’s Exegesis states: Empty yet all-embracing—this is embodying the Dao, hence it is called supreme virtue. Capable of response and reception, thus it resembles a valley.

Great whiteness appears tarnished.
The Imperial Commentary states: Purifying the profound mirror, not perceiving a single flaw—this is great whiteness. Yet it dwells where the multitude abhors, thus appearing tarnished.

The Minister’s Exegesis states: Great whiteness is unstained by color, pure to the utmost. Appearing tarnished refers to its capacity to receive defilement.

Vast virtue appears insufficient.
The Imperial Commentary states: Virtue embraces all without self-regard for abundance, thus appearing insufficient. When the autumn waters arrive, the River Earl rejoices—yet this is why he is ridiculed by those of great understanding.

The Minister’s Exegesis states: The capacity of virtue is boundless, truly limitless—yet it appears insufficient. This is the ever-expanding nature of virtue, aligning with the inexhaustible Dao.

Established virtue appears indolent.
The Imperial Commentary states: The sage hesitates before acting, yet every endeavor succeeds.

The Minister’s Exegesis states: The sage acts only when unavoidable; when establishing something, they follow the time and yield to spontaneity, appearing indolent and negligent, not hastening in their affairs. Thus the virtue they establish becomes all the greater.

True essence appears altered.
The Imperial Commentary states: Is it not said to be firm? Yet it is ground but not worn thin. Is it not said to be white? Yet it is steeped but not blackened.

The Minister’s Exegesis states: Still and unified, unchanging—pristine in its purity. The essence is preserved in oneness, complete in its truth. Though myriad changes arise, its essence remains ever true—this is the perfection of truth. Yet when truth reaches its utmost, the world does not recognize it as truth, for its concealment runs deep, thus it appears altered.

The great square has no corners.
The Imperial Commentary states: The great square is the square without square. Square yet not cutting, thus it has no corners.

The Minister’s Exegesis states: To speak of a square with corners is to speak of it as a vessel. The great square without corners is the boundless square of the Dao.

The great vessel is slow to complete.
The Imperial Commentary states: The great vessel is the vessel without vessel. It does not hasten growth nor force development, thus it is slow to complete.

The Minister’s Exegesis states: Only the great vessel possesses far-reaching foresight and does not seek swift completion, thus it neither hastens growth nor forces development. To rush toward utility is the way of shallow vessels.

The great tone is rarely heard.
The Imperial Commentary states: Moving without fixed direction, yet responding when stimulated—thus it is rarely heard.

The Minister’s Exegesis states: The great tone is the unheard resonance of virtue, thus its sound is rare.

The great image has no form.
The Imperial Commentary states: Entrusted to the profound and obscure, it cannot be seen, thus it has no form.

The Minister’s Exegesis states: The formless image is the complete embodiment of the Dao, rooted in constant non-being—what form could it possess?

The Dao is hidden and nameless. Only the Dao is good at bestowing and completing.

The Imperial Commentary states: From "the luminous Dao" to "the great image," all speak of the Dao. The marvel of the Dao cannot be sought through intellect nor grasped through form—it may be said to be utterly concealed. Though illuminated, it cannot be obtained. The sage, having attained the Dao, bestows without depletion, responds without exhaustion, and completes the myriad things without ever claiming possession, simply acting and then withdrawing. The essence of the Dao is hidden in namelessness, yet its function excels at bestowing and completing. Thus, when set in motion and practiced, it creates without manifesting and ceases without transformation. Even its secondary effects suffice to establish the achievements of emperors and kings. The *Tradition* states: "Learning begins with being a scholar and ends with being a sage."

The Minister’s Exegesis states: The essence of the Dao returns to utmost non-being. The function of the Dao is concealed in the unfathomable. Illuminating it appears obscure; imaging it yields no form—such is its concealment. Thus it cannot be sought through knowledge nor grasped through form—who could name it?

The act of "bestowing" responds to others' lack, meaning to give and seek its return. The Dao responds without fixed direction, nourishing the myriad things, bringing each to its own completion without end. What beings receive ultimately returns to their root. In this, nothing is lost to the giver, nothing expended by the receiver—hence it is called "excelling at bestowing and completing."

**Chapter 42: The Dao Gives Birth**
The Dao gives birth to the One.
*Imperial Annotation* states: In the great beginning, there was Non-Being-without-non-being, Non-Being-with-name—the arising of the One.
*Minister’s Exegesis* states: The Dao is eternally without right; the One is born from it, the undifferentiated state of qi.

The One gives birth to the Two.
*Imperial Annotation* states: Heaven is one, Earth is two; water follows, then fire, essence is complete, and spirit follows.
*Minister’s Exegesis* states: The One qi divides, and the qi of yin and yang become distinct—thus Heaven and Earth are born.

The Two give birth to the Three.
*Imperial Annotation* states: One and speech make two; two and one make three.
*Minister’s Exegesis* states: Heaven and Earth take their places, and the harmonizing qi arises—thus the Three Powers are complete.

The Three give birth to the myriad things.
*Imperial Annotation* states: Heaven initiates the One in the north, Earth pairs the Two in the south, humanity completes the position of the Three. The Three Powers are complete, and the ten thousand forms divide.
Counting the number of things is called "the ten thousand." Beyond this, even the most skillful reckoning cannot calculate.
*Minister’s Exegesis* states: With yin and yang present, the harmonizing qi brings harmony, and things are born. Numbered as the ten thousand, how could they be counted?

The myriad things bear yin and embrace yang, harmonized by the harmonizing qi.
*Imperial Annotation* states: Yin is still and at rest—all things bear it. Thus the noble person enters at sunset to rest. Yang is fluid and flourishing—all things embrace it. Thus the sage responds to dawn and governs.
There must be the mean between yin and yang—this is the harmonizing qi.
*Zhuangzi* states: Supreme yang blazes brightly; supreme yin is solemn and still. Solemn stillness emerges from Heaven; blazing brightness issues from Earth. The two intermingle and form harmony, and things are born.
*Minister’s Exegesis* states: All things receive form between Heaven and Earth, discarding qi to yin and yang to live—thus none fail to bear and embrace it. The harmony of the harmonizing qi is what engenders life.

What people despise are only "orphaned," "widowed," and "unworthy," yet kings and dukes take these as their titles.
*Imperial Annotation* states: Things do not rise without falling, affairs do not flourish without declining—this is the movement of yin and yang, the principle of things. Waxing and waning, fullness and emptiness proceed together without losing harmony—is this not the sage alone?
Thus, "orphaned," "widowed," and "unworthy"—what people despise—kings and dukes take as their titles. Having reached the extreme, they return; having become full, they diminish. This is how they remain above without peril.
*Minister’s Exegesis* states: Lords and kings occupy positions of noble eminence, yet "orphaned," "widowed," and "unworthy" are what people despise. Kings and dukes, though in exalted positions, adopt what people despise, knowing the principle of fullness and diminution and thus maintaining the mean without loss.
Yin at its extreme generates yang; yang at its extreme generates yin. Losing balance in partiality, one must return to the harmony of yin and yang—thus the principle of diminution and augmentation is made clear.
Therefore, some things are diminished yet augmented, augmented yet diminished.
*Imperial Annotation* states: When leaves fall, they fertilize the roots—this is diminution that augments. When the moon waxes to fullness, it must wane—this is augmentation that diminishes. Heaven and Earth wax and wane with the seasons—how much more so for humans?
Thus, what kings and dukes adopt is precisely the way to attain augmentation while occupying noble eminence.
*Minister’s Exegesis* states: Augmentation leads to diminution; diminution leads to augmentation. The waxing and waning of Heaven and Earth, the advance and retreat of yin and yang—this principle is never otherwise. Kings and dukes adopt what people despise, attaining the Way of diminution and augmentation, moving with the seasons. This is how they occupy high and noble positions without stumbling.

What others teach, I also take as my teaching. "The strong and overbearing do not meet their proper end"—I take this as the father of teachings.

**Imperial Commentary** states: To subdue the weak with strength, to overcome the soft with hardness—this is what others teach. What I teach differs from this. The strong and overbearing cling to the self and delight in contention; this is the way to death. The wise observe this and take it as a warning, thus making it the father of teachings.

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: Some things, when diminished, are augmented; when augmented, are diminished. Yin and yang advance and retreat—where there is advance, there is retreat; where there is retreat, there is advance. The transformations of things, the waxing and waning of yin and yang—such is their inherent principle. Where principle resides, there lies the teacher of right teaching. To subdue the weak with strength, to overcome the soft with hardness—this is what others teach. The strong and overbearing delight in contention, for they are strong in their attachment to the self. Believing others cannot surpass them, they rely on force and never reflect. Thus, they are ultimately overcome by the strong and do not meet a good end. Those who understand this principle take the waxing and waning, the augmentation and diminution of others as a mirror, making it the foundation of teaching—this is the way of the father.

**Forty-Third Chapter: The Softest Under Heaven**
The softest under heaven gallops through the hardest under heaven.

**Imperial Commentary** states: What is hard will break; what is sharp will be blunted. Only the sage can accumulate countless small victories to achieve great triumph.

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: The rigid cannot harmonize with things and is easily broken, thus destroyed by hardness and blunted by sharpness. The supremely soft does not oppose things, thus it can enter them and act, making even the hardest serve its purpose, galloping without awareness.

*"Non-being enters where there is no gap."*

**Imperial Commentary** states: The *Zhuangzi*’s Outer Chapters discuss how the one-legged *kui*, the snake, the wind, and the eye each cherish one another, concluding with the eye cherishing the heart. For the feet’s movement has limits, the eye’s vision has bounds, but only spirit is without direction. The Inner Chapters discuss the master of nurturing life, and how much more so the butcher’s dissection of the ox. *Ding* (丁) is the yin of fire and the minister of spirit, thus his blade moves with effortless abundance. Yet to enter where there is no gap—who but one whose body is utterly without limits and whose movement leaves no trace could achieve this?

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: "Non-being" means both self and things are absent. When self and things are absent, body and spirit merge with the Dao, thus attaining marvelous freedom without direction, roaming without trace. From this, we know the benefit of non-action.

**Imperial Commentary** states: The soft overcoming the hard, the absence embracing presence—such is the marvelous function of the Dao, truly embodied here. Abandoning affairs, the body is not wearied; forgetting life, essence is not depleted—this is how it is beneficial.

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: The Classic says: "The heavenly vessel cannot be acted upon; those who act upon it ruin it." The marvelous function of the Dao lies in the unfathomable—how could it involve deliberate action? Those who act wearily shape themselves with affairs, depleting essence and harming life—what benefit is there in this? The teaching without words, the benefit of non-action—few under heaven attain these.

**Imperial Commentary** states: The teaching without words is established through spirit; the benefit of non-action does not deplete one’s true nature. The sage embraces simplicity with this, and all under heaven submit. Without acting, the myriad things are transformed—thus few attain this.

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: Embodying spirit, their teaching is wordless; exhausting the Dao, their benefit is non-action. That all under heaven submit and the myriad things are transformed—truly few attain this.

**Forty-Fourth Chapter: Fame and the Self**
Fame and the self—which is closer? The self and wealth—which is greater?

**Imperial Commentary** states: If one’s arms are heavier than all under heaven, then which is closer—fame or the self? Life is not merely as precious as a *Sui* pearl—then which is greater, the self or wealth? The highest aspiration lies within me; fame is not what I cherish. The greatest abundance lies within me; wealth is not what I value. Only those who do not discern the distinction between closeness and distance, between more and less, harm their lives and injure their natures, sacrificing themselves. Like Boyi, who died for fame beneath Mount Shouyang, or Robber Zhi, who died for profit on Eastern Ridge—are they not deluded? Understanding the essence of life and not pursuing what is unnecessary for it—this is how those who possess the Dao differ from the vulgar.

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: Those who lose the principle of life and nature, entangled in the realm of fame and profit, and do not return—do not know the distinction between closeness and distance, between more and less. The self is within me—it is the source of utmost nobility and wealth, self-sufficient and requiring nothing from without. Fame and wealth are external—mere transient lodgings of things. To lose what is within me and pursue what is external, harming life and injuring nature—how foolish! Moreover, the self is closer than fame; if the self can be forgotten, how can fame be clung to? The self is greater than wealth; if the self is not possessed, how can wealth be a burden? Thus, which is closer and which is greater—those who possess the Dao do not permit such questions.

Gain and loss—which is more harmful?

**Imperial Commentary** states: Knights sacrifice themselves for fame; the greedy sacrifice themselves for profit. What they gain is fame and wealth, but what they lose is the uncarved simplicity of namelessness and the priceless vessel of the body—what harm could be greater?

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: What is gained from things lies without; what is lost from the self lies within. Reflecting inwardly, one轻 external things and avoids the suffering of pursuit and loss. Insatiable greed leads to essence being depleted and life not preserved, the body wearied and the spirit not released—this harm is extreme.

Thus, excessive love must lead to great expenditure; excessive hoarding must lead to heavy loss.

**Imperial Commentary** states: Without longing for externals, one’s harmony is not spent. Without being burdened by things, one’s hold is not lost. The balance between taking and giving, the alternation between accumulation and dispersal—this outcome is certain, like a cycle. How can it last long?

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: Taking and giving, accumulation and dispersal—this is the inevitable principle. Yet if one is attached to things, the heart is enslaved and the spirit is spent. If one hoards things, the spirit is wearied and life cannot be enriched.

To know contentment is to avoid disgrace.
**Imperial Commentary** states: Abiding within the measure of restraint, what disgrace could there be?
**Minister’s Exegesis** states: Content with what one attains of oneself, knowing what is inherently noble—what disgrace could there be?

To know when to stop is to avoid peril.
**Imperial Commentary** states: Roaming where the myriad things begin and end, thus free from the calamity of peril.
**Minister’s Exegesis** states: Knowing to stop within the bounds of one’s nature, making no demands beyond—thus free from worldly troubles.

This is how one may endure long.
**Imperial Commentary** states: Things have their gathering and dispersal, but nature knows no past or present. The people of the world exchange their nature for things, thus pursuing fame and chasing profit, only to suffer disgrace and endanger themselves. The sage fulfills their nature and is content. The world is vast indeed, yet they do not harm their life thereby—thus they may endure long and stand alongside heaven and earth.
**Minister’s Exegesis** states: To know contentment is to return the heart to emptiness; to know when to stop is to unite the spirit with stillness. Emptiness gathers the Dao; stillness restores the Dao—thus one may endure long.

*Explication of the Dao and Its Virtue by Emperor Huizong of Song, Scroll Six, Completed*
*Composed by Court Gentleman for Promotion, Minister Zhang An*

**The Virtue Classic, Chapter Forty-Five: "Great Perfection Seems Deficient"**

Great perfection seems deficient, yet its use is inexhaustible.

**Imperial Commentary** states: Within the realm, there are four great things, and the Dao occupies one of them. To embody the Dao in its entirety is thus called "great." Without completion or loss, this is called "great perfection." Not clinging to its completion, it therefore seems deficient. Knowing transformation and harmonizing with change, without clinging to the old, its use is thus inexhaustible. This is how Confucius, in gathering great perfection, became a sage for all times.

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: "Great perfection" refers to the Dao. Who can perceive the Dao’s great perfection in its entirety? Thus it seems deficient. "Seems deficient" means its completion is not apparent. Though complete, its means of completion are unseen, so its use is inexhaustible.

Great fullness seems empty, yet its use is unending.

**Imperial Commentary** states: Filling all without bound, sufficing the ten thousand things—this is "great fullness." Responding to things with emptiness, using them with moderation, its application is thus inexhaustible, its use unending. A skilled merchant conceals his wealth as if empty, his demeanor as if dull.

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: "Great fullness" refers to virtue. When virtue is greatly full, its use is humble, as if empty. Being empty, its use is unending. For one who possesses virtue and dwells in fullness will find it exhausted.

Great straightness seems bent.

**Imperial Commentary** states: Adapting to the changes of things, yielding in curves and straightness, without seeking to impose itself, it thus seems bent.

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: Bending to respond to change, flowing without abiding, not contending with things for straightness—yet things do not perceive its straightness, though it never loses it.

Great skill seems clumsy.

**Imperial Commentary** states: Bestowing forms upon things, whether round or square, curved or straight, its marvel is unseen, thus it seems clumsy.

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: Carving the myriad forms without deliberate action—acting without acting, yet skill is realized in things. This is great skill. Its means of accomplishment unseen, its marvel unperceived, it thus seems clumsy.

Great eloquence seems inarticulate.

**Imperial Commentary** states: Eloquence without words is called "great eloquence." Hui Shi’s many arguments were but petty eloquence.

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: Words clarify the Dao, yet it is better to not speak and let the Dao clarify itself.

Restlessness overcomes cold; stillness overcomes heat. Clarity and stillness govern the world rightly.

**Imperial Commentary** states: Yang moves and is restless, thus it overcomes cold. Yin abides and is still, thus it overcomes heat. These two incline toward yin and yang without attaining the mean, now confounding things, now contending with movement—how can they rightly govern the world? Only without the restlessness that overcomes cold and the stillness that overcomes heat can one be unadulterated and clear, embracing the spirit in stillness, so the world will right itself.

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: Restlessness generates inner heat, and with inner heat, even cold can be overcome. Stillness brings clarity of heart, and with a clear heart, even heat can be overcome. Cold and heat are the qi of yin and yang, yet when the heart’s restlessness and stillness prevail, cold and heat become imperceptible—how then can one govern rightly? When qi returns to oneness, it is clear and unadulterated; when spirit embraces oneness, it is still and unconfused. Only clarity and stillness, attaining oneness, can rightly govern the world.

**Chapter Forty-Six: "When the World Follows the Dao"**

When the world follows the Dao, warhorses are retired to fertilize the fields.

**Imperial Commentary** states: Those who govern the world with the Dao allow the people to each delight in their work without contention, tending only to their fields.

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: With the Dao, there are no desires; without desires, there is no contention. Thus warhorses are retired, the people attend to fundamentals and delight in their work, not shunning even the humblest labor.

When the world lacks the Dao, warhorses breed on the outskirts.

**Imperial Commentary** states: The strong oppress the weak, the many tyrannize the few—even borders cannot be rightly maintained.

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: When warhorses breed on the outskirts, fields lie fallow and the five grains go to waste. The weak and few are oppressed by the strong and many, so borders cannot be rightly maintained. Thus the people lose their place—this is evident.

No sin is greater than indulging desire.

**Imperial Commentary** states: Not seeing what can be desired keeps the heart from disorder. When people have desires, they go so far as to harm their nature and life to contend for them—this is the origin of sin.

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: Without what can be desired, the heart forgets. With what can be desired, contention begins. Desiring what is permissible leads to strife when desires are unmet. Strife brings chaos—no sin is greater.

No disaster is greater than not knowing contentment.

**Imperial Commentary** states: Levelness is fortune; excess is disaster. Knowing contentment brings no disgrace—what disaster could there be?

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: Not knowing contentment makes desires boundless, so disaster becomes inescapable.

No fault is greater than desiring to obtain.

**Imperial Commentary** states: Obtaining what one desires invites the censure of others.

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: Obtaining what one desires leads each person to seek gain, incurring fault with the multitude.

Thus, knowing contentment’s contentment is ever content.

**Imperial Commentary** states: Seeing what can be desired, people do not know contentment; not knowing contentment, they desire to obtain; desiring to obtain, contention arises and disaster follows. When excess reaches its limit, warhorses breed on the outskirts. Yet knowing contentment, each rests in their allotted nature and life, without employing their cunning. Daily needs—food and drink—what contention or chaos could there be?

**Minister’s Exegesis** states: Boundless seeking cannot be satisfied even by heaven and earth. Knowing contentment within one’s nature and lot leaves nothing lacking, ever self-sufficient.

**Chapter Forty-Seven: "Without Leaving the Door"**

Without leaving the door, one knows the world. Without looking out the window, one sees the Dao of heaven.

**Imperial Commentary** states: Though the world is vast, the sage knows it through wisdom. Though the Dao of heaven is distant, the sage sees it through the heart. Wisdom encompasses the ten thousand things, nothing is too remote to perceive—thus there is no need to leave the door. The heart abides in spirit and illumination, nothing is too obscure to illumine—thus there is no need to look out the window. Zhuangzi said: "In the time it takes to lower and raise one’s head, one may caress the lands beyond the four seas." This is how the sage subtly moves and transforms alone.

**Minister Yi states:** To know by stepping out the door is merely to know what can be known. To see by peering through the window is merely to see what can be seen. The vastness of the world and the boundlessness of the Dao of heaven—how can one know through knowing or see through seeing? Stepping out the door, one departs from this and gains some knowledge, yet how much can that knowledge encompass? Peering through the window, one turns toward that and gains some sight, yet how far can that sight reach? The sage subtly moves and transforms alone, not knowing through knowing—thus there is nothing unknown. Not seeing through seeing—thus there is nothing unseen. The world is where the myriad things reside, made so by their nature. The Dao of heaven is where the Dao abides, made so by its spontaneity. Its coarse aspect lies in things, its wondrous aspect lies in the Dao. Both are never apart from their immediate reality, yet exhaust all that can be known and seen—what need is there to step out, what reliance on peering?

The farther one goes, the less one knows. **The Imperial Commentary asks:** Does this not return to the heart of heaven and earth? When one draws near and takes from one’s own body, the ten thousand things are all complete. Seek it in yin and yang, seek it in measures and numbers, yet the farther one strays from the Dao, the less one knows.

**Minister Yi states:** To step out is to depart from stillness and move toward activity, growing ever more distant—thus one cannot return to the origin. The farther one goes, the less one knows; this is the principle. Therefore, the sage knows without moving, names without seeing, and accomplishes without acting.

**The Imperial Commentary states:** To know the world through one’s own wisdom is called knowing without moving. To see the Dao of heaven through one’s own heart is called naming without seeing. Knowing without moving, naming without seeing—what is there to do? Yet their success is towering; this is called accomplishing without acting.

**Minister Yi states:** Never departing from the immediate reality, one exhausts all that can be known and seen—what need is there to step out the door or peer through the window? Stepping out entails movement; peering entails seeing. The sage does not move but relies on wisdom, does not see but relies on the heart. Thus, the affairs of the world can all be known, and the wonders of the Dao of heaven can all be named. Able to know and able to name, one thus acts without acting, accomplishing what is self-accomplished.

**Chapter Forty-Eight: "Increasing by Learning"**

In learning, one daily increases.

**The Imperial Commentary states:** Learning is for attaining the Dao. One begins as a scholar and ends as a sage. Daily, one adds to oneself, and the Dao accumulates within until the body is sated. Confucius said of Yan Yuan, "I see his progress."

**Minister Yi states:** Learning is for seeking extensive knowledge; extensive knowledge is for exhausting principle. Thus, progress and increase are essential.

In cultivating the Dao, one daily diminishes.

**The Imperial Commentary states:** Those who attain the Dao cast off their limbs and body, dismiss their hearing and vision, depart from form and abandon wisdom, until all affairs dissolve into oblivion—thus it is called daily diminishing. This is how Shan Bao-yu, at sixty, underwent sixty transformations.

**Minister Yi states:** Cultivating the Dao is for attaining emptiness; attaining emptiness is for fully realizing one’s nature. Thus, daily diminishing is essential.

Diminish and further diminish, until one reaches non-action. Through non-action, there is nothing that is not done.

**The Imperial Commentary states:** Learning exhausts principle to encompass being; the Dao fully realizes nature to reach non-being. Diminish and further diminish, until there is not yet anything. That which is not yet anything and has no non-being—this is non-action. Serene and unmoving, yet nothing is left undone. Moved, one penetrates the causes of all under heaven. Through stillness, one becomes a sage; through movement, one becomes a king.

**Minister Yi states:** Fully realizing one’s nature is to reach utmost emptiness, where self and things are forgotten. Forgetting what is to be forgotten, one’s destiny returns to the Great Beginning. Thus, through non-action, one returns to the essence of the Dao; through nothing left undone, one exhausts the function of the Dao. Therefore, in stillness, one is a sage; in movement, one is a king.

To take the world, one always does so through non-affair. When there is affair, it is insufficient to take the world.

**The Imperial Commentary states:** The world is a great thing. Those who possess this great thing cannot be ensnared by things, yet can master things. Thus, those who take the world always do so through non-affair. The world is a divine vessel, not to be acted upon. Those who act upon it ruin it; those who grasp it lose it. Thus, when there is affair, it is insufficient to take the world. The sage embodies the Dao and governs the self with its truth. When emperors rise and kings emerge, they respond by chance, and the world submits of its own accord. This is how King Tai of the Zhou left Bin and established his state beneath Mount Qi.

**Minister Yi states:** The world is where the wonders of the Dao reside, where divine transformations occur—thus it is called a great thing, a divine vessel. Only those with the Dao are where the world returns, yet they never treat affairs as affairs. Without affair, they act, yet return to non-action—thus the world is governed by me. When there is affair, one seeks to take the world by force, yet ultimately meets with ruin.

**Chapter Forty-Nine: "The Sage Has No Constant Heart"**

The sage has no constant heart; the hearts of the common people become his heart.

**The Imperial Commentary states:** The sage’s heart is the mirror of the ten thousand things. Empty, it can receive; still, it can respond. Like a mirror reflecting forms, it reveals their beauty or ugliness. Like an echo answering a sound, it matches its greatness or smallness. What constancy could there be? Observing the myriad things, he knows their feelings and follows the people—this is called making the hearts of the common people his heart. *Zhuangzi* said: "The lowly, yet one cannot but follow—they are the people."

**Minister Yi states:** The sage takes non-heart as his heart, thus he adapts to the times and responds to change—how could there be constancy? The hearts of the common people vary with the times. The sage follows them, making their time his heart. Thus, like a mirror reflecting forms, he neither advances nor retreats, revealing their beauty or ugliness. Like an echo answering a sound, he neither amplifies nor suppresses, matching their greatness or smallness—what need is there for constancy?

Those who are good, I treat as good; those who are not good, I also treat as good—thus virtue is goodness. Those who are trustworthy, I trust; those who are not trustworthy, I also trust—thus virtue is trustworthiness.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: Good and bad oppose each other, truth and deceit deceive one another—such are the sentiments of the vulgar world, where people create distinctions of sameness and difference. How can this be virtue? When virtue is goodness, one sees all actions as nothing but good, thus even the not-good are treated as good. When virtue is trustworthiness, one sees all dispositions as nothing but trustworthy, thus even the untrustworthy are trusted. Forgetting both truth and falsehood, unifying right and wrong—this is called the person of complete virtue. This is how Shun, in his dealings with Xiang, was sincere and joyful toward him.

**Minister Yi states:** The sage, in relation to others, is without ungoodness and without untrustworthiness. Thus, people follow goodness and follow trustworthiness, daring not to transgress. For good and evil, trust and deceit all arise from deluded emotions and false views, privately creating distinctions of sameness and difference. When right and wrong are blended into one, there is no good and no not-good. When trust and deceit are mystically united, there is no trust and no untrustworthiness. Treating the not-good as good and the untrustworthy as trustworthy, one discards no one. Thus, the not-good will themselves become good, and the untrustworthy will themselves become trustworthy—hence it is called "virtue as goodness" and "virtue as trustworthiness."

The sage’s presence in the world is marked by trepidation, blending his heart with the world. The *Imperial Commentary* says: While he is in the world, fortune and misfortune are shared with the people. Though he has no constant heart, he cannot but be vigilant. Thus, in what he does for himself, he is ever cautious and never at ease. In what he does for the world, he equalizes good and bad, unites trust and deceit, forgets both, and closes off what he might praise—merely blending all into one.

**Minister Yi states:** "Trepidation" describes a state of fear and caution. Though the sage has no constant heart, with the Dao in the world, when he responds to the age, he is never without trepidation as a warning. For he acts with purpose, not daring to take it lightly—thus sharing the people’s concerns. In acting for the world, his heart is blended into oneness, seeking to return to simplicity and restore inherent nature, uniting with the primordial beginning.

The people all fix their ears and eyes upon him; the sage treats them all as children. The *Imperial Commentary* says: Heaven sees through the eyes of the people, Heaven hears through the ears of the people. Thus, the sage takes the people’s hearts as his own. When the sage acts, the myriad things behold him, so the people all fix their ears and eyes upon him. If the people rely on the sage’s seeing and hearing, then the sage is the people’s parent. With compassion and care, he shelters them as if protecting an infant, and his benevolence covers the world.

**Minister Yi states:** The sage blends the world into oneness with the Dao, his heart without any differing heart. Thus, the people, in relation to their ruler, fix their ears and eyes to perceive and be transformed by the Dao. The sage regards them as children, for their hearts are unified, free from the affairs of cunning and artifice—thus returning to simplicity.

**Chapter Fifty: Emerging into Life, Submerging into Death**

Emerging into life, submerging into death. The *Imperial Commentary* says: All things emerge from the mechanism, all things submerge into the mechanism. The heavenly mechanism unfolds of itself, arising together with life. The heavenly mechanism ceases of itself, perishing together with death. Life is the beginning of creation’s workings; death is the transformation of yin and yang.

**Minister Yi states:** The life and death of the myriad things emerge and submerge within the transformative mechanism of yin and yang, unable to stop themselves. Emerging as yang, thus they live; submerging as yin, thus they die. What is born here dies there; what dies here is born there. Inherent nature has no life or death, yet it has existed since ancient times. The spirit emerges and submerges, while the form has life and death—hence it is called "emerging and submerging into life and death."

Those who follow life are thirteen; those who follow death are thirteen. The *Imperial Commentary* says: Those who accompany life and death, emerging and submerging within the mechanism of life and death, are not yet free from entanglement.

**Minister Yi states:** Emerging and submerging within the mechanism of life and death, they live together with life and die together with death, never parting from it—hence they are called "followers." The thirteen refer to the six that are carved into form and the seven that cling to the po. Emerging from form and life, sinking into dark yin, they all accompany it as followers.

The people’s lives, in their movements, reach the realm of death—also thirteen. The *Imperial Commentary* says: Clinging to life while turning from principle, forgetting life while pursuing profit—such are the lives of the common people. In their movements, they reach the realm of death. How do their lives differ from death?

**Minister Yi states:** Having form, they have life; having life, they pursue externals and turn from principle, losing their inherent nature to the point that all their movements reach the realm of death, unable to preserve life. It is also through the desires of the ears, eyes, mouth, nose, limbs, and the mind’s knowledge that they are lured into the realm of death without realizing it. Though they may briefly secure their lives, how is this different from death? Why is this so? Because of their excessive attachment to life.

The *Imperial Commentary* says: Those who follow life delight in life yet burden their forms; those who follow death hasten toward stillness and forget their bodies. Those who move toward the realm of death are like the linked cangues of prisoners stretching endlessly, like the executed corpses in view of one another—all unaware that the body is the great affliction, that life has its limits, and that their preservation of life is excessively thick. The ancients who attained the Dao did not use wealth and honor to nourish what harms the body, nor did they use poverty and low status to burden the form with profit. They did not rejoice in long life nor grieve over early death. With dawn-like clarity, they perceived the solitary, thus transcending past and present to enter the realm of neither death nor life.

**Minister Yi states:** Those who know life can be joyful and death can be grieved are mired in form, taking life and death as burdens. Those who know that life and death have never truly existed are immersed in unity, not taking life and death as burdens. Precisely because they take life and death as burdens, they delight in life and fear death. Because of this delight, they preserve life excessively, thereby harming life—never realizing that life has its limits.

It is said that those skilled in nurturing life, when traveling on land, do not encounter rhinoceroses or tigers; when entering battle, they are not struck by armor or weapons. The rhinoceros has nowhere to thrust its horn, the tiger has nowhere to place its claws, and weapons have nowhere to lodge their blades. Why is this so?

**For they abide in no realm of death.**

**The Imperial Commentary states:** Those skilled in nurturing life preserve their form in wholeness and restore their essence, uniting with Heaven as one. Their celestial guard remains intact, their spirit never retreats. They move unseen without obstruction, tread fire without scorching, walk above the myriad things yet feel no dread. Thus, though they encounter things, they are not daunted—nothing can harm them. *The Book of Changes* says: *"One who penetrates the Dao of day and night, who enters and exits the mechanism of life and death—nothing is not thus."* Yet those who comprehend the discourse on life and death, who transcend and penetrate the very source of things—are they not the perfected ones?

**Minister Yi states:** Where there is life, there is death. For qi gives rise to essence, essence gives rise to spirit, and spirit animates form. When spirit departs, form dissolves—this is the domain of life and death. But those skilled in nurturing life restore qi to its origin, preserve essence in unity, and refine spirit to the marvelous void, merging with the Dao in body. Thus, in their living, Heaven moves while they have never been born. In their departure, they feel no joy; in their death, things transform while they have never changed. In their return, they offer no refusal. Above, they wander with the Creator in the undivided wholeness of qi. They regard life as an excrescent growth, death as the bursting of a boil—then who would cling to form, who would cling to life? Only by forgetting life do they abide in no realm of death. Abiding in no realm of death, what do they fear of what others dread? Nothing can burden them, nothing can harm them.

**Chapter Fifty: "The Dao Gives Them Life"**—
*The Dao gives them life...*

The *Yuzhu* Annotation states: The Dao is ever non-active yet accomplishes all; the myriad things fulfill their offices, all growing from non-action.

Minister Yi says: The Dao returns to perfect nothingness, yet the host of beings depend on it to live.

It nurtures them with virtue—
The *Yuzhu* Annotation states: That by which things attain life is called virtue.
Minister Yi says: Things are born of the Dao, each receiving from the Dao, thus they are reared in virtue.

It shapes them—
The *Yuzhu* Annotation states: It retains movement to generate things; when things take form and embody principle, this is called shaping.
Minister Yi says: The myriad things assume myriad forms, each possessing its own attainment.

It completes them through configuration—
The *Yuzhu* Annotation states: Once form and substance are fully arrayed, their bodily configuration is thus achieved—the mutual measuring of long and short, the mutual inclination of high and low—such is the nature of their configuration.
Minister Yi says: They mutually engender and mutually perfect, mutually shape and mutually incline; thus their configuration is completed.

Therefore, the myriad things none fail to revere the Dao and honor virtue.
The *Yuzhu* Annotation states: That to which the myriad things all turn their heads is the Dao. That which completes and elevates them is virtue. Reverence enables them to overcome things, while what is honored by the humble cannot be despised by them. Mencius said: "The nobility of Zhao Meng can debase what he has ennobled"—for it is not virtue.
Minister Yi says: The Dao is the origin of Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things, hence it is called revered. Reverence implies honor beyond measure, thus the Dao is spoken of as revered. Virtue follows the Dao, yet things cannot debase it, hence virtue is spoken of as honored.

The reverence of the Dao, the honor of virtue—none confer rank upon them, yet they are ever naturally so.
The *Yuzhu* Annotation states: Things may decay in time, configurations may incline in time, but the true sovereign transcends the world. True honor resides within, not borrowed from external configurations, ever abiding in self-possession.
Minister Yi says: The Dao governs the myriad things yet is not governed by them, hence it is revered. Virtue follows the Dao, and no thing can precede it, hence it is honored. The Dao does not revere itself—it is what the myriad things revere. Virtue does not honor itself—it is what the myriad things honor. Their reverence and honor are not sought yet ever naturally so.

Thus the Dao gives them life, rears them, nurtures them, matures them, ripens them, sustains them, and shelters them.
The *Yuzhu* Annotation states: Spoken separately, there are distinctions between Dao, virtue, configuration, and things; spoken together, all issue from the Dao. The Dao is the mystery of the myriad things: when things transform and arise, the Dao is born with them; when things withdraw and are stored, the Dao is completed with them. It issues from *Zhen*, is completed in *Gen*, is nurtured in *Kun*, and shelters in *Qian*. Hard and soft rub against each other, the eight trigrams surge together—like a mechanism that cannot cease, the Dao truly enfolds them.
Minister Yi says: That virtue rears, that things take shape, that configurations are completed—all issue from the Dao. The Dao enfolds the myriad things, serving as their mystery. Thus, in their issuing and entering, their living and completing, their transforming and sheltering—what lies beyond the Dao?

It gives life yet does not possess, acts yet does not rely, leads yet does not dominate—this is called profound virtue.
The *Yuzhu* Annotation states: In giving life, it manifests in movement and emergence; in acting, it effects through transformation; in leading, it appears in unity and governance. The Dao descends, yet what resides in virtue is this: giving life yet not possessing its achievement, acting yet not relying on its ability, leading yet not displaying its art of control—who but the subtlest of virtues could attain this? Hence it is called profound virtue.
Minister Yi says: There is that which is given life, that which is acted upon, that which is led—these are manifestations of virtue apart from the Dao. Only because it can give life yet does not possess its achievement, can act yet does not rely on its ability, can lead yet leaves no trace of its governance—hence it is the profundity of virtue.

**Chapter Fifty-Two: "The World Has a Beginning"**
The world has a beginning, which serves as the mother of the world.
The *Yuzhu* Annotation states: The nameless is the beginning of Heaven and Earth; the named is the mother of the myriad things. Beginning and mother are both the Dao. From the inception of qi, it is called beginning; from its ceaseless generation, it is called mother. Having a beginning, it can generate without end.
Minister Yi says: The world has a beginning—this is simply the One of the Dao, not the beginning of Heaven and Earth. It is what is called the named mother of the myriad things. The One is the beginning of the named, hence it is the mother of the world. The inception of qi is the One. That from which things are self-generated has the Dao of the mother.

Having attained its mother, one knows her children.
The *Yuzhu* Annotation states: The Dao can mother the myriad things and nurture them, thus things are its children. Those who penetrate the Dao embrace things, and things thus attain their mother to know their children.
Minister Yi says: The arising of the One is how the Dao generates things. Having attained the mother, one knows the myriad things as her children.

Having known her children, one returns to guard their mother; thus one’s body does not perish.
The *Yuzhu* Annotation states: Hearing much, one guards it with simplicity; seeing much, one guards it with transcendence. Exhausting the principles of things yet not burdened by them, attaining the subtleties of the Dao yet not losing its wonder—then one’s utility enters and exits without exhaustion, coming and going without end. One can preserve life, fulfill one’s years, and suffer no peril of decay.
Minister Yi says: Zhuangzi said: "Form does not arise without the Dao," thus the myriad things originate in the Dao, born of the One. Knowing that the myriad things are all mothered by the One, one cannot but guard the One. Guarding the One, though the body may be cast aside, the spirit does not perish.

Block its openings, close its gates—thus one labors not to life’s end.
The *Yuzhu* Annotation states: *Dui* speaks of delight; *men* speaks of exit. Things entice from without, and the heart delights within. Ears, eyes, nose, and mouth—from these the spirit and brightness issue forth. Be cautious within, close off without; do not take communion with things as joy. If things cannot draw one in, then one delights in Heaven and attains self-possession—who would toil toilsomely for things?
Minister Yi says: *Dui* is delight in externals, so the heart has somewhere to go.

When the gates are opened, the spirit turns toward movement. When its *dui* is blocked, the heart attains unity. When its gates are closed, the spirit attains stillness. With the heart unified and the spirit stilled, this is how one guards the Mother. Guarding the Mother, one returns to the Dao and finds self-possession in all directions—what toil could there be?

Opening its *dui* and aiding its affairs, one remains beyond salvation to life’s end.
The *Yuzhu* Annotation states: Deluded by pleasing appearances, one engages with them in contention, pursuing tasks and clashing with things, toiling endlessly without ever seeing success.
Minister Yi says: When the heart delights in things, it chases illusions and pursues ends, going forth without return, exhausting its spirit in use, laboring to the point of depletion in affairs to seek their completion—what folly could be greater? In this way, one loses essence and forfeits numinosity—how could it be saved?

To see the small is called illumination.
The *Yuzhu* Annotation states: The small is the subtlety of the Dao. One who perceives the subtlety of the Dao knows oneself and thus lacks nothing in clarity.
Minister Yi says: Though "small" and "uncarved" are alike in smallness, to see it is called illumination, for it is self-knowledge.

To guard the soft is called strength.
The *Yuzhu* Annotation states: The soft is the root of the Dao. One who guards the root of the Dao overcomes oneself and thus overcomes all.
Minister Yi says: Knowing to guard the soft, one knows how to overcome oneself.

Use its light, return to its brightness.
The *Yuzhu* Annotation states: Brightness is the essence of light; light is the function of brightness. The sage’s response to the world arises from essence and manifests as function, scattering radiance as light. Drawing function back to essence, wisdom becomes penetrating clarity. Displaying it in transformation, concealing it in function—like the sun and moon illuminating all things without ever diminishing—this is how one spiritually embodies virtue.
Minister Yi says: Light is brightness manifest externally. The sage reveals the Seven to engage the world—this is the function of light. Concealing function to return to the Dao is the return of brightness.

To leave no calamity upon the self is called following constancy.
The *Yuzhu* Annotation states: The transformations of things are without constancy. Only one who returns to life leaves things and departs from people, returning to brightness without being transformed along with things. Thus one embodies constancy and knows no harm, unlike those who, like fireflies, glow briefly with form.
Minister Yi says: To use one’s light without returning is to regard oneself as substantial while opposing the endlessness of things. When the use of light is exhausted, things become calamities for the self—this is to bring calamity upon oneself. Only by returning to brightness and reverting to the uncarved does one embody constancy without being transformed by things—this is called following constancy. The wondrousness of true constancy can be obscurely unified but cannot be overtly grasped, hence it is called "following."

**Chapter Fifty-Three**
Were I to possess even slight knowledge, I would walk the great Dao and fear only deviation.
The *Yuzhu* Annotation states: The Dao rejects extravagance and excess. Extravagance is indulgence in virtue; excess is profligacy in nature—both are deviations of application.
