One who is slight yet discerning of things, knowing inwardly, deepens roots and strengthens foundations, drawing sufficiency from the self—thus fearing only deviation.
Minister Yi says: One who is wholehearted may attain the Dao. To forget the heart is to be without fear. Possessing even slight knowledge and walking the great Dao is to have the heart set upon the Dao—thus one cannot be without fear of action.

The great Dao is utterly level, yet the people prefer byways.
The *Yuzhu* Annotation states: The Dao is level, while byways are swift. Seeking speed to hasten near-term results, one strays ever farther from the Dao.
Minister Yi says: The Dao is easy to know and easy to practice, without difficulty—like a great road, utterly level. Yet the people prefer byways, employing cleverness and seeking shortcuts to pursue quick gains, thus losing the Dao.

The court is excessively adorned, the fields lie fallow, the granaries are empty.
The *Yuzhu* Annotation states: Exalting the worthy and employing the capable to bring order to the court, yet failing to devote effort to agriculture and accumulate resources to foster a prosperous populace—this is to pursue ends while abandoning roots, an unsustainable path.
Minister Yi says: When the court is excessively adorned, external embellishment is pursued; when fields lie fallow and granaries are empty, the root is neglected. To adorn the external while abandoning the root is no enduring way.

Adorned in brocade, girded with sharp swords, sated with food and drink, possessing surplus wealth—this is called thievish ostentation, not the Dao!
The *Yuzhu* Annotation states: Those who act within the contract of life move without seeking fame; those who act beyond it are intent on lavish expenditure. Moving without seeking fame, one fears only deviation. Intent on lavish expenditure, one adorns in brocade, girds with sharp swords, sates with food and drink, and takes surplus wealth as glory rather than shame—clinging to excess, destroying righteousness, arrogant and extravagant, how could this be the Dao?
Minister Yi says: Abandoning roots to chase ends, pursuing externals to harm the internal, forgetting one’s inherent nature while possessing what is not one’s own, forgetting one’s innate worth while boasting of what is not inherently noble—this is called thievish ostentation.

*End of Emperor Huizong of Song’s* Explication of the *Virtue-Power Classic of the Dao*, *Scroll Seven*
*Emperor Huizong of Song’s* Explication of the *Virtue-Power Classic of the Dao*, *Scroll Eight*

**Attributed to Gentleman of Court Rank, Serving Official Zhang An**

*Explication of the* Virtue-Power Classic, *Chapter Fifty-Four: "What is Well-Established Cannot Be Uprooted"*

What is well-established cannot be uprooted.
*Imperial Annotation* states: Established in the center, it encompasses above and below—thus it cannot be uprooted.
*Minister’s Explication* says: When the heart is bound, it leans and loses the center. Centered, it is free from bias or attachment—this is what is well-established, and thus cannot be uprooted.

What is well-embraced cannot be lost.
*Imperial Annotation* states: Embracing the One to respond to all changes—thus it cannot be lost.
*Minister’s Explication* says: To embrace well is to embrace the One. Pure and undivided, singular and unwavering, it can respond to endless transformations without ever being lost.

Descendants will continue their sacrifices without cease.
*Imperial Annotation* states: Established in the center without straying from the Dao, embracing the One without departing from essence—such a one does not merely act for a single state or a single generation. Rather, their influence extends to all under heaven and to future ages; their transmission endures far.
*Minister’s Explication* says: Established in the center and embracing the One, their influence is boundless, their Dao overspreads all under heaven. Posterity will enjoy unceasing blessings, and thus descendants will continue their sacrifices without cease.

Cultivate it in the self, and one’s virtue becomes true.
Cultivate it in the family, and one’s virtue becomes abundant.
Cultivate it in the village, and one’s virtue becomes enduring.
Cultivate it in the state, and one’s virtue becomes bountiful.
Cultivate it in all under heaven, and one’s virtue becomes universal.
*Imperial Annotation* states: Cultivate it in the self, and one’s virtue becomes true—this is what is meant by the true essence of the Dao for governing the self. Cultivate it in the family, and one’s virtue becomes abundant; cultivate it in the village, and one’s virtue becomes enduring—this is what is meant by its residual threads for governing others. Cultivate it in the state, and one’s virtue becomes bountiful; cultivate it in all under heaven, and one’s virtue becomes universal—this is what is meant by its unadorned substance for governing the state and all under heaven. The farther one’s cultivation extends, the broader one’s virtue becomes. What is within me is all its truth; what lies beyond is but its outward manifestation. Thus, abundance precedes endurance, bounty precedes universality—these are external to the Dao.
*Minister’s Explication* says: Established in the center, there is no excess or deficiency; embracing the One, essence remains whole and undivided. Cultivating this in the self, virtue becomes true. Cultivating this in the family, virtue becomes abundant. Cultivating this in the state, virtue becomes bountiful. Cultivating this in all under heaven, virtue becomes universal. The farther one’s cultivation extends, the broader one’s virtue becomes. The true essence of the Dao resides within me; thus, cultivating the true in the self, the self is perfected. Only then does one extend this virtue—from the family to the village, to the state, to all under heaven—and virtue grows ever broader.

Therefore, observe the self through the self, observe the family through the family, observe the village through the village, observe the state through the state, observe all under heaven through all under heaven.
*Imperial Annotation* states: All things are complete within me. Turning inward to sincerity, there is no greater joy. Thus, observe the self through the self, and the self is governed—extend this principle. All under heaven has its constancy; observe it through this, and all under heaven is governed.
*Minister’s Explication* says: The principles of all under heaven are constant, each with its own perfection. Those who fully realize them cannot harbor private cleverness. Thus, one who observes the self does not look beyond the self; one who observes the family does not look beyond the family. The same applies to observing the village, the state, and all under heaven. Those who deviate from this are not true observers.

How do I know that all under heaven is thus? By this.
*Imperial Annotation* states: Through simplicity, the principles of all under heaven are attained.
*Minister’s Explication* says: To know that all under heaven is thus and to exhaust its principles, one must return to the center and the One—this is simplicity. It is nothing more than following one’s nature and adhering to principle.

*Chapter Fifty-Five: "Those Who Contain Profound Virtue"*

Those who contain profound virtue are like newborn infants.
*Imperial Annotation* states: The people are born with inherent thickness, but they are swayed by external things. Those who contain profound virtue are not swayed by things—their qi is concentrated, their will singular. *Mencius* says: The great person does not lose their childlike heart.
*Minister’s Explication* says: Human virtue is originally whole and undivided, but it is swayed by external things, becoming shallow rather than profound. Only when both self and things are forgotten, and one contains profound virtue, is essence preserved whole and undiminished, spirit singular and undivided. Thus, one returns to a state of unknowing, and the heart becomes like that of a newborn.

Poisonous insects do not sting them, fierce beasts do not seize them, birds of prey do not strike them.
*Imperial Annotation* states: Those who contain profound virtue cannot be entered by anxiety or restlessness, nor can perverse qi invade. Thus, nothing can harm them. *Zhuangzi* says: If one can empty the self to wander through the world, who could harm them?
*Minister’s Explication* says: Like newborn infants, their essence is whole and spirit secure. The heart returns to a state of non-heart, and fear, anxiety, or restlessness cannot enter. Thus, they have never harbored a mind that startles things, and things do not harm them.

Their bones are weak and their sinews soft, yet their grip is firm. They do not know the union of male and female, yet their organ stirs—such is the perfection of essence.
*Imperial Annotation* states: When virtue is whole, the form is whole—thus, bones are weak and sinews soft, yet their grip is firm. When the form is whole, the spirit is whole—thus, they do not know the union of male and female, yet their organ stirs. The perfection of essence can enter the spirit. *Zhuangzi* says: The sage values essence.
*Minister’s Explication* says: When virtue is whole, qi is whole; when qi is whole, spirit is whole; when spirit is whole, form is whole. When spirit is whole, form is not bound by existence, and qi transforms it. Thus, bones are weak and sinews soft, yet their grip is firm. Qi is not stirred by movement, and essence remains whole in the One—thus, they do not know the union of male and female, yet their organ stirs.

They cry all day without their throat becoming hoarse—such is the perfection of harmony.
*Imperial Annotation* states: Attaining the One is called essence; when essence is perfected, virtue is whole and spirit is not diminished. The harmonious qi is the mean—when harmony is perfected, qi is whole and the throat does not become hoarse. In human life, essence is received from the One of Heaven as the source of wisdom, and harmony is received from the Five of Heaven as the root of trust. At their utmost, they can enter the spirit and return to the mandate. But if one loses the childlike heart, essence wavers and does not endure, qi becomes violent and does not nourish. The form and nature are scattered, and one is submerged in the myriad things—is this not lamentable?

Minister Yi states: The infant’s form engages with things, yet its heart remains ever still and unified. The sounds it emits and the movements of its qi arise spontaneously—not because the heart commands the qi, nor does it bear the burden of moving qi. Thus, it preserves the wholeness of essence, completes the harmony of qi, its spirit remains unharmed, and its voice does not grow hoarse. Such is the subtlety that allows entry into the spirit, the constancy that enables the return to the mandate—this is the perfection of harmony.

"To know harmony is called constancy."
*Imperial Annotation* states: Guarding the purity of qi, life’s command is held within; the form transforms, yet nature does not perish.
Minister Yi states: Constancy is the ultimate reality, ever thus and never altering. Though the form may undergo myriad changes, nature remains unshaken. Preserving the purity of qi and safeguarding supreme harmony, one knows harmony and permits no deviation—hence it is called constancy.

"To know constancy is called clarity."
*Imperial Annotation* states: Clarity sufficient to perceive the Dao lies in knowing that nature does not perish.
Minister Yi states: Knowing that nature possesses constancy and never alters, never perishes, one’s vision of the Dao becomes clear—hence it is called clarity.

"To augment life is called omen."
*Imperial Annotation* states: An omen is the precursor of things. The principle of life, when augmented, becomes superfluous—fortune and misfortune remain undetermined.
Minister Yi states: The principle of life is already complete; why seek augmentation? Augmentation becomes an omen, for fortune and misfortune are not yet fixed.

"When the heart commands the qi, it grows strong daily."
*Imperial Annotation* states: When the body aligns with the heart, and the heart aligns with qi, qi remains harmonious and not violent. Stumbling or rushing—these are movements of qi, yet the heart truly commands them, thus becoming strong. This is to contend with things, not the way of self-mastery.
Minister Yi states: When the heart returns to nothingness, qi is preserved in harmony. When the heart commands qi, qi becomes violent and loses its nourishment. Things grow strong, then age—this is called not following the Dao.

*Imperial Annotation* states: The Dao knows no past or present; things have their prime and their decline. Strength may turn to weakness, flourishing to decay. Governed by time and bound by fate—how can this be the Dao?
Minister Yi states: With strength comes age, shifting with time and transformation. The form changes accordingly—such is the nature of things. Those who embody profound virtue, like the infant, possess constancy and do not change—this approaches the Dao.

"Not following the Dao brings early demise."
*Imperial Annotation* states: The Dao has no beginning or end, yet the people’s delusion has persisted for ages.
Minister Yi states: Without the Dao, though life may be strong, it has already lost the principle of life.

**Chapter Fifty-Six: The Wise Do Not Speak**
The wise do not speak; those who speak do not know.
*Imperial Annotation* states: The Dao cannot be questioned, and questions receive no answer. Those who know the Dao recognize it in silence, without need for words. When Nie Que questioned Wang Ni four times, he received four responses of ignorance. Excessive speech leads to exhaustion, and one drifts far from the Dao.
Minister Yi states: The Dao cannot be spoken, thus those who embody it do not speak but recognize it in silence. What can be spoken is but the coarse aspect of the Dao. Excessive speech leads to exhaustion, so those who speak do not know.

"Block the apertures, close the gates."
*Imperial Annotation* states: Seal the senses and guard the spirit, withdrawing and concealing in secrecy.
Minister Yi states: Blocking the apertures ensures the heart takes no delight in externals; closing the gates ensures the spirit does not stray toward things—this is how one withdraws and conceals in secrecy.

"Blunt the sharpness, untangle the knots."
*Imperial Annotation* states: Take depth as the root, simplicity as the guiding principle.
Minister Yi states: Blunting the sharpness makes it dull before it becomes truly effective. Untangling the knots means not being ensnared by things. When the root is deep, sharpness is not apparent; when the principle is simple, what need is there to untangle knots?

"Harmonize the light, merge with the dust."
*Imperial Annotation* states: Flow with things, moving in unison with their waves.
Minister Yi states: Harmonizing the light is returning to one’s inherent brightness. Merging with the dust is blending indistinguishably with things. By returning brightness and blending with things, one moves in unison with their waves.

This is called "mysterious sameness."
*Imperial Annotation* states: The Dao returns to the utmost subtlety, uniting with the utmost oneness. The utmost subtlety is called "mysterious," the utmost oneness is called "sameness." In mysterious sameness, the myriad things and I become indistinguishable—how could one then be entangled in the petty distinctions of closeness and distance, benefit and harm, nobility and baseness?
Minister Yi states: The utmost subtlety encompasses all wonders; the utmost oneness admits no duality. Being and non-being, self and other—all merge in mysterious sameness. One cannot be drawn near or kept distant, cannot be benefited or harmed, cannot be made noble or base. Thus, one is honored by all under heaven.

*Imperial Annotation* states: The people of the world attack one another with love and hate, displaying intimacy and estrangement. Their emotions and deceits interact, giving rise to views of benefit and harm. Their choices and rejections vie for dominance, creating distinctions of nobility and baseness. These shift and alternate endlessly—how could such things be honored by all under heaven? Those who know the Dao forget words, and in forgetting words, they dissolve preferences and aversions, transcend emotions and deceits, abandon choices and rejections, and merge in mysterious sameness within the unity of nature. True honor is self-sufficient; when the heart of all under heaven is forgotten, one becomes honored by all under heaven.
Minister Yi states: Those who are objectified by things can be drawn near or kept distant, benefited or harmed, made noble or base. But when self and other merge in mysterious sameness, one objectifies without being objectified by things—thus, none can confer rank upon them, yet they remain ever in accord with nature.

**Chapter Fifty-Seven: Governing with Rectitude**
Govern the state with rectitude, deploy troops with奇 (unconventional tactics), and win the world through non-action.
*Imperial Annotation* states: Rectitude is the constancy of the Dao;奇 (unconventionality) is its adaptability; non-action is its true essence. The state is stabilized through rectitude, victory in war is achieved through奇, and the true essence of the Dao admits no selfishness. Following the natural course of things, the world is governed.
Minister Yi states: Governing the state with rectitude to establish order is the constancy of the Dao. Deploying troops with奇 to achieve victory is its adaptability. Winning the world through non-action is its true essence. When lords and kings attain the One as rectitude, nothing fails to align—thus the state is well-governed.

War achieves victory through adaptability as its unconventionality, and nothing can overcome it—such is the function of military force. When laws are enacted, deceit arises; when commands are issued, fraud emerges. Thus, the ruler who practices non-action holds the left tally, and the world responds—this is what is meant by "winning the world through non-action."

How do I know this is so? The more taboos there are in the world, the poorer the people become.

*Imperial Annotation* states: The people are not difficult to gather—cherish them, and they draw near; benefit them, and they come. But impose what they detest, and they scatter. Now, without cherishing or benefiting them, and with many taboos and prohibitions, the people will scatter to the four directions, hence their increasing poverty.

Minister Yi states: Many taboos lead to many affairs and many prohibitions, so the people are harassed by laws and cannot secure their livelihoods. Thus, the people first fall into disorder and then into deeper poverty.

When people possess many sharp instruments, the state grows ever more benighted.

*Imperial Annotation* states: Where there are mechanical devices, there must be mechanical affairs; where there are mechanical affairs, there must be mechanical minds. Once mechanical minds arise, purity and simplicity are lost, and some may deceive their superiors by means contrary to the Dao.

Minister Yi states: When people vie with one another through mechanical devices, deceit and falsehood sprout. They alternately form cliques to deceive and obscure, which is why the state grows ever more benighted.

When people value cunning and skill, strange things proliferate.

*Imperial Annotation* states: When craftiness and skill prevail, people pursue frivolous ends, and bizarre attire and strange implements emerge to corrupt customs.

Minister Yi states: When cunning and skill are esteemed, customs are corrupted by the strange and unusual.

When laws and decrees grow ever more elaborate, thieves and bandits multiply.

*Imperial Annotation* states: Excessive scrutiny inevitably elicits unworthy responses.

Minister Yi states: When the state is benighted by people vying with sharp instruments, and customs are corrupted by the esteem for cunning and skill, with many taboos and prohibitions leaving the people impoverished and unemployed, then governance loses its rectitude, and the world is harassed by excessive affairs. As laws and decrees grow ever more elaborate and controls ever more stringent, the people become increasingly shameless. When the people are without shame, there is nothing they will not do—hence the proliferation of thieves and bandits.

Therefore, the sage declares: "I practice non-action, and the people transform themselves."

*Imperial Annotation* states: Heaven is non-active and thus clear; Earth is non-active and thus tranquil. When the two non-actions combine, the myriad things are transformed. The sage is but Heaven and Earth, so the people daily move toward goodness without knowing who effects it.

Minister Yi states: The sage embodies the Dao through non-action, restoring nature to its original nature. Thus, the people are transformed from above without knowing how they are transformed.

"I value stillness, and the people rectify themselves."

*Imperial Annotation* states: When a mirror reflects a form, it does not employ cleverness, yet the square, round, curved, and straight cannot escape it. How could it require compasses and squares to be correct?

Minister Yi states: Stillness unifies governance, and the people are all unified in rectitude.

"I engage in no affairs, and the people enrich themselves."

*Imperial Annotation* states: The world is originally without affairs—mediocre people disturb it. Do not disturb it, and the people will enrich themselves.

Minister Yi states: When the ruler engages in no affairs, the people have surplus energy, live in peace, delight in their work, and become prosperous.

"I have no desires, and the people return to simplicity."

*Imperial Annotation* states: Do not esteem the worthy, and the people will not contend. Do not prize rare goods, and the people will not steal. Align with desirelessness, and the people’s nature is preserved.

Minister Yi states: Without desire, there is no intent. When the ruler has no intent, the people recover their nature, find sufficiency in their allotted nature, return their minds to unity, and preserve their simplicity.

**Its Governance Unadorned**

The *Imperial Annotation* states: In governing the world through non-interference, the people below merely know of the ruler’s existence, yet feel no exuberant joy.

Minister Yi states: When the ruler governs the world through the Dao’s non-interference, the people below follow the Dao to fulfill their lives. Above and below align in unity, returning to the Dao. The ruler does not display action, and the people do not perceive the emperor’s power—thus is the obscurity of governance.

Its people are pure and simple.
The *Imperial Annotation* states: They embrace plainness and hold to simplicity, with few selfish desires and scant cravings.

Minister Yi states: "Pure and simple" means being naturally unadorned, free from selfish desires, and returning to one’s original livelihood.

Its governance is meticulous and exacting.
The *Imperial Annotation* states: Combing one’s hair with fine teeth, counting grains while cooking, and priding oneself on petty precision—this is the governance of meticulous exactitude.

Minister Yi states: Such governance, with its ostentatious clarity, pursues the minutest details. New policies are enacted, and severity is mistaken for wisdom.

Its people are deficient and flawed.
The *Imperial Annotation* states: When the worthy are promoted, the people contend; when cunning is employed, the people steal. Thus, their virtue is never whole.

Minister Yi states: When governance is exacting, the people grow crafty in deception. Harmed by their own artifice, they cannot preserve wholeness—hence, their virtue is never complete.

Misfortune leans upon fortune, and fortune hides within misfortune. Who knows their ultimate end?
The *Imperial Annotation* states: Clarity arises from obscurity, order emerges from formlessness. Virtue, wisdom, and sagacity are preserved through affliction. In the households of the lofty and wise, ghosts peer into their chambers. Knowing the times, one does not linger; knowing one’s allotment, one does not cling to constancy. Recognizing that beginnings and ends cannot be fixed, how can misfortune and fortune, with their mutual concealment, have any permanence?

Minister Yi states: Prosperity and adversity, fortune and misfortune, success and failure—these alternate and intertwine like a ceaseless cycle, without end. Only by resting in one’s allotted time and portion can one accept the natural arising of misfortune and fortune, for how can they be constant?

Is there no true standard?
The *Imperial Annotation* states: If you make those who agree with you the standard, how can you correct them once they already agree? If you make those who differ from you the standard, how can you correct them once they already differ? Then who is the ultimate standard for the world?

Minister Yi states: To know goodness as goodness is already not goodness. To know beauty as beauty is already ugliness. When one merges with the supreme Dao without knowing how, who can claim a standard? Moreover, with misfortune and fortune boundless, who can impose one?

The standard reverts to the strange; goodness reverts to the ominous. The people’s delusion has endured for so long.
The *Imperial Annotation* states: The world is but a single qi. Today’s "right" was yesterday’s "wrong"; what was first resisted is later embraced. The divine and the foul, the putrid and the fragrant—all alternate in cycles. Thus, the strange and the standard, the ominous and the good, transform of themselves within the qi. The world has existed for so long, yet minor delusions distort direction, and great delusions distort nature. Has the prevalence of self-serving customs not obscured this truth? If misfortune and fortune’s mutual concealment are further scrutinized with exacting governance, how can the people return to their true nature?

Minister Yi states: Standards and strangeness, goodness and ominousness—all judgments of right and wrong arise from delusion and are not the great constancy. The people’s loss of their original nature has endured for so long. Unable to return to the root and align with the Dao, they merely seek fortune in their flawed state. If governance remains exacting, imposing a partial standard as the sole truth, can the world truly be made uniform under it?

Thus, the sage is square but does not cut,
The *Imperial Annotation* states: "Square" distinguishes things, yet the great square has no corners. It remains still without flowing, leaving no trace of distinction.

Minister Yi states: "Square" implies distinction, yet being square without cutting means merging with the undivided Dao, not severing from things.

Sharp but does not pierce,
The *Imperial Annotation* states: "Sharp" prides itself on purity, yet great purity is not fastidious. It is clear yet accommodates all, without harshness or severity.

Minister Yi states: Standing immaculate above the myriad things, yet embracing all without displaying harshness.

Straight but not unrestrained,
Radiant but not dazzling.
The *Imperial Annotation* states: Straight yet unrestrained breeds arrogance toward others; radiant yet dazzling invites the exhaustion of ostentation. Inwardly straight but outwardly yielding, using one’s light yet returning to one’s clarity—only the sage achieves this! The people’s delusion lies in taking squareness as rightness, like Zimo’s clinging to the mean; in taking sharpness as rightness, like Zhongzi’s austerity. Knowing expansion but not contraction, knowing brilliance but not subtlety, they flaunt themselves in the corrupt customs of the age, losing the sage’s wholeness. How can this rectify the world?

What, then, does the sage use to rectify the world? Only this.

Minister Yi states: "Utmost straightness appears curved," for it follows the meandering of things yet does not lose the straightness of the Dao. Straight yet unrestrained, one possesses straightness yet overbears what is not straight. Harmonizing one’s light and returning to one’s clarity, one blends with the dust of things yet never differs from them—how could one then display light to dazzle externally? Straight yet not unrestrained, radiant yet not dazzling—this is how the sage rectifies the world.

**Chapter 59: Governing People and Serving Heaven**
In governing people and serving heaven, nothing surpasses frugality.
The *Imperial Annotation* states: Hearing and seeing, wisdom and discernment belong to heaven. Movement and stillness, thought and deliberation belong to man. Adapting to the rhythm of movement and stillness, reducing the burden of thought and deliberation—this is how one governs people. Not exhausting the power of hearing and seeing, not depleting the task of wisdom and discernment—this is how one serves heaven. This is called frugality. The primordial unity resides in the storehouse, attending to the kidneys, standing in the garden of inexhaustible bounty. When the wellspring of wisdom overflows and its resonance emerges, human affairs are ordered and heavenly principle is attained.

Minister Yi states: The toil of movement and stillness wears the body; the labor of thought and deliberation taxes the mind—this is how man fails to govern himself. Exhausting hearing and seeing, depleting wisdom and discernment—this is how heaven cannot be served. When both are unified in frugality, the spirit is not expended, and the spirit is preserved in essence and oneness. In governing people and serving heaven, nothing is more essential than this. Thus, the sage does not err in movement and stillness, is not burdened by thought and deliberation, gathers hearing and seeing, forgets wisdom and discernment, and becomes one with heaven. This is called frugality. Then the storehouse is deep, yet its presence is not displayed; it is firm yet not expended.

Only through frugality does one return early.
The *Imperial Annotation* states: Those who return after losing their way do so late. Those who return before straying do not transgress their nature—thus they return early.

Minister Yi states: When resonance preserves the spirit without expenditure, return comes early.

Early return is called accumulating virtue abundantly.
The *Imperial Annotation* states: Return is the root of virtue. Returning through self-awareness, the Dao within me daily accumulates and renews.

Minister Yi states: Returning through self-awareness, virtue accumulates within. Returning early, what is gained grows ever more abundant.

Abundantly accumulating virtue, there is nothing one cannot overcome.
The *Imperial Annotation* states: To overcome is to be victorious. Governing the myriad things, commanding the host of movements, and overcoming all—only virtue achieves this.

Minister Yi states: When virtue is abundantly accumulated, it grows ever more profound. With profound virtue, the Dao suffices to overcome one’s selfishness, reaching the point of forgetting the self. Forgetting the self, one forgets things. When self and things are forgotten, there is nothing one cannot overcome.

Nothing one cannot overcome—then none know its limit.
The *Imperial Annotation* states: When virtue reaches the point of overcoming all, it responds boundlessly without exhaustion—who knows its limit?

Minister Yi states: When virtue reaches the point of overcoming all, it is because self and things are forgotten. When self and things are forgotten and one returns to utmost nothingness, how can its limit be known?

None knowing its limit, one can possess the state.
The *Imperial Annotation* states: Embracing the boundless, one’s use of the world is inexhaustible—how much more so for possessing a state?

Minister Yi states: Only after embodying the Dao and preserving the spirit can one possess a state. In governing people and serving heaven, this is the utmost perfection.

Possessing the mother of the state, one can endure long.
The *Imperial Annotation* states: The Dao is the mother of the myriad things. Those who possess the Dao endure for ten thousand generations without decay.

Minister Yi states: The mother of the state is the limitless Dao. Only the Dao is constant and unchanging—thus it endures long.

This is called delving into the root and firming the foundation, the Dao of long life and enduring vision.
The *Imperial Annotation* states: The Dao is the mother of things, and things are its children. Nature is the root of form, and form is its foundation. Knowing the child, one guards the mother—thus one does not perish even in death. This is how one endures long. Roots deep, foundations firm; nature restored, form preserved—one becomes constant with heaven and earth, thus attaining long life. One shares radiance with the sun and moon, thus attaining enduring vision. While people and things transform, I alone endure—this is called the Dao.

Minister Yi states: When nature returns to the Dao, its roots run deep. When form is born of the Dao, its foundation is firm. If roots are not deep, essence is not whole; if essence is not whole, spirit is not secure; if spirit is not secure, form departs. Thus, deep roots and firm foundations enable long life, enduring vision, and steadfast existence.

**Chapter 60: Governing a Great State**
Governing a great state is like cooking a small fish.

The Imperial Commentary states: Managing great affairs with frequent disturbances yields little success. Storing great vessels and frequently moving them leads to much damage. Cooking small fish with constant stirring causes them to disintegrate. Governing a great state with frequent changes to the law causes confusion. Thus, the Way of governance values clarity and stillness, and the people naturally settle.

Minister Yi states: When cooking fish, allow them to mature on their own, without employing cunning or skill. The sage practices non-action, entrusting the myriad things to their natural course—thus it is like cooking small fish. Those who govern the world with the Dao render even ghosts powerless.

The Imperial Commentary states: The sage, master of spirits, people, and the myriad things, must reluctantly oversee the world—nothing is better than non-action. The Dao is ever without action; in governing the world thus, there is nothing that is not well-ordered. Those who rely on others to act will all find peace and rest, none emerging to cause harm. Thus it is said, "their ghosts are not potent."

Minister Yi states: If one does not govern the world with the Dao, people are overcome by private and perverse influences, causing yin and yang to fall into disorder and ghostly spirits to seize opportunities, thereby manifesting their malevolence. When the ruler governs with the Dao, heaven is clear, earth is tranquil, people are harmonious, and qi is balanced—even ghosts find peace, for rectitude prevails. Thus, ghosts are not potent. It is not that ghosts lack potency, but that their potency does not harm the people. It is not that their potency does not harm the people, but that the sage also does not harm the people. When the two do not harm one another, virtue and power converge.

The Imperial Commentary states: Those who govern the world with the Dao act without contrivance, ever in accord with nature. Without the calamities of war or the punishments of execution, this is called "not harming the people." At such a time, spirits and people do not harm one another, and virtue and power converge. Spirits have no occasion to display their numinous responses, content merely with abundant blessings. Thus it is said, "their potency does not harm the people," and the people have no need to employ their cunning or skill. Daily sustenance and food—what harm could there be?

Minister Yi states: When one acts with deliberate effort and busies oneself with affairs, the world abounds with taboos. Laws and decrees proliferate, punishments grow severe, taxes become onerous, enemies are provoked, fields lie fallow, and the people grow ever poorer. The hundred clans suffer hardship, unable to secure their lives—this is self-inflicted harm upon the people. When the ruler governs the world with the Dao and does not harm the people, following the principles of the myriad things’ nature and lives, without disrupting the harmony of yin and yang, then all good things flourish in great accord, each finding its proper place, and qi resonates with the harmony of heaven and earth. How could spirits harm people? They are content merely with abundant blessings. At this point, people and spirits converge in mutual return, virtue and power do not harm one another—this is the perfection of governance.

*End of Volume Eight of the Imperial Annotated and Interpreted "Classic of the Dao and Its Virtue" by Emperor Huizong of Song*
*Beginning of Volume Nine of the Imperial Annotated and Interpreted "Classic of the Dao and Its Virtue" by Emperor Huizong of Song*

**Commentary by Gentleman of Court Rank, Attendant Gentleman Zhang An**

**Chapter 61: "The Great State Is the Lower Reaches"**

The great state is the lower reaches.

*Imperial Annotation states:* No one lacks the heart that hastens toward commerce, yet those who hasten toward the heights often stumble. The reason the rivers and seas can be kings of the hundred valleys is that they excel in lowering themselves.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Flowing downward, they become the destination of all streams, thus achieving their greatness. In the intercourse of the world, the world’s female—femininity ever prevails through stillness, while masculinity is made low through stillness.

*Imperial Annotation states:* The world all contends with things through hardness and strength, yet I alone dwell in the realm of softness, stillness, and non-contention. Who then could overcome me? This is the Way by which one engages with the world.

The *Classic* says: Know its masculinity, yet guard its femininity, and become the world’s stream.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* If one establishes oneself through hardness and strength, things become one’s adversaries. Softness does not contend; stillness does not initiate. Only through softness and stillness can one be without conflict with things—this is how one becomes the world’s female, the Way by which one engages with the world. Thus, when a great state lowers itself before a small state, it wins over the small state. When a small state lowers itself before a great state, it wins over the great state. Therefore, some lower themselves to take, while others are lowered to be taken.

*Imperial Annotation states:* To gather, one must first expand; to take, one must first give.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Only by lowering oneself can one take. Those who do not lower themselves will inevitably be lowered by others and remain beneath them. A great state desires nothing more than to nurture and shelter others; a small state desires nothing more than to serve and attend others. When both attain what they desire, the greater should therefore be the one to lower itself.

*Imperial Annotation states:* The Way of Heaven descends and illuminates, thus nothing is not covered. The Way of Earth is lowly yet ascends, thus it can bear Heaven. Humans emulate Earth, Earth emulates Heaven—therefore, the greater should be the one to lower itself.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* If a small state does not lower itself before a great state, calamity swiftly follows. When a great state lowers itself before a small state, the small state rarely fails to submit. Those who cannot lower themselves remain beneath the great state—thus, the greater should be the one to lower itself.

**Chapter 62: "The Dao Is the Mystery of the Myriad Things"**

The Dao is the mystery of the myriad things.

*Imperial Annotation states:* The northwest is the mystery of Heaven, where essence transforms in seclusion. The Yellow Springs are the mystery of Earth, where spirits and flourishing lie hidden. The mystery of humans lies in thought, which harbors the most profound spirit. Heaven, Earth, and humans each have what is called mystery, yet all are enveloped by the Dao. The Dao is difficult to exhaust, difficult to fathom, difficult to measure, difficult to know—thus it is the mystery of the myriad things. If the Dao is the mystery of the myriad things, then are things the manifestation of the Dao?

*Minister’s Exegesis:* The Dao is that from which the myriad things emerge, yet it cannot be seen—thus it is called the mystery of the myriad things. All things are enveloped by the Dao; the Dao shelters and covers all without exception, its wondrous workings deep and hidden—this is the meaning of "mystery." It is the treasure of the good person.

*Imperial Annotation states:* Acting with benefit and accumulating goodness refines one’s nature, so that spirit and clarity are naturally attained, and the sagely mind follows.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* The good person knows to value and cherish the Dao, thus does not lose themselves in things and knows what is truly precious. It is the refuge of the unvirtuous.

*Imperial Annotation states:* Turning away from it brings only harm; following its principle preserves wholeness. Movement without deviation from the norm, stillness that rectifies one’s nature—thus it can preserve the self.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* "Preserve" is like the preservation of a tutor, as in "preserving an infant." Though a person may be unvirtuous, when their nature is still and their principle is aligned, they attain wholeness and rectitude—this is what the Dao preserves. How could it be abandoned? Since the Dao does not abandon them, it is called "preservation."

Fine words can be traded in the marketplace; esteemed conduct can be imposed upon others. The unvirtuous—how could they be abandoned?

*Imperial Annotation states:* Words are like wind and waves; conduct is like gain and loss. Neither is what the Dao values. Yet if words are fine and pleasing, if conduct is esteemed and exalted, they can still be traded and imposed upon others, and people will submit. How much more so the mystery of the Dao, which universally covers and embraces all without distinction? Then how could the unvirtuous be abandoned?

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Fine words and esteemed conduct are ever more distant from the Dao, yet they can still be traded if they benefit others. How much more the mystery of the Dao, which covers all without exception? Thus, though a person may be unvirtuous, the Dao preserves them and does not abandon them.

Thus, in establishing the Son of Heaven and appointing the Three Dukes, though one may offer large jade discs preceded by teams of four horses, it is not as good as sitting and advancing this Dao.

*Imperial Annotation states:* The noble person’s duty is to cultivate the self so that the world is at peace. The Son of Heaven and the Three Dukes may possess jade and horses to summon the worthy, yet if they do not devote themselves to advancing the Dao to cultivate the self, they will abandon themselves to follow others, losing the Way of self-governance. It is not as good as sitting and advancing this Dao—seeking it within oneself alone. Where the Dao resides, the sage esteems it, and the people follow as if returning to the marketplace.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* The Son of Heaven and the Three Dukes govern the world through the Dao. Jade discs and teams of four horses are the most precious of things, yet if one’s heart is set on things, one loses the Dao and follows things, unable to govern. Sitting and advancing this Dao transforms the world, and those who return do so as if to a marketplace—this too is seeking it within oneself alone.

Why was this Dao esteemed by the ancients? Did they not say: Seek and you shall obtain; with transgressions, you shall be spared? Thus it is esteemed by the world.

*Imperial Annotation states:* To seek is to obtain—seeking lies within oneself. The reason the ancients sought it in the calculations of yin and yang yet did not obtain it was because their seeking lay outside. The wicked turn toward goodness; the foolish become wise—is this not how transgressions are spared? The Dao’s ability to save is like this—thus it is esteemed by the world.

The *Tradition* says: There is nothing in the world that does not esteem the Dao.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Only the Dao can save people without abandoning them, save things without abandoning them. Thus, seek and you shall obtain; with transgressions, you shall be spared—this is why it is esteemed by the world.

Seek what lies within oneself, thus seeking yields attainment. Restore one’s nature to its origin, thus transgressions are spared.

*Chapter 63: Acting Without Acting*
Act without acting, attend without attending, savor without savoring.

*Imperial Annotation states:* The essence of the Dao is non-action, hence “without acting.” It is without form, hence “without attending.” It is without desire, hence “without savoring.” The sage responds to the existence of things while embodying the non-existence of the Dao—through these three, its outline may be glimpsed.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* The Dao is without acting, for it allows things to achieve completion on their own. The Dao is without attending, for it allows things to unfold on their own. Without acting, there is no contrivance; without attending, there is no form. Without contrivance and without form, one abides in flavorless simplicity—what desire could there be? Those who pursue externals, indulging in worldly flavors and cravings, ensnared by the mouth’s burdens—how could they grasp the essentials of embodying the Dao?

*Great and small, many and few.*
*Imperial Annotation states:* “Great and small” refer to form; “many and few” refer to quantity. The measure of things is boundless and cannot be demarcated. If the great are not many and the small are not few, who can discern the distinction between the repayment of enmity and gratitude? This is how the sage unifies the ten thousand things in the One of non-existence, thereby accomplishing greatness.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Though “great and small, many and few” pertain to form and quantity and thus differ, when gratitude and enmity are regarded with equal vision, who can find fault? Repay enmity with virtue.

*Imperial Annotation states:* Rank and emolument are insufficient to encourage; execution and disgrace are insufficient to shame—what enmity could there be? What is esteemed is virtue alone.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* When one is entangled in existence and the breath of the body, one is startled by favor and disgrace, hence there is gratitude and enmity. When one abides in the realm of self-attainment, who distinguishes between self and other? Thus, there is no gratitude or enmity. What is esteemed is virtue—nothing appears worthy of enmity, hence it is said: repay enmity with virtue.

Plan for difficulty while it is easy; accomplish the great while it is small. The difficult affairs of the world must arise from the easy. The great affairs of the world must arise from the small.

*Imperial Annotation states:* A thousand-zhang dike collapses from the hole of an ant; a hundred-chi chamber burns from the smoke of a chimney crack. When the Lord of Bai Gui patrolled the dike, he plugged the holes—thus there was no flood disaster. When the old man was cautious with fire, he sealed the cracks—thus there was no fire calamity. The affairs of the world often begin in the most minute, and by their end, they cannot be mastered. Therefore, the sage acts early.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Affairs lie hidden in what is overlooked; difficulty lies in planning at the outset. If one knows to plan for what is easy and small, there will be no failure in the end. Once a matter stumbles at the first step, nothing can be done. Thus, the sage never acts as if great, and so is able to accomplish greatness.

*Imperial Annotation states:* Acting when it is small, one is able to accomplish greatness. If one waits to govern until after disorder has formed, is it not too late?

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Accumulate the small to become lofty and great.

One who makes light promises is surely lacking in trust; one who treats much as easy is surely beset by difficulty. Thus, the sage treats things as difficult, and so ultimately encounters no difficulty.

*Imperial Annotation states:* Disasters often lie hidden in the minute and arise from what is overlooked. The sage’s response to the world is always cautious of the minute and never negligent. Thus, there is initially no light or easy conduct, and ultimately no calamities of difficulty to be planned against—all because one embodies non-existence.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Light promises are a trivial matter, yet they lead to the calamity of lost trust. Treating much as easy is to overlook the minute, yet it leads to the burden of many difficulties. The sage treats things as if they were difficult—thus, the affairs of the world ultimately present no difficulty. This is because one fears the minute.

*Chapter 64: What Is Secure Is Easily Upheld*
What is secure is easily upheld; what has not yet shown signs is easily planned for. What is fragile is easily dissolved; what is minute is easily scattered. Act before things arise; govern before disorder begins.

*Imperial Annotation states:* Security is the opposite of danger; what has not yet shown signs is the opposite of what has taken form. Fragility is the opposite of firmness; the minute is the opposite of the manifest. Uphold what is secure, and there will be no danger. Plan for what has not yet shown signs, and it will not take form. The sage’s knowledge of the incipient: dissolve what is fragile, and it will not become solid ice; scatter what is minute, and it will not become manifest. This is the perilous nearness of the worthy.

A speeding chariot, a sunken boat—the sage has no use for wisdom. Why employ wisdom for what has not yet sped or sunk? This is what is meant by “act before things arise; govern before disorder begins.”

*Minister’s Exegesis:* “What is secure” and “what has not yet shown signs” refer to affairs that have not yet taken form. “What is fragile” and “what is minute” refer to affairs that are extremely small. Acting before affairs take form is to act before things arise. Governing what is extremely small is to govern before disorder begins.

A tree of arm-spanning girth grows from a tiny sprout. A nine-tiered terrace rises from a basket of earth. A journey of a thousand *li* begins beneath one’s feet.

*Imperial Annotation states:* Among things with form, the great must grow from the small, the high must be founded on the low, the distant must begin from the near. Their beginning is simple, but their completion is surely vast. The sage sees the beginning and thinks of the end, observes the finger and knows the destination. Thus, one does not precede fortune nor initiate calamity, hesitates to undertake affairs, and always succeeds.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* The tree is a metaphor for growth from small to great; the terrace is a metaphor for ascent from low to high; the journey is a metaphor for progression from near to far. The great, the high, the distant—all begin from the minute. This is how the sage undertakes affairs and achieves success.

One who acts fails; one who grasps loses. Thus, the sage does not act, and so does not fail; does not grasp, and so does not lose.

*Imperial Annotation states:* The sage does not engage in busyness, hence there is no failure. One does not cling to the past, hence there is no loss. The deluded measure and rigidly adhere—how can they avoid failure and loss when they depart further from the Dao?

*Minister’s Exegesis:* To act once and seek completion is to invite failure. To grasp and demand a peck is to invite loss. Only by non-action can one allow things to achieve completion on their own and avoid failure. Only by non-grasping can one attain what is naturally attained and avoid loss.

Thus, when the people engage in affairs, they commonly fail at the point of near-completion.

*Imperial Annotation states:* To halt midway, to abandon halfway, to begin with vigor yet end in slackness—such is the nature of the common people, and none are exempt. Hence, affairs often reach near-completion only to end in failure.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Only the sage possesses both beginning and end. When the common people depart from the Dao to undertake affairs, how can those affairs endure? Thus, they invariably fail. If one remains as vigilant at the end as at the beginning, no affair will fail.

*Imperial Annotation states:* All things have a beginning, but few attain their end. When beginning and end are as one, renewal occurs day by day. Applied to affairs, what could not be accomplished?

*Minister’s Exegesis:* To maintain unity from beginning to end is the height of vigilance; thus, affairs will not fail. Therefore, the sage desires what others do not desire and does not prize rare and precious goods. They study what others do not study, thereby restoring what the multitude has lost.

*Imperial Annotation states:* Those who desire profit exchange themselves for things; those who pursue learning drown their minds in breadth. How can this lead to non-action? The sage does not allow profit to burden the body; their desire lies in non-desire. Nourishment of self and others is fulfilled and then ceases. Thus, they do not prize rare goods, nor do they allow human artifice to obliterate Heaven. Their learning lies in non-learning, in gathering brilliance within illumination alone. Thus, they restore what the multitude has lost. The Dao is not made manifest. Even the worthy transgress it—how much more the common people? Restoring their transgressions and returning them to their nature is why those who abandon learning have no sorrow and find joy.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* The allotment of nature is originally sufficient, hence there is no desire. The principle of nature is originally luminous, hence there is no need for learning. Only by forgetting what is inherently sufficient does one multiply desires and lose oneself. Only by obscuring what is inherently luminous does one drown the mind in excessive learning. The sage does not allow profit to burden the body, and their desire lies in non-desire; thus, they do not prize rare goods. They do not allow human artifice to obliterate Heaven, and their learning lies in non-learning; thus, they restore what the multitude has lost. To call it a transgression is to lose the mean. Correcting this transgression is how one restores nature. This is how one assists the spontaneity of the myriad things without daring to act.

*Imperial Annotation states:* Heaven is high, earth is low, and the myriad things are utterly distinct—who compels them? It is simply the spontaneity of their nature. Assisting their spontaneity allows them to fulfill their nature. To act is to fail; thus, one does not dare to act. This is why the sage relies on the transformative power of the Dao and does not employ cunning or skill.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* The nature of the myriad things is each endowed with spontaneity. The sage embodies the Dao through non-action and merely assists them. What lives fulfills its life; what reaches completion fulfills its completion. Human effort plays no part. To act is to fail; thus, one does not dare to act.

**Chapter Sixty-Five: Those of Old Skilled in the Dao**

Those of old skilled in the Dao did not enlighten the people but sought to make them simple.

*Imperial Annotation states:* The people may be made to follow, but they cannot be made to understand. Those of old skilled in the Dao merely made them follow. Reversing their usual state, the Dao can be carried and journeyed with, without employing cunning or skill—hence, it is called simplicity. Since the Three Dynasties, they have abandoned tranquil non-action and delighted in the earnest and sincere, bending and shaping ritual and music to rectify the forms of the world, and sighing over benevolence and righteousness to console the hearts of the world. They sought to enlighten the people, calling it governance—yet what greater disorder could there be?

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Since the Three Dynasties, teachings have grown increasingly detailed, their refinement ever more elaborate, yet by the end of the Zhou, great corruption arose—this was the fault of enlightening the people. Thus, the Old Master sought to make them simple. He caused them not to know what goodness is as goodness, what beauty is as beauty, and to have no aspirations, each content in their allotted nature, not harming their Heaven-given truth, complete in self-attainment, and not employing artifice. Thus, they could return to constancy and restore simplicity.

The people are difficult to govern because of their excessive knowledge.

*Imperial Annotation states:* The world is repeatedly thrown into great disorder, and the fault lies in the love of knowledge.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* When people compete with knowledge, falsehood and deceit grow daily—how can this lead to good governance? Thus, governing the state with knowledge is the state’s bane.

*Imperial Annotation states:* Laws are issued, and treachery arises; commands are given, and deceit follows.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* When knowledge is esteemed, people compete with cunning and deceit, making governance difficult. Thus, knowledge is the state’s bane. Not governing the state with knowledge is the state’s blessing.

*Imperial Annotation states:* Burn the tallies, break the seals, and the people return to rustic simplicity. Smash the measuring cups, break the scales, and the people cease to contend.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Not employing knowledge leads the people to simplicity and non-contention—this is the state’s blessing, a qi greater than all else.

Knowing these two paths is also a model.

*Imperial Annotation states:* Knowing these two paths is to know how to govern the state. Knowing how to govern the state, the people will emulate and pattern themselves after it as a model.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* When superiors know that governance lies in both employing and not employing knowledge, the people will follow their example and have a model to emulate.

To constantly know the model is called profound virtue.

Profound virtue is deep and far-reaching, turning back from things.

*Imperial Annotation states:* Profundity is the color of Heaven. To constantly know the model and not employ one’s knowledge is to unite one’s virtue with Heaven—deep beyond measure, far beyond reach, standing alone above the myriad things. No thing can pair with it; thus, it is said to turn back from things.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Knowing the model and not employing knowledge returns the mind to non-mind and knowledge to non-knowledge—this is the profundity of virtue. Profound virtue is most deep and most far-reaching, thus it turns back from things and merges with the Dao.

Only then does one attain great compliance.

*Imperial Annotation states:* Compliance is the principle of Heaven. To attain great compliance is to abandon knowledge and habit, following the principle of Heaven alone. Zhuangzi said: "To unite with Heaven and Earth is to merge seamlessly, as if foolish, as if dull." This is called profound virtue uniting with great compliance. Only by being as if foolish, as if dull, can one abandon knowledge.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* In the age of profound virtue, each thing follows its own nature without knowing why—this is called great compliance.

**Chapter 66: The River and Sea as Kings of the Hundred Valleys**
The river and sea can be kings of the hundred valleys because they excel at being lowly; thus they become kings of the hundred valleys.

*Imperial Annotation:* In initiating affairs and establishing enterprises, the one who rises above all is called king. "King" implies a place to which all return. If a ruler can humble himself before those below, the people will return to him as water flows downward.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* The river and sea excel at being lowly, so all streams return to them—thus they are great.

Therefore, if the sage wishes to be above the people, he must place his words below them. If he wishes to lead the people, he must place himself behind them. Thus, though the sage dwells above, the people do not feel oppressed; though he dwells ahead, the people do not feel harmed. Therefore, the world delights in exalting him and never tires of it.

*Imperial Annotation:* The *Book of Changes* says of the initial hexagram *Tun*: "The noble lowers himself before the lowly—this is how he greatly wins the people." To win the people is to win their hearts. If he dwells above yet the people do not feel oppressed, following him becomes effortless. If he dwells ahead yet the people do not feel harmed, those who benefit from him are many. In such a case, none will fail to submit, and thus they will never tire of him. The *Book of Changes* says: "The hundred clans support the capable."

*Minister’s Exegesis:* The sage humbles his words, and the people exalt him—thus, though he dwells above, the people do not feel oppressed. He places himself behind, and the people push him forward—thus, though he dwells ahead, the people do not feel harmed. The people’s hearts delight in exalting and returning to him, so they never tire of him. Because he does not contend, no one in the world can contend with him.

*Imperial Annotation:* To practice worthiness while abandoning the conduct of self-aggrandizement—where could one go and not be loved?

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Without contention, the mind is empty; thus the world responds with emptiness—what contention could there be?

**Chapter 67: The World Calls My Dao Great**
The world says my Dao is great, yet it seems to resemble nothing. Precisely because it is great, it seems to resemble nothing. If it resembled anything, it would long since have been small.

*Imperial Annotation:* To resemble things is small; to be resembled by things is great. The Dao covers and sustains the ten thousand things—vast and boundless, it is great indeed, and thus seems to resemble nothing. If it resembled anything, the Dao would have something outside itself—how could it then be great?

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Once it enters into forms and vessels, it cannot avoid resemblance. But the Dao has nothing outside itself—what could it resemble? Nothing is greater than the Dao—how could it be small?

I possess three treasures, which I treasure and hold fast.

*Imperial Annotation:* Unlike the views of the vulgar world, these are what I treasure and never lose.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* What can be held fast and never lost is like a treasure—it is not the view of the vulgar world.

The first is called *ci* (compassion).

*Imperial Annotation:* *Ci* is to love things—it is the essence of benevolence.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* *Ci* is to love things, thus not harming life or injuring things, allowing the ten thousand things to follow their nature.

The second is called *jian* (frugality).

*Imperial Annotation:* *Jian* is to suffice with what one has—it is the essence of propriety.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* *Jian* is to suffice with what one has, not losing one’s nature to external things. Thus it does not waste resources or harm the people, and the people naturally follow the highest principle.

The third is called *not daring to be first in the world*.

*Imperial Annotation:* To be first is to seek to surpass others, valuing force over virtue.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* To respond without initiating, to place oneself behind and below, to value humility and not seek to surpass—this is how one becomes a vessel of leadership.

Thus, *ci* enables courage.

*Imperial Annotation:* King Wen regarded the people as if they were wounded; with one outburst of anger, he pacified the people of the world.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Because of *ci*, the people love their ruler as they love their parents and elder brothers, as hands and feet protect the head and eyes. Who in the world could oppose him?

*Jian* enables expansiveness.

*Imperial Annotation:* To store up in winter is to flourish in spring. Even Heaven and Earth cannot constantly indulge in excess or waste—how much less can humans?

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Only through *jian* can one avoid excess and waste, be self-sufficient and never lacking, not harm the root with the branch, or lose the internal for the external—thus one can be expansive.

Not daring to be first in the world enables one to become a vessel of leadership.

*Imperial Annotation:* To not contend yet excel at victory is the Dao of Heaven. The Dao is revered, thus it becomes the leader of vessels.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Heaven, Earth, humans, and spirits all value humility. To place oneself below and not contend—this is how one becomes a vessel of leadership.

Now, if one abandons *ci* yet seeks courage, abandons *jian* yet seeks expansiveness, abandons being last yet seeks to be first—this is death.

*Imperial Annotation:* The world knows that courage can overcome others but does not know that *ci* enables courage. It knows that expansiveness can impress the masses but does not know that *jian* enables expansiveness. It knows that leadership is esteemed but does not know that placing oneself behind is essential. Such are the ways of the rigid and strong—they lead only to death.

*Ci* in battle brings victory; in defense, it brings solidity.

*Imperial Annotation:* The benevolent are invincible in the world. Thus, in battle they are victorious. The people love their ruler as hands and feet protect the head and eyes, as children and younger brothers defend their parents and elder brothers, willing to die without fleeing—thus, in defense they are solid.

Heaven will save them, protecting them with *ci*.

*Imperial Annotation:* Those whose hearts are set on benevolence are inwardly guided by Heaven; those whose hearts are set on malevolence are deprived of Heaven’s mirror. Thus, what Heaven uses to save and protect them is *ci* alone—this is why *ci* is the first of the three treasures.

**Chapter 68: The Good Warrior**
The good warrior is not martial.

*Imperial Annotation:* Martiality is a lesser Dao; the warrior values aspiration, saying only "benevolence and righteousness." Confucius said: "I have never studied the affairs of armies and war."

The good fighter is not angry.

*Imperial Annotation:* The highest warfare is to defeat the enemy’s strategy; anger truly overcomes thought.

The good conqueror of enemies does not contend.

*Imperial Annotation:* Contention is contrary to virtue. To contend over land in battle, killing enough to fill the fields; to contend over cities in battle, killing enough to fill the walls—victory or defeat is still undetermined.

The absence of martiality is how martiality is perfected; the absence of anger is how anger is mastered; the absence of contention is how contention is resolved. These three all emerge from virtue—thus they are called "good."

The good at employing others places themselves below.

*Imperial Annotation:* Though wisdom may surpass all under Heaven, the wise do not scheme for themselves—thus the wise devise strategies for others. Though ability may exhaust all within the seas, the able do not act for themselves—thus the able serve others. Though eloquence may adorn the myriad things, the eloquent do not speak for themselves—thus the eloquent are employed by others.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* To be good at placing oneself below is to have others work for me, and all under Heaven assist me. This is called the virtue of non-contention.

*Imperial Annotation:* Virtue transcends reputation; wisdom arises from contention. Those whose talents are complete and virtue unmanifest have never been heard to initiate—they only ever harmonize with others.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Not being martial, not being angry, not contending, and placing oneself below—these are virtues, not mere tactics.

This is called employing the strength of others.

*Imperial Annotation:* The keen of sight and hearing exhaust their perception; the wise and capable expend their strategies and abilities—yet those who position them remain without knowledge.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Gathering keen perception and forgetting wisdom and ability—this is how the keen perception, wisdom, and ability of all under Heaven are fully realized.

This is called matching Heaven, the ultimate of antiquity.

*Imperial Annotation:* To act through non-action is called Heaven. To employ others without contention is thus to match Heaven. To match Heaven is to reach the utmost—nothing can be added to it. Hence it is called the ultimate of antiquity. *Ultimate* means "utmost." The utmost of wood is the ridgepole of a house.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* The Way of Heaven suppresses the high and elevates the low. To be able yet place oneself below and not contend is called matching Heaven. To match Heaven is to unite with Heaven—this is the utmost of the Dao. Hence it is called the ultimate of antiquity.

Thus when armies clash in battle, the compassionate prevail.

*Imperial Annotation:* The sage’s use of arms rescues the people from water and fire, taking only what is broken. Divine martiality does not kill, but treasures *ci*—thus the seven realms of Heaven submit in reverence, and the four corners of the earth stand in awe. This is called constant victory.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* To exalt killing and conquest is the affair of warfare. To grieve and rescue is the Way of warfare. The compassionate constantly prevail—this is the warrior’s use of arms by the humane. Thus one may win without fighting.

*Exegesis of the Dao and Its Virtue by Emperor Huizong of Song, Scroll Nine, Concluded*
*Exegesis of the Dao and Its Virtue by Emperor Huizong of Song, Scroll Ten*

**Exegesis of the Virtue Classic by Gentleman Attendant for Promotion, Zhang An**

**Chapter Seventy: My Words Are Exceedingly Easy to Know**

My words are exceedingly easy to know, exceedingly easy to practice,
yet none under Heaven can know them, none can practice them.

*Imperial Annotation:* The Dao shines forth and is easily perceived—thus when expressed in words, it is exceedingly easy to know. It is essential and easily preserved—thus when manifested in action, it is exceedingly easy to practice.
Mencius said, “The Dao is like a great road—how could it be difficult to know?”
Thus the Dao presents no difficulty, and none under Heaven are incapable of it. Those who find it difficult or impossible have simply failed to turn inward and seek within themselves.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* The words of the sage merely clarify the Dao and analyze principle. Therefore, his teachings all arise from the inherent nature of life and destiny, removing obstructions to reveal what is already so. Turning inward, one attains it—it is not difficult to know or practice. Yet people abandon the near to pursue the distant, chase after branches while losing the root, seek externally, drown in excessive words, and sink in a flurry of affairs, never grasping the source. Thus none can know, none can practice.

“Words have their source; affairs have their lord.”

*Imperial Annotation:* Words are inexhaustible, yet principle is their foundation. Affairs are boundless, yet the Dao is their master. Following principle in inquiry and adhering to the Dao in action, nothing under Heaven is difficult.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Words derive their source from principle—grasp principle, and words are forgotten, for principle is their root. Affairs take their lord from the Dao—attain the Dao, and affairs dissolve, for affairs are but its branches. It is only due to ignorance that they do not know me.

*Imperial Annotation:* The knowledge of petty persons does not extend beyond official documents. Though they claim to know, they are in truth without knowledge—how could they comprehend the Dao?

*Minister’s Exegesis:* If they do not even know the source of words or the lord of affairs, how could they know me? To know me is to know the Dao.

“Those who know me are few—thus I am esteemed.”

*Imperial Annotation:* Those who possess conduct surpassing the world are reviled by the multitude. Those who harbor thoughts of solitary wisdom are startled by the people. Thus there are those who are valued for their rarity.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* My Dao is vast, so those who know it are few. Few know it—thus I am esteemed. Esteemed by the Dao, none confer rank upon me, yet I ever remain noble of myself. Therefore, the sage wears coarse cloth while holding jade within.

*Imperial Annotation:* The sage conceals himself in the world yet does not parade his constancy.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Possessing the Dao, he does not boast outwardly but retreats to store it in secrecy.

**Chapter Seventy-One: To Know Yet Not Know Is Supreme**

To know yet not know is supreme.

*Imperial Annotation:* The essence of the ultimate Dao is profound and obscure, its apex dark and silent. To know it externally is not true knowing; not to know it internally is true knowing. To know it superficially is shallow; not to know it is deep. To claim knowledge while not knowing—this is called the perfection of knowing the Dao, thus it is supreme.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* To cling to knowledge is to lose the Dao; to return to non-knowledge is to attain its supremacy.

“Not to know yet claim to know is a malady.”

*Imperial Annotation:* Not knowing the essence of the ultimate Dao yet knowing the coarse details of things, not knowing the apex of the ultimate Dao yet knowing the trivialities of affairs—ever entangled in trifles, ever ensnared by objects, they daily hasten toward the path of sorrow. Thus it is a malady.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Not knowing the Dao yet believing oneself to know—what they know are merely external things. To mistake such delusions for true knowledge is a grave malady.

“Only by recognizing the malady as a malady does one avoid malady.”

*Imperial Annotation:* To know one’s foolishness is not true foolishness. To know one’s confusion is not true confusion. The truly confused never awaken in their lifetime; the truly foolish never gain wisdom.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* To be afflicted yet unaware of the affliction is an incurable malady. Only by recognizing the malady does one avoid it. The sage’s freedom from malady lies in his recognition of the malady—thus he is without malady.

*Imperial Annotation:* The sage moves freely yet disdains entanglement in affairs, establishes the root and comprehends the divine. Possessing true knowledge, he yet appears as if he does not—thus he is without malady.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* The sage’s freedom from malady stems from true knowledge. Regarding knowledge as a malady, he constantly appears as if he does not know—thus he is without malady.

**Chapter Seventy-Two: When the People Do Not Fear Authority**

When the people do not fear authority, then great authority arrives.

*Imperial Annotation:* Petty persons regard minor transgressions as harmless and do not abandon them. Thus evil accumulates until it cannot be dispelled. The *Book of Changes* says, “Carrying a cangue until the ears are destroyed—calamity.”

*Minister’s Exegesis:* The people, lacking fear, ultimately plunge into disaster.

“Do not constrict their dwelling.”

*Imperial Annotation:* The dwelling is the abode of nature. Human nature is vast and cannot be confined, yet cramped scholars, unfit to discourse on the Dao, constrict their dwelling. Expand and fill it, and it will pervade Heaven and Earth, enveloping the six directions—nothing exists beyond its reach. Mencius said, “Dwell in the vastest dwelling of the world.”

*Minister’s Exegesis:* The abode of nature is the vastest dwelling in the world. Daily entangled with artificial things, it becomes obstructed and confined to a single corner—thus its dwelling is constricted.

“Do not stifle their life.”

*Imperial Annotation:* Life is the gathering of qi. Human life connects with the creations of things, yet those who stifle their life engage in daytime actions that shackle and lose it. Repeatedly bound and released, their nighttime qi is insufficient to sustain them. Only by preserving the great harmony without premature decay do they avoid stifling their life.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Humans are born from the Dao, their root originally unified. Unmoved by things, they connect with the creations of things. Yet when nature is stirred by emotion, emotion follows things astray—thus qi is lost, spirit is exhausted, essence is depleted, form departs, and life cannot be preserved. They stifle their life without realizing it. Embracing unity and returning to simplicity, preserving essence and embodying spirit, they attain longevity with the Dao—this is called not stifling their life.

Only by not stifling life does one not grow weary of it.

*Imperial Commentary:* Calamity and fortune are none but self-sought.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Not stifling one’s life, one’s life does not grow weary of itself. Thus the sage knows the self without displaying it, cherishes the self without exalting it. Therefore, they discard that and embrace this.

*Imperial Commentary:* The sage possesses the clarity of self-knowledge yet does not display it to flaunt ability. They hold the benevolence of self-love yet do not exalt themselves over others. Acting thus, they dwell among things without harming them, and nothing can harm them. Rejoicing in Heaven and free from care, what dread should they hold for power or wrath? The sage’s choice to discard or embrace is thus made clear.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* The sage’s spirit is whole within, so they truly know themselves. Not displaying themselves means they do not assert the self to dominate others. The Dao resides in them, so they truly cherish themselves. Not exalting themselves means they forget both self and others—how then could they value themselves above others? Those who constrict their dwelling and stifle their life, who display and exalt themselves, embody "that" which is discarded; its opposite is "this" which is embraced.

**Chapter 73**
Boldness in daring leads to death; boldness in restraint leads to life.

*Imperial Commentary:* The rigid and strong belong to the ranks of the dead. The soft and weak belong to the ranks of the living. Boldness in daring is mere recklessness—able to be bold but unable to be cautious, it is no true virtue but only hastens one’s destruction. Thus Confucius deemed Zilu’s love of courage as having "no redeeming quality."

Boldness in restraint knows how to hold back. Holding back is the way to precede others. As *Liezi* states, "The constant Dao of Heaven and Earth is softness."

*Minister’s Exegesis:* The recklessly bold lose themselves to violence; their nature is cruel, seeing others as mere straw. Thus their actions inevitably lead to death. Those who hold back do not underestimate opponents; their nature is compassionate, seeing the people as wounded. Thus their actions inevitably lead to life. These two paths bring either benefit or harm.

*Imperial Commentary:* What is correct may also err; what opposes may also align.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Boldness in killing pertains to the practice of warfare. Boldness in restraint pertains to the Dao of warfare. Dao and practice bring either benefit or harm—this much is evident. Yet as tactics shift unpredictably, what is correct may err, and what opposes may align.

Who knows the reason for Heaven’s aversion?

*Imperial Commentary:* Those who defy humanity align with Heaven. What humanity deems beneficial, Heaven deems harmful. Who can comprehend this?
