*Minister’s Exegesis:* Boldness in daring and killing is what humanity deems beneficial, but what Heaven deems harmful—for Heaven’s virtue favors life. Humanity pursues what it deems beneficial without knowing Heaven’s aversion. Hence, who knows the reason? Thus even the sage finds this difficult.

*Imperial Commentary:* Those who follow Heaven endure; those who defy it perish. Even the sage does not dare act lightly.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* When Yu campaigned against the Sanmiao, he heeded Yi’s counsel—such was his caution. Thus the sage prioritizes following Heaven and does not dare act lightly.

The Dao of Heaven does not contend yet excels at victory.

*Imperial Commentary:* When the myriad things emerge, it emerges with them without refusal. When they return, it returns with them without obstruction. This is called "not contending." Through the ebb and flow of fullness and emptiness, it moves with things, and the multitude of beings all receive their mandate—this is called "not contending yet excelling at victory."

*Minister’s Exegesis:* When the myriad things are born, it allows them to be born of themselves. When they perish, it lets them perish of themselves, without contention. All things are sheltered under Heaven’s canopy and receive their mandate—thus its excellence in victory is supreme.

It does not speak yet excels at response.

*Imperial Commentary:* What does Heaven say? It transforms through thunder and wind, reveals through fortune and misfortune, with no deviation even by a hair’s breadth, yet with responses like echoes and shadows.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Good deeds bring blessings; evil deeds bring calamity. The correspondence of good and evil admits no deviation, even by a hair’s breadth—such is its excellence in response.

It does not summon yet comes of itself.

*Imperial Commentary:* When a mandate is received, the one who issues it can summon it. Yet the myriad things, though entangled, are governed by Heaven’s mandate—who can summon it? It moves ceaselessly, entrusting itself to the self-motion of the single qi.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* To be summoned and arrive is the duty of ministers and children. Heaven governs the mandate of the myriad things—its supremacy is such that none can summon it. The single qi moves of itself, not by external command.

It is vast and excels at planning.

*Imperial Commentary:* Virtuous conduct is ever unobstructed, thus aware of peril.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Ever unobstructed, it is vast; aware of peril, it excels at planning.

Heaven’s net is vast and wide, sparse yet never failing.

*Imperial Commentary:* Dense yet with gaps—this is the work of humans. Heaven, though sparse, admits no gaps. The accumulation of good and evil brings blessings and misfortunes, each arriving according to its kind—this is why it never fails.

Those who contend only then prevail, who speak only then respond—these are humans. Heaven does not contend yet excels at victory, does not speak yet excels at response, and when summoned, arrives. Humans find it difficult to know Heaven. Yet Heaven does not summon yet comes of itself, vast and excelling at planning. Only the sage can embody this. Thus they neither pursue profit nor avoid harm, ever benefiting without harm—thereby aligning with Heaven’s virtue, unlike those bold in daring.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Heaven’s sheltering of things makes no distinction between good and evil; its vastness is boundless—who can fathom its limits? Fortune and misfortune, blessings and disasters, each arrive according to their kind. Though their timing may vary, none escape retribution—thus it is said to never fail.

**Chapter 74**
The people constantly do not fear death—how can they be frightened with death?

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** The people possess constancy in their hearts, and life for them is joyous. If they lack this constancy, why should they fear death? When axes and saws govern them, inked cords execute them, hammers and chisels decide their fate—this is called frightening them with death. Yet the people will defy and transgress, never truly transformed.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* The people forget what they naturally cherish, losing the principle of preserving life whole. Customs carve them down, wear them out; they defy laws and flout prohibitions, until even the six relations cannot protect one another. Mired in mud and ashes, emaciated in ditches and gullies, their lives become no better than death—this is why they do not fear death. If the ruler does not govern by the Dao, so that the people cease to fear death, yet still seeks to frighten them with death in hopes of restoring order—is this not exceedingly difficult?

If the people were constantly in awe of death and some acted perversely, would I not seize and execute them—who would dare? **Wang Bi’s Commentary:** When the world delights in life and dreads violating the law, only then are those who speak perversely punished and those who act strangely restrained. As Xunzi said, the crime of defying governance is indeed grave.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* When the people know life is joyous, they know death is fearful. Fearing death, they dread violating the law. There is always an executioner who kills; to kill in place of the executioner is to take the place of the master carpenter’s hewing. **Wang Bi’s Commentary:** The ruler must practice non-action and employ the world; the ministers must act and be employed by the world—this is the unchanging Dao. To kill in place of the executioner, to hew in place of the master carpenter, is for ruler and minister to share the same virtue, inverting the Dao in speech and delighting in what contravenes it. What men govern, how can it govern men? King Wen did not personally oversee every word, every case, every caution—only the officials and shepherds of the people were responsible for this.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Non-action is the ruler’s Dao; action is the minister’s Dao. The ruler is noble, the minister base; the distinction between above and below is constant and never confused. The ruler relies on the completion of those below; the ministers serve diligently and revere those above—thus great order is achieved. The state has fixed punishments; the executioner kills. If one usurps that killing, then killing is not by statute, and the punishment is unjust. This is like taking the place of the master carpenter’s hewing—only to wound oneself. For he who takes the place of the master carpenter’s hewing, few escape injuring their hands.

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** If even hewing in another’s place risks injury, how much more so killing? This is why the ancients prized non-action. Through non-action, those responsible for affairs bear their duty.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* To assign tasks beyond one’s proper role is to err. The master carpenter uses axe and adze; to take his place is to wound one’s hand. The executioner’s duty is punishment and execution; to take his place is to make punishments excessive. Thus one harms the people—and harming the people is to harm oneself.

**Chapter 75**
The people’s hunger arises from their ruler’s excessive taxation—this is why they hunger.

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** Heavy taxes leave fields and wastelands overgrown; the people lack sufficient food.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* The five grains are what the people rely on to live, yet the ruler’s taxes are excessive—thus the people’s food is insufficient.

The people’s unruliness arises from their ruler’s excessive action—this is why they are unruly.

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** When governance is overbearing, deceit and falsehood flourish; the people lose their simplicity.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* The more the ruler acts, the more the people respond with cunning—this is why they are unruly.

People’s disregard for death arises from their excessive devotion to preserving life—this is why they disregard death.

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** When attachment to life is too great, desire for profit stirs intensely. Waywardness, depravity, excess, and extravagance—nothing is left undone.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Excessive attachment to life leads to endless craving, harming life without self-love.

Only those who do not treat life as something to be clung to are superior to those who prize life.

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** *Zhuangzi* says: "Those who grasp the truth of life do not strive for what is of no use to life." Those who do not treat life as something to be clung to do not strive for what is of no use to life. They abandon affairs and forget life—thus abandoning affairs spares the body from labor, and forgetting life preserves the essence from depletion. Body whole, essence restored, they unite with Heaven—this is why they are superior to those who prize life. Prizing life differs from disregarding death; forgetting life is superior to prizing life. Extending what benefits one’s own life to the people—lightening taxes, simplifying punishments, ensuring every household is provided for and every person content—then even with distinct clothing and emblems, the people will not transgress. This is the ultimate achievement of emperors and kings.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Those who do not treat life as something to be clung to follow the natural course of principle. They do not strive to act for the sake of life. For if they do not possess life, their life remains perpetually whole. The body is not wearied by affairs; the essence is not depleted of its wholeness—this is the reason. Prizing life is superior to disregarding death, yet knowing life is precious, they still do not understand that preserving life whole is the foundation.

**Chapter 76**
In life, people are supple and weak; in death, they are rigid and strong. In life, plants and trees are tender and fragile; in death, they are withered and dry. Thus the rigid and strong are followers of death; the supple and weak are followers of life.

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** All things bear yin and embrace yang, blending qi to achieve harmony. Yang’s virtue is to engender; yin’s duty is to bring about decay. When decay prevails, the harmonious qi is lost. Thus it is said: the rigid and strong are followers of death; the supple and weak are followers of life.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* Supple weakness is the function of the Dao—thus it is a follower of life. Those who are harsh and overbearing do not meet their proper end—thus they are followers of death. In death, the harmonious qi is lost, and the principle of life is reversed—hence rigidity and strength. Therefore, when armies are strong, they do not prevail.

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** When opposing forces clash, the sorrowful side wins.

*Minister’s Exegesis:* To rely on strength for victory is to invite defeat.

When trees are strong, they are felled and used.

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** The slender paulownia and catalpa, everyone knows to nurture them. When they grow strong, they are cut down and put to use.

**Minister’s Exegesis:** When trees are strong, they are felled. Thus, the rigid and strong remain below, while the supple and weak reside above.

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** That the soft overcomes the hard and the weak overcomes the strong—this is where the Daoist arts of Laozi lie. *Zhuangzi* says: "Take meekness, humility, and deference as one’s outward bearing."

**Minister’s Exegesis:** Laozi takes softness and weakness as the root; the subtlety of the Daoist arts lies herein.

---

**Chapter 77**
The Way of Heaven—is it not like the drawing of a bow?
What is high is pressed down, what is low is lifted up.
What has surplus is diminished, what is deficient is replenished.

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** The Dao neither adds nor subtracts; things have their fullness and emptiness. That which is poured into yet never fills, drawn from yet never exhausts—this is what the sage preserves. Descending into things, Heaven and Earth wax and wane, ebb and flow with the seasons—how much more so for man?

The Way of Heaven takes the mean as its perfection. Thus, what is high is pressed down so it does not exceed, what is low is lifted up so it does not fall short. What is to come advances, what has succeeded retreats; the four seasons turn, each in its proper order.

**Minister’s Exegesis:** Increase and decrease, fullness and emptiness—these alternate and revolve, ever returning from extremes, all rooted in spontaneity. What is high and in surplus is in a state of excess and abundance, thus it must be pressed down and diminished. What is low and deficient is in a state of loss and depletion, thus it must be lifted up and replenished. All follows the principle of spontaneity. Therefore, the Way of Heaven is like the drawing of a bow: it ceases only when the mean is attained.

The Way of Heaven diminishes the surplus to replenish the deficient.

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** Fullness invites loss; humility gains increase. Such is the Way of Heaven.

**Minister’s Exegesis:** It abhors fullness, thus it diminishes the surplus. It favors humility, thus it replenishes the deficient.

The Way of Man is not so: it diminishes the deficient to serve the surplus.

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** The human heart rejects the lowly and advances the exalted, oppresses the solitary and fears the eminent.

**Minister’s Exegesis:** It exploits the deficient and diminishes them, seeking its own gain. It fears the surplus and serves them, seeking its own gratification.

Who can diminish the surplus to serve the deficient in the world? Only the Dao.

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** It does not oppress the solitary but bestows upon the destitute. It does not fear the eminent but reduces the abundant. None but the man of the Dao can do this.

**Minister’s Exegesis:** It bestows upon the destitute, freeing them from the burden of insufficiency. It reduces the abundant, sparing them the disaster of excess. Only the Dao is capable of this.

Thus, the sage acts yet does not rely on his actions; he accomplishes yet does not dwell in his achievements. Is it not that he does not wish to display his worth?

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** He does not rely on his actions, thus he has no heart for self-boasting. He does not dwell in his achievements, thus he has no will to self-satisfaction. While others adorn their wisdom, he alone remains as if foolish. While others seek victory, he alone bends to preserve wholeness. Only by not wishing to display his worth does he remain undiminished, attaining the Way of Heaven.

**Minister’s Exegesis:** The sage embodies the Dao in the world, thus he can offer his surplus to the world—not relying on that surplus. He acts yet does not rely on his actions; he accomplishes yet does not dwell in his achievements. He does not wish to boast or be self-satisfied to display his worth. Worth here refers to his deeds. The surplus is his own Dao: he awakens later awakenings with his prior awakening, enlightens later understandings with his prior understanding. Extending this to light taxes and modest levies, he disperses benefits throughout the world rather than hoarding them above—all of which is what is meant by offering his surplus to the world. Only by not displaying his worth do those below remain unaware of the ruler’s power. This is how the sage shares the virtue of Heaven. Thus it is said: he attains the Way of Heaven.

---

**Chapter 78**
In the world, nothing is more supple and weak than water, yet in attacking the rigid and strong, none can surpass it, for there is nothing by which it can be replaced.

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** The *Book of Changes* uses the well to illustrate nature, speaking of its unchanging quality. Laozi calls water "near to the Dao," for there is nothing by which it can be replaced. If there were something to replace it, it would follow others and lose itself—how could it overcome things? Only because there is nothing to replace it does it remain constant through ten thousand changes, and nothing can overcome it.

**Minister’s Exegesis:** The nature of water is to conform to vessels and assume their shapes, bending or straight, square or round, changing with them. Utterly soft and weak, yet in attacking the rigid and strong, none can surpass it. This is because its nature does not change, its essence ever abides in unity. This is how softness and weakness secure their victory.

That the soft overcomes the hard and the weak overcomes the strong—none in the world does not know this, yet none can practice it.

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** Knowledge reaches it, but benevolence cannot preserve it.

**Minister’s Exegesis:** Observing how water attacks the rigid and strong, none does not know that softness and weakness prevail—this is the reach of knowledge. Yet none can practice it because the self clings to a love of victory, and benevolence cannot preserve it.

Thus, the sage says: He who bears the world’s disgrace is called the lord of the altars of soil and grain. He who bears the world’s misfortune is called the king of all under Heaven.

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** Rivers and marshes receive filth; mountains and forests harbor pests. The lord of a state endures disgrace, embodying the emptiness of the Dao. The broader his capacity to receive, the more things return to him, and the farther his dominion extends. The *Classic* says: "Know its glory, guard its disgrace, and be the valley of the world."

**Minister’s Exegesis:** Water is near to the Dao, thus the sage models his virtue on water: soft, weak, and humble, bearing disgrace and receiving filth, containing all without distinction of good or evil, great or small, pure or foul, near or far. Thus he can be lord of the altars of soil and grain and king of all under Heaven.

Straight words seem contrary.

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** Words are not confined to one form. Opposing things yet aligning with the Dao—this is called the utmost correctness in the world.

**Minister’s Exegesis:** To call one who bears disgrace and misfortune the lord of the altars of soil and grain and king of all under Heaven—these are the sage’s straight words.

To speak thus seems contrary to custom, for it is by opposing custom that one attains correctness. Hence it is said: opposing things yet aligning with the Dao—this is called the utmost correctness in the world.

**Chapter 79: Reconciling Great Resentment**
To reconcile great resentment is necessarily to leave residual resentment. How can this be deemed good?

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** The towering tree, though unbroken by the storm’s trunk, may yet be struck by a flying tile—yet it bears no lingering resentment. Thus, there is no residual resentment. To love others is the root of harming them. To lay down arms is the root of forging them. How can this be deemed good?

**Minister’s Exegesis:** Those skilled in governing others in antiquity made them govern themselves, rather than imposing governance upon them. The nature of the people is swayed by profit and desire, leading them to excess, contention, and strife, seeking only the fulfillment of their wants. When those above establish prohibitions, punishments, and executions to govern them in every way, the world cannot attain its desires, and thus resentment arises. Great resentment stems from the people’s great desires, for they are denied what they seek, hence it is called great resentment. Because the people harbor such resentment, those above then seek to reconcile and restrain it with ritual, music, education, rewards, punishments, and decrees—yet the world’s residual resentment only grows. Thus, the sage holds the left tally.

Therefore, the sage holds the left tally and does not make demands of others.

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** The sage follows great transformations without obstruction, receiving all with joy. Thus, he makes no demands of others, and others make none of him. Tallies have left and right to distinguish giving and receiving. To hold the left tally is simply to give.

**Minister’s Exegesis:** Holding the left tally means acting without contrivance, entrusting all to spontaneity. Not engaging with things, one allows the myriad beings to arrive of themselves, responding to their arrival and harmonizing with them. One constantly bears the world’s burdens yet never imposes them on others.

Thus, one of power holds the tally.

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** To apportion to others by virtue is called sageliness.

**Minister’s Exegesis:** The power of non-power is how one holds the tally.

One without power enforces thoroughness.

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** To delight in connecting with things is not sageliness. One without power does not attain his own attainment but seeks the attainment of others. Ever striving to connect with things, harboring a mind to reconcile resentment—this is thoroughness, which only becomes an obstruction. As *Zhuangzi* says: "One who loses himself in things is called a benighted person."

**Minister’s Exegesis:** To gain nothing for oneself yet seek thoroughness in things is utter benightedness.

The Dao of Heaven shows no partiality; it constantly accords with the good.

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** If one is good, it accords with him—what partiality is there?

**Minister’s Exegesis:** Reconciling great resentment cannot be deemed good or possessed of power. One who holds the tally is called good, and thus the Dao of Heaven accords with him.

**Chapter 80: A Small State with Few People**

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** Vast lands and teeming people bring affairs too numerous to manage and wisdom too strained to discern; virtue declines from this, and punishments arise from this. The disorders of later ages begin here. When Master Lao lived at the end of the Zhou, he wearied of its chaos and sought the intent of the Dao, embedding it in his writings. He aimed to remedy the corruption of excessive refinement and elevate society to a realm of purity and simplicity. Thus he spoke of a small state with few people. For in the age of utmost virtue—from Rongcheng to Shennong, twelve rulers in all—those hailed as paragons of governance achieved this alone.

**Minister’s Exegesis:** A small state means its affairs are easily governed; few people means desires are readily satisfied. Affairs easily governed lead to the forgetting of cunning and calculation; desires readily satisfied leave no room for artifice and deceit. Only then can one return to simplicity and restore antiquity.

*"Let the people possess tools a hundredfold in number yet not employ them."*

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** Unified and impartial, free from the burdens of multitudes.

**Minister’s Exegesis:** Each person abides in their station and fulfills their life; without the encumbrance of cliques or factions, though tools may be a hundredfold, they have no use for them.

*"Let the people hold death in awe and not wander far."*

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** Life is joyous, death is fitting for burial—thus the people do not treat death lightly or scatter to the four directions. Confucius said: "When those above lose the Way, the people have long been dispersed." Does this not refer to distant wandering?

**Minister’s Exegesis:** Knowing life’s joys, they cherish their existence—thus they hold death in awe. Recognizing their livelihood as their foundation, they delight in their work—thus they do not wander far.

*"Though there be boats and carriages, none shall ride them. Though there be armor and weapons, none shall array them."*

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** Mountains have no paths or tunnels; marshes have no boats or bridges. Alike in their unknowing, their virtue does not depart. Without traces of perilous traverses, though there be boats and carriages, none shall ride them. Without the affliction of attack or war, though there be armor and weapons, none shall array them.

**Minister’s Exegesis:** At peace with their lot, delighting in their work, they have no cause for movement. Though there be boats and carriages, none shall ride them, for they have no cause for contention. Though there be armor and weapons, none shall array them, for they are content with their nature and joyful in self-sufficiency.

*"Let the people return to knotting cords for use."*

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** It suffices for recording essentials; no need for written contracts.

**Minister’s Exegesis:** Where mistrust arises, written contracts appear. When the people return to antiquity, they trust in knotted cords.

*"Sweet be their food, fine be their garments, secure be their customs, joyful be their work."*

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** They till to eat, weave to clothe, play with food in their mouths, and wander with bellies full—this is all the people need. Content with their portion, thus their food is sweet; eschewing adornment, thus their garments are fine; undisturbed, thus their customs are secure; preserving life, thus their work is joyful.

**Minister’s Exegesis:** Sweet be their food—not from rich flavors, but from sweetness in their allotted portion. Fine be their garments—not from embroidered silks, but from the fineness of their clothing. Secure in their dwellings, they find no place where they are not at ease. Joyful in their customs, they find no circumstance where they are not content.

*"Though neighboring states are within sight, and the sounds of cocks and dogs are heard, the people shall grow old and die without ever visiting one another."*

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** They dwell in proximity, their voices carry to one another, yet they do not visit—at such a time, without desires or demands, nothing is done yet all is naturally so. This is called utmost virtue.

**Minister’s Exegesis:** Sighing in benevolence, standing alone in righteousness—this is why the people value visitation. In an age of utmost virtue, the people forget one another in the realm of self-sufficiency, desiring nothing for themselves, seeking nothing from others. Though they see and hear one another, they grow old and die without visiting. How then could there be the corruption of arrogance, pretense, or mutual flattery?

---

**Chapter Eighty-One**

*"Truthful words are not beautiful."*

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** The Dao uttered is bland, without flavor. It transcends all sages without shame and endures through the ages without flaw.

**Minister’s Exegesis:** Truthful words are true to the Dao, not merely what people prefer. They cannot be bent to俗 taste—hence the world does not find them beautiful.

*"Beautiful words are not truthful."*

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** Ornate speech is florid; pursuing embellished phrases, taking branches for the essence, thus it lacks truth.

**Minister’s Exegesis:** Beautiful words may please people, but they are not true to the Dao—hence they fall short in truth.

*"The good do not dispute."*

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** Their words uphold essentials, speaking only what accords with the law.

**Minister’s Exegesis:** The good need no argument to make their goodness clear—thus their words are concise and not wasteful.

*"The disputatious are not good."*

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** Excessive branching and digressions lose the world’s utmost rightness.

**Minister’s Exegesis:** Disputation seeks clarity, but prolixity lacks principle—hence it is not good.

*"The wise are not learned."*

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** Those who grasp the Dao’s subtlety return to essentials.

**Minister’s Exegesis:** The Dao is one—it cannot be known through learning. Returning to essentials is reverting to unknowing; what need is there for learning?

*"The learned are not wise."*

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** The multitude of hearsay is not as good as restraint. *Zhuangzi* says: "Learning does not necessarily bring wisdom; disputation does not necessarily bring intelligence."

**Minister’s Exegesis:** Learning burdens the mind with excess; the more one learns, the more one strays outward. Knowledge comes from without, hence it boasts of its breadth. Self-knowledge knows one’s nature; knowing one’s nature knows Heaven—thus restraint is better.

*"The sage accumulates nothing."*

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** To accumulate is to be insufficient; to store nothing is to have surplus. *Zhuangzi* says: "The sagely Dao moves without accumulation." Confucius said: "I daily diminish."

**Minister’s Exegesis:** The sage is utterly empty, without thing—what accumulation could there be? This emptiness supplies the world’s myriad demands. If one accumulates and then disperses, the accumulation will eventually be exhausted. Only without accumulation does the sagely Dao operate.

*"Having acted for others, he has more; having given to others, he has much."*

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** He lends and completes, yet never depletes himself. All things turn to him for resources, yet he is never lacking.

**Minister’s Exegesis:** What others cannot do, awaiting him to do—this is called "acting for others." What others lack, awaiting him to provide—this is called "giving." The sage’s actions and gifts all arise from the Dao—thus he incurs no cost to himself and no depletion.

*"The Way of Heaven benefits and does not harm."*

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** Qian begins by beautifully benefiting all under Heaven. It does not speak of its beneficence, yet things are truly benefited—it has never harmed.

**Minister’s Exegesis:** It gives life to what naturally lives, completes what naturally completes, beautifully benefits the myriad things without claiming beneficence—what harm could there be? This is the Way of Heaven.

*"The Way of the sage acts and does not contend."*

**Wang Bi’s Commentary:** Following without opposing, its movement is like water. Responding without hoarding, its stillness is like a mirror. Harmonizing without leading, its response is like an echo. Though it acts, its action arises from non-action. It embodies Heaven—what contention could there be? This virtue aligns with the Dao—thus the *Dejing* concludes here.

**Minister’s Exegesis:** The sage empties himself to respond to the world, acts without acting to embody the Dao, entrusts the self-moving qi, and lets the myriad things follow their nature. In movement, it is sudden, like water flowing without opposition. In stillness, it is luminous, like a mirror reflecting without hoarding.

When things arise, they respond like an echo—such action emerges from non-being. With non-action, there is no self; with no self, there is no other. Self and other both forgotten, what contention could there be? To act without contending is to embody the Dao.

Master Lao elucidated the meaning of virtue and the Dao, with its essence lying in softness, weakness, and non-contention as the foundation. Thus, he concluded with the sage’s Way of acting without contending. Only those who attain the Dao can be likened to this, and so he spoke of it at the end of the *Virtue Classic*.

*The Exegesis on the Authentic Classic of Dao and Virtue by Emperor Huizong of Song, Scroll Ten, concludes.*
